# Avoid This Pronunciation to Sound Educated

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson focuses on challenging English vocabulary and common pronunciation mistakes. It highlights words with difficult consonant clusters or confusingly similar sounds, helping you master precise American pronunciation to sound more educated and professional.

## Key Points

- Pay attention to every consonant in difficult clusters (like "SK" in asterisk or "XTH" in sixth).
- Do not add or drop letters (e.g., pronouncing "height" with an "HT" sound, not "TH").
- Understand the subtle differences between similar words (e.g., moot vs. mute, prostate vs. prostrate).

## How to Review

Read the target vocabulary aloud, focusing specifically on the consonant sounds at the ends of words. Practice linking the target words within full sentences, and record yourself to check if you are clearly enunciating every syllable and consonant cluster.

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake | Meaning / natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Asterisk | AS-tuh-risk | AS-tuh-risks | A star symbol (*). "Please put an asterisk next to an item you consider urgent." |
| Prostate | PRAH-state | prostrate | A part of the male reproductive system. "That test is used to diagnose prostate cancer." |
| Prostrate | PRAH-strate | prostate | Lying face down on the ground. "I saw a man prostrate on the ground." |
| Arctic | ARK-tik | AR-tik | The area around the North Pole. "His grandfather was an arctic explorer." |
| Height | HYT | HYTH | How tall something is. "I need to adjust the height of this chair." |
| Sixth | SIKSTH | SIKS | Number six in a sequence. "We threw a big party for his sixth birthday." |
| Realtor | REEL-ter | REE-luh-ter | A real estate agent. "The realtor showed us some houses last weekend." |
| Supposedly | suh-PO-zed-lee | suh-PO-zab-lee | Assumed to be true but doubtful. "He supposedly graduated from college." |
| February | FEB-roo-air-ee | FEB-yoo-air-ee | The second month. "Valentine's Day is on February 14th." |

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Moot vs. Mute:** "Moot" means irrelevant to the discussion (e.g., "a moot point"). "Mute" means unable to speak or making no sound. A very common mistake even among native speakers is saying "mute point" instead of the correct "moot point".
- **Height vs. Length/Strength:** "Length" and "strength" end in a "TH" sound, which causes people to incorrectly add a "TH" to "height". Remember that "height" ends with a sharp "T" sound.

## Reusable Sentence Patterns

| Pattern | Use it for | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Please put an asterisk next to... | Highlighting important items in a document. | Please put an asterisk next to the budget figures. |
| It's a moot point. | Ending a debate when the outcome no longer matters. | I disagree with your plan, but it's a moot point now since the deadline passed. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Please put an asterisk next to an item that you consider urgent.
2. That test is used to diagnose prostate cancer.
3. His grandfather was an arctic explorer.
4. I need to adjust the height of this chair.
5. I sat in the sixth row.
6. The realtor showed us some houses last weekend.
7. He supposedly graduated from college.
8. I think you made the wrong decision, but it's a moot point; nothing can be done now.
9. I can't find the mute button on this remote.

## Vocabulary Review List

- Asterisk
- Prostate
- Prostrate
- Arctic
- Height
- Sixth
- Realtor
- Supposedly
- February
- Moot point
- Mute

## Practice Prompts

1. Read aloud: "The sixth asterisk indicates the height of the arctic glacier."
2. Write a sentence about a past argument, explaining why the topic is now a "moot point."
3. Record yourself saying "February" and "sixth" three times fast.
4. Create a sentence describing a house hunt using the word "realtor."

## Mini Quiz

1. Does the word "height" end with a "T" sound or a "TH" sound?
2. If an issue is no longer relevant, is it a "mute point" or a "moot point"?
3. What is the difference between "prostate" and "prostrate"?
4. Which letter is commonly (and incorrectly) dropped when pronouncing "Arctic"?

### Answer Key

1. A "T" sound.
2. A moot point.
3. Prostate is a medical term (gland); prostrate means lying flat on the ground.
4. The first "c" (k sound).

# 10 English Proverbs about LOVE

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson covers ten classic English proverbs concerning love and relationships. Understanding these proverbs will improve your listening comprehension, as native speakers frequently use them to offer relationship advice or express common truths about romance.

## Key Points

- Proverbs contain cultural wisdom and are used as shorthand for complex advice.
- Some proverbs contradict each other depending on the situation (e.g., "absence makes the heart grow fonder" vs. "out of sight, out of mind").
- Learning these phrases as complete units will make your conversational English sound highly natural.

## How to Review

Memorize the proverbs as fixed phrases. Practice active recall by thinking of a real-life relationship scenario (like a breakup or a long-distance relationship) and identifying which proverb best applies to the situation.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Ignoring flaws | Love is blind | When you love someone, you don't see their faults. | You know what they say, love is blind; she can't see what a bad person he is. |
| There are other people | There are other fish in the sea | Used to comfort someone after a breakup, meaning they will find someone else. | Don't worry about the breakup, you'll meet a great guy soon. There are other fish in the sea. |
| Looks aren't everything | Beauty is only skin deep | A person's character matters more than their physical appearance. | She's very attractive, but when you get to know her you realize beauty is only skin deep. |
| Everyone has different tastes | Beauty is in the eye of the beholder | Different people find different things beautiful. | Jenny is beautiful to him, but I think she's plain. It just proves beauty is in the eye of the beholder. |
| Different people like each other | Opposites attract | People who are very different often fall in love. | She loves to socialize, but he prefers staying home. Opposites attract. |
| Distance makes love stronger | Absence makes the heart grow fonder | You love someone more when you are apart from them. | Ever since her boyfriend moved away, she can't stop thinking about him. Absence makes the heart grow fonder. |
| Distance ruins love | Out of sight, out of mind | If you don't see someone, you forget about them. | When he went away for a year, she lost interest. Out of sight, out of mind. |
| Pain will pass | Time heals all wounds | Emotional pain and sadness fade as time passes. | I know the breakup is painful, but remember that time heals all wounds. |
| Heartbreak is worth it | It's better to have loved and lost than never to have loved at all | Experiencing love is valuable, even if it ends in sadness. | Even though it ended badly, I don't regret it. It's better to have loved and lost than never to have loved at all. |
| Dating takes time | You have to kiss a lot of frogs to find your prince | You must date many wrong people before finding the right partner. | Online dating is frustrating, but you have to kiss a lot of frogs to find your prince. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Can't he see what a bad person his girlfriend is? You know what they say, love is blind.
2. I'm so sad that my boyfriend and I broke up.
3. You'll meet a great guy soon; there are other fish in the sea.
4. She's very attractive, but when you get to know her better you'll realize that beauty is only skin deep.
5. Jenny is the most beautiful woman I've ever seen. You must be kidding, I think she's quite plain.
6. She loves to go out and socialize, but her husband prefers to stay home.
7. I know the breakup is painful for you now, but remember time heals all wounds.

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Fixed Expressions:** Proverbs are fixed idioms. Do not change the vocabulary inside them (e.g., do not say "there are *more* fish in the *ocean*"). Say them exactly as written.
- **Context:** "There are other fish in the sea" and "Time heals all wounds" are specifically used to comfort someone who is grieving a relationship.

## Vocabulary Review List

- Love is blind
- There are other (plenty more) fish in the sea
- Beauty is only skin deep
- Beauty is in the eye of the beholder
- Opposites attract
- Absence makes the heart grow fonder
- Out of sight, out of mind
- Time heals all wounds
- It's better to have loved and lost than never to have loved at all
- You have to kiss a lot of frogs to find your prince

## Practice Prompts

1. A friend is crying over a recent breakup. Write two sentences comforting them using a proverb.
2. Describe a couple you know who are completely different from one another. End your description with a proverb.
3. Write a short paragraph arguing whether you believe "absence makes the heart grow fonder" or "out of sight, out of mind."
4. Use "beauty is in the eye of the beholder" to explain why you like an unusual piece of art or clothing.

## Mini Quiz

1. Which proverb means you shouldn't judge a person's character by their appearance?
2. Which proverb advises you to expect some bad dates before finding a good relationship?
3. What is the opposite of "Out of sight, out of mind"?
4. If your friend thinks a painting is ugly but you think it's beautiful, which proverb applies?

### Answer Key

1. Beauty is only skin deep.
2. You have to kiss a lot of frogs to find your prince.
3. Absence makes the heart grow fonder.
4. Beauty is in the eye of the beholder.

# 10 Ways to Apologize in English with a Perfect American Accent

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson teaches polite, professional, and sincere ways to apologize in American English. It covers crucial grammatical structures (using "that" + past tense versus "for" + gerund) and pronunciation tips to sound genuinely remorseful and natural.

## Key Points

- Use an open "ah" sound for the "o" in "sorry" (SAH-ree) to sound authentically American.
- You can apologize using "sorry that I [past tense verb]" or "sorry for [verb+ing]".
- Admitting fault is a key component of a good apology. Use phrases like "I was wrong to..." or "I shouldn't have...".

## How to Review

Practice apologizing out loud. Pick a scenario (e.g., forgetting a meeting, saying something rude) and formulate a complete apology combining an expression of regret ("I sincerely apologize"), a grammatical reason ("for forgetting..."), and taking responsibility ("It completely slipped my mind").

## Grammar and Structure Rules

When giving a reason for your apology, use one of two structures:

1. **Subject + Verb (Past Tense):**
   - I'm sorry (that) I **said** that.
   - I'm sorry I **came** late.
2. **For + Gerund (Verb-ing):**
   - I'm sorry for **saying** that.
   - I'm sorry for **coming** late.

## Reusable Sentence Patterns

| Pattern | Use it for | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| I'm [truly/so/very] sorry for [verb+ing] | General sincere apologies. | I'm truly sorry for hurting your feelings. |
| I [sincerely] apologize for [verb+ing] | Formal or professional apologies. | I sincerely apologize for forgetting the meeting. |
| It was wrong of me to [verb] | Taking full responsibility for a bad action. | It was wrong of me to blame you. |
| I shouldn't have [past participle] | Expressing regret about a past action. | I shouldn't have spoken to you that way. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word / Phrase | Correct pronunciation | Important rule |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Sorry | SAH-ree | Use an open 'ah' sound, not 'so-ree'. |
| Apologize | uh-PAH-luh-jize | Stress the second syllable with an open 'ah'. |
| Hurting | HER-ding | The 't' between 'r' and a vowel often becomes a fast flap 'd'. |
| Shouldn't have | SHUD-ent-uv | Often casually pronounced as "shouldn't of". |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I'm truly sorry.
2. I'm sorry that I forgot your birthday.
3. I apologize for forgetting your birthday.
4. Please accept my sincerest apologies.
5. Please forgive me, it won't happen again.
6. I'm sorry, it completely slipped my mind.
7. I was wrong to speak to you that way.
8. It was wrong of me to blame you.
9. I shouldn't have said that.
10. I should have known better.

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Sorry vs. Apologize Formality:** "I'm sorry" is used universally. "I apologize" or "my apologies" is slightly more formal, making it excellent for business or written contexts.
- **Slipped my mind:** This is a very natural idiom to use when you forget an appointment, a birthday, or a small task. It implies the forgetting was an honest accident.

## Vocabulary Review List

- I'm so / truly / very sorry
- I apologize / I sincerely apologize
- My apologies / My sincerest apologies
- Please forgive me
- It completely slipped my mind
- I was wrong to...
- It was wrong of me to...
- I shouldn't have...
- I should have known better

## Practice Prompts

1. You forgot to send an important email to your boss. Write a formal apology using "I sincerely apologize for...".
2. You accidentally broke your friend's favorite mug. Apologize and offer a reason using "I'm sorry that I...".
3. Write a sentence expressing regret about going to bed too late last night using "I shouldn't have...".
4. Say out loud: "I'm sorry, it completely slipped my mind" making sure to use the open American 'O' in sorry.

## Mini Quiz

1. What is the correct grammatical form to follow "I'm sorry for..."?
2. Which idiom means "I completely forgot"?
3. Is it better to say "I apologize that I forgetting" or "I apologize for forgetting"?
4. In American English, what sound does the "t" in "hurting" often change to?

### Answer Key

1. A gerund (verb + ing).
2. It completely slipped my mind.
3. I apologize for forgetting.
4. A fast "d" flap sound.

# Breath or Breathe? 3 Rules

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson clarifies the confusing difference between the noun "breath" and the verb "breathe." You'll learn three essential rules covering spelling, vowel sounds, and voiced/voiceless "th" sounds, along with natural idiomatic expressions native speakers use daily.

## Key Points

- **Rule 1 (Spelling):** "Breath" is a noun. "Breathe" (with an "e" at the end) is a verb.
- **Rule 2 (Vowels):** "Breath" uses a short "e" sound (like bed). "Breathe" uses a long "e" sound (like please).
- **Rule 3 (Consonants):** "Breath" ends with a voiceless "th". "Breathe" ends with a voiced "th" (vocal cords vibrate).

## How to Review

Read the idioms aloud. Put your hand on your throat when practicing "breathe" to ensure your vocal cords are vibrating for the voiced "th" sound.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Stop breathing normally | Out of breath | Breathing heavily after exercise or exertion. | That was really difficult; I'm out of breath. |
| Rest and breathe normally | Catch my breath | To rest until your breathing returns to normal. | I need a few minutes to catch my breath. |
| Very amazing / beautiful | Breathtaking / Takes my breath away | So beautiful that you stop breathing in shock. | The view from my hotel was breathtaking. |
| Don't bother talking | Save your breath | Stop talking because it won't make a difference. | He's not listening to you, save your breath. |
| Something new and better | A breath of fresh air | A refreshing new change or approach. | The new manager is a breath of fresh air compared to the old one. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake | Meaning / natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Breath | BRETH (voiceless th) | BREE-th | (Noun) The air taken in and out. "Take a deep breath." |
| Breathe | BREE-TH (voiced th) | BRETH | (Verb) The action of inhaling/exhaling. "Breathe in and out." |

## Common Mistakes

| Mistake | Correct version | Why |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Take a deep breathe. | Take a deep breath. | "Breath" is the noun object here. |
| I can't breath. | I can't breathe. | "Breathe" is the verb needed after "can't". |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The doctor used a stethoscope and said, "Take a deep breath."
2. I need a few minutes to catch my breath.
3. The sunset tonight was breathtaking.
4. Don't tell me what to do, save your breath.
5. Your positive attitude is a breath of fresh air in this negative environment.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence explaining why a friend should "save their breath" in an argument.
2. Record yourself saying: "I need to take a deep breath before I breathe out." Ensure the vowel and "th" sounds are distinct.

## Mini Quiz

1. Is "breath" a noun or a verb?
2. Which word has a vibrating (voiced) "th" sound: breath or breathe?
3. What idiom means a refreshing, positive change?

### Answer Key

1. Noun.
2. Breathe.
3. A breath of fresh air.

# Advanced English Expressions with a Psychologist

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson features an interview with a Los Angeles psychologist who works with actors and writers. It focuses on high-level expressions related to performance, criticism, nuance, and professional pressure.

## Key Points

- Differentiate between noun and adjective forms of "complex" through syllable stress.
- Learn the difference between "critique" (evaluate) and "criticize" (judge negatively).
- Master expressions related to pressure, such as "hit the mark," "be on," and "difficult to take."

## How to Review

Create a personal story about a stressful professional experience, purposefully incorporating 5-7 expressions from this lesson (e.g., "hit a deadline," "putting out fires," "critiqued my work").

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| In many ways | In a multitude of ways | In various, numerous ways. | The experience impacted me in a multitude of ways. |
| Notice subtly | Pick up | Notice something difficult to see or feel. | I'm surprised nobody else picked up that he was angry. |
| Subtle / relaxed | Low-key | Subtle, or referring to a relaxed person/event. | It's not a big party, just a low-key gathering. |
| Exactly | The very | Emphasizing one particular specific thing. | Beethoven lived in this very house. |
| Unemployed | Out of work | Not having a job currently. | He was out of work for two years. |
| Evaluate | Critique | Give a detailed evaluation (not necessarily bad). | Could you please critique my speech? |
| Judge negatively | Criticize | Point out faults and negative aspects. | The politician was criticized for his bad decisions. |
| Finish on time | Hit a deadline (meet) | To finish something by the required time. | I hope we can hit the deadline this Friday. |
| Exactly what's needed | Hit the spot | Perfectly satisfying (often for food/drink). | I was so thirsty, that cold drink really hit the spot. |
| Examine closely | Scrutinize | Look at every detail very carefully. | The actor's every move is being scrutinized by the media. |
| A lot / intensely | To a very high degree | To a large extent. | It's done to a very high degree of detail. |
| Succeed / be perfect | Hit the mark | Succeed in pleasing people or meeting expectations. | They need to look good and be ready to hit the mark. |
| In performance mode | To be on | Ready to perform; acting engaged and perfect. | My job requires me to be on all the time. |
| Hard to tolerate | Difficult to take | Hard to deal with emotionally. | When people are brutal, it's difficult to take. |
| Over and over | Time and again | Repeatedly over a long period. | I've asked you to do that time and again. |
| Completely | Altogether | (One word) On the whole; entirely. | I don't altogether agree with you. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Important rule |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Complex (noun) | COM-plex | Stress first syllable (e.g., apartment complex). |
| Complex (adjective) | com-PLEX | Stress second syllable (e.g., a complex industry). |
| Critique | cri-TEEK | Stress second syllable. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The situation is complex, and it impacted me in a multitude of ways.
2. I can't believe you bought the very same dress that I did.
3. You are constantly being critiqued for your work.
4. That cold drink really hit the spot.
5. The lawyer scrutinized every detail of the contract.
6. Even successful people find it difficult to take time and again.
7. I have to watch every word I say around her.
8. This is an altogether different situation.

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe a time you had to "hit a deadline."
2. Write a sentence about a "low-key" weekend.
3. Explain the difference between someone critiquing your work and criticizing it.

## Mini Quiz

1. If you are examining a document very closely, what verb should you use?
2. Does "altogether" (one word) mean "everyone together" or "completely"?
3. If an actor feels pressure to perform constantly, they feel they always have to be what?

### Answer Key

1. Scrutinize.
2. Completely / entirely.
3. On ("to be on").

# Advanced Vocabulary for Buying a House in the US

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this interview with an LA real estate agent, you learn the specific terminology required to navigate buying or selling a house in the US, alongside versatile professional expressions like "putting out fires" and "on top of it."

## Key Points

- Master real estate jargon: *escrow*, *fund*, *closing costs*, *condo*, and *fixer*.
- Use "key" as an adjective to mean crucial or most important.
- Understand how to use "x amount" as a placeholder for an unknown variable in speech.

## How to Review

Pretend you are explaining the process of buying a home to a friend in English. Try to use words like *escrow*, *first-time buyers*, and *move-in ready* in your explanation.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| All types of people | All walks of life | Various social classes or professions. | I get to meet people from all walks of life. |
| Very important | Key | (Adjective) Crucial to success. | Good communication is key in my profession. |
| Finalizing a sale | Closing skills | The ability to get a buyer/seller to say yes. | A salesperson needs good closing skills; he's a good closer. |
| New buyers | First-time buyers | People buying a house for the first time. | They were first-time buyers and almost lost the deal. |
| Third-party account | Escrow | A neutral company holding money during a sale. | We opened escrow on a house. |
| An unknown number | X amount | An unspecified variable or amount. | You have X amount of days for that to fund. |
| Provide money/loan | To fund | Bank sending the loan money to escrow. | We made sure everything was getting funded. |
| Handle crises | Put out fires | Dealing with urgent emergencies at work. | I spent the workday putting out fires. |
| In control / aware | On top of it | Managing a situation successfully. | Good job, you're really on top of it. |
| Forbidden | A no-no | Something unacceptable or not allowed. | Cheating on a test is a major no-no. |
| Perfect condition | Move-in ready | A house requiring no repairs. | Buyers nowadays are looking for move-in ready homes. |
| Needs repairs | A fixer (-upper) | A house that requires a lot of work. | We can only afford a fixer-upper. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. People from all walks of life attended the conference.
2. If you want to improve your English, consistent practice is key.
3. We are closing escrow tomorrow and getting our keys.
4. I didn't get my work done because I was busy putting out fires.
5. The situation keeps changing, so we need to stay on top of it.
6. Swapping mortgage companies during escrow is a major no-no.
7. We don't want to remodel; we prefer a move-in ready house.

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **X amount:** Say the literal letter "X". You can also say "X, Y, and Z" to refer to a list of unspecified tasks or things.
- **Key:** When used as an adjective, it does not take an article. Say "Communication is key" (NOT "Communication is a key").

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence explaining why a "fixer-upper" is cheaper than a "move-in ready" home.
2. Use "putting out fires" to describe a busy day at work.
3. State one rule at your workplace and call it a "no-no."

## Mini Quiz

1. What term describes a house that needs a lot of repairs?
2. What phrase means you are in control of a situation and handling it?
3. What does it mean if a salesperson is a "good closer"?

### Answer Key

1. A fixer (or fixer-upper).
2. On top of it.
3. They are skilled at finalizing sales and convincing clients.

<br/>

# English Fluency Practice - Buying a House

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Continuing the real estate theme, this lesson delves into market conditions and financial terminology. You will learn how to discuss housing inventory, price stability, and the strict financial checks (underwriting and credit scores) required for mortgages in the US.

## Key Points

- Differentiate between "square footage" (indoor living space) and "lot size" (outdoor land size).
- Learn market terms: *inventory*, *lagging*, *holding*, and *listings*.
- Understand financial terms: *HOA*, *pre-approval*, *underwritten*, and *credit score*.

## How to Review

Review the financial terms specifically. Try to define *HOA*, *credit score*, and *underwriting* out loud as if explaining the US banking system to a foreigner.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Desirable | Sought after | Highly desired and in demand. | Houses in good school districts are highly sought after. |
| Land size | Lot / Lot size | The area of land a house is built on. | The closer you get to the beach, the smaller the lot. |
| Staying stable | Holding | Prices maintaining their current level. | Real estate prices will continue to hold. |
| Houses for sale | Listings | Properties currently advertised for sale. | There are 14 listings in this neighborhood. |
| Houses available | Inventory | The total supply of houses for sale. | Inventory is low right now. |
| Falling behind | Lagging | Developing or moving more slowly than expected. | Inventory is lagging behind demand. |
| Workable / doable | Feasible | Possible and practical to do. | A townhome is more financially feasible for me. |
| Monthly building fee | HOA (Homeowners Assoc.) | Fee paid for outside maintenance of condos. | What is your top HOA fee budget? |
| Didn't hear | Didn't catch | A polite way to ask someone to repeat their name. | I'm sorry, I didn't catch your name. |
| Financially verified | Underwritten | Bank has deeply researched your financials for a loan. | He put in an offer that has been underwritten. |
| Bank letter | Pre-approval letter | Letter stating you qualify to borrow a certain amount. | I have been pre-approved for $700,000. |
| Polite word for man | Gentleman | Respectful term for a male. | The gentleman outside is another buyer. |
| Submit an offer | Put in an offer | To formally propose buying a house. | We'd like to put in an offer. |
| Ready to go | Ready to rock and roll | Fully prepared to begin an activity. | They're underwritten and ready to rock and roll. |
| Detailed analysis | Deeper dive | A closer, more detailed examination. | We decided to take a deeper dive into the financials. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Important rule |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Gentleman / Gentlemen | JEN-tul-min | Singular and plural sound identical because the final syllable is unstressed. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Engineers are highly sought after in Silicon Valley.
2. The weather forecast predicts the weather will be holding for the weekend.
3. Your proposal is technically feasible, but we must consider the risks.
4. I'm sorry, I didn't get your name.
5. I put in an order for pizza.
6. The bank did a deeper dive into his credit history.
7. I can't get a loan because my credit score is low.

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Get vs. Catch:** If someone *never told you* their name, say "I didn't get your name." If they *said it but you didn't hear it*, say "I didn't catch your name."
- **Pre-approved vs. Underwritten:** Pre-approval is a light check; underwriting is a "deeper dive" into a buyer's financials.

## Practice Prompts

1. Use "lagging" to describe a slow runner or a struggling student.
2. Write a sentence putting in an offer on a house that is "sought after."
3. Explain why a high "credit score" is important in your own words.

## Mini Quiz

1. What word refers to the land a house is built on?
2. If prices are not dropping, what verb is used?
3. What does "HOA" stand for?

### Answer Key

1. A lot.
2. Holding.
3. Homeowners Association.

<br/>

title: "10 English Expressions for Business - Sound Like a Native Speaker"

---

# 10 English Expressions for Business

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson teaches ten crucial business expressions that use the word "turn." Mastering these phrasal verbs and nouns will make you sound much more natural and fluent in an office or corporate environment.

## Key Points

- Learn business nouns like *turnover rate*, *downturn*, and *turnout*.
- Master phrasal verbs for office actions: *turn down* (reject) and *turn in* (submit).
- Practice the difficult American "tur-" pronunciation, blending the T and R sounds.

## How to Review

Write a short paragraph about a hypothetical failing business that recently improved. Use the expressions *downturn*, *turnover rate*, *turn a corner*, and *turn a profit*.

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Important rule |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Turn | CHURN / T-URN | The "T" before "ur" is pronounced halfway between a "T" and a "CH". |
| Turtle | TER-dul | Start with the "tur" sound, hit a fast flap "D" for the "t", and hold the "L" up. |
| Colleague | CAH-leeg | Stress the first syllable with an open "AH" sound. |

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Employee quit rate | Turnover rate | The percentage of employees leaving a company. | That company has a very high turnover rate. |
| Economic decline | Downturn | A period when business/economy is doing poorly. | There's an increase in unemployment during a downturn. |
| Attendance | Turnout | The number of people who attend an event. | We had a great turnout at the conference. |
| Make money | Turn a profit | To generate financial profit. | They expect to turn a profit next quarter. |
| Reject | Turn down | To refuse an offer or applicant. | I turned down the job offer for a higher salary. |
| Submit | Turn in | To submit a document or item. | Please turn in your paperwork to HR. |
| Resulted / Happened | Turn out (verb) | The unexpected end result. | We hesitated, but it turned out to be a great decision. |
| Improve | Turn a corner | To begin improving after a bad period. | The company turned a corner when they launched the product. |
| Ask for advice | Turn to (someone) | To go to someone for help or support. | When I need help, I turn to my co-worker. |
| Alternate | Take turns | To alternate doing a task. | My colleague and I take turns working on Saturdays. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Due to the stressful work environment, that company has a very high turnover rate.
2. There is an increase in unemployment during an economic downturn.
3. Because we sent an email announcement, we had a great turnout at the conference.
4. I turned down the job offer because I wanted a higher salary.
5. Please turn in your paperwork to HR by next Friday.
6. We hesitated about hiring him, but it turned out to be a great decision.
7. The company turned a corner when they launched their new product.
8. When I need help, I turn to my more experienced co-worker.
9. My colleague and I take turns working on Saturdays.

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Turnout vs. Turn out:** *Turnout* (noun) is attendance. *Turn out* (verb) is the result. (e.g., "The turnout was low, but it turned out to be a good meeting.")
- **Colleague pronunciation:** A very common mistake is stressing the second syllable or using an "o" sound. It is CAH-leeg.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence explaining why a restaurant might have a high "turnover rate."
2. Create a sentence describing a situation where you had to "turn down" an invitation.
3. Say aloud: "My colleague and I take turns turning in the paperwork." Pay attention to the "tur" sounds.

## Mini Quiz

1. If attendance at a meeting was very high, what noun describes this?
2. What phrase means a business is finally starting to improve after a bad period?
3. Which phrase means to submit an assignment or document?

### Answer Key

1. A high turnout.
2. Turned a corner.
3. Turn in.

title: "10 Common English Proverbs You Should Know"

category: "english-writing"

mood: "study"

# 10 Common English Proverbs

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson explores ten traditional English proverbs that convey cultural wisdom and life advice. You'll learn not only their meanings but also how to naturally pronounce difficult English words like "word," "worm," and "iron."

## Key Points

- A proverb is a short, traditional saying that offers advice or states a general truth.
- Learn to differentiate similar sounding words like "early," "bird," "word," and "worm."
- Master the pronunciation of complex words like "leopard" and "iron."

## How to Review

Test yourself by reading the first half of a proverb and trying to complete it from memory (e.g., "Don't count your chickens..."). Then, try to use one proverb in a conversation this week.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression / Proverb | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Don't plan too early | Don't count your chickens before they hatch | Wait until you actually have something before making plans based on it. | I will buy a new car when I get the bonus money. Don't count your chickens until they hatch! |
| Deeds matter | Actions speak louder than words | What you do is more important than what you say. | You promise you'll finish by Friday, but actions speak louder than words. |
| Be early | The early bird gets the worm | If you wake up or act early, you will be successful. | Let's arrive early to get the best seats; the early bird gets the worm. |
| Jealousy | The grass is always greener on the other side (of the fence) | Other people's lives always seem better than our own. | You think her job is easy, but the grass is always greener on the other side. |
| Don't risk everything | Don't put all your eggs in one basket | Don't put all your resources or efforts into one single place/plan. | Apply for several jobs so you don't put all your eggs in one basket. |
| Looks deceive | You can't judge a book by its cover | Don't judge the value of something simply by its appearance. | He doesn't look smart, but don't judge a book by its cover—he has a PhD. |
| Like parent | The apple doesn't fall far from the tree | A child has a similar character to their parents (often used negatively). | That politician is corrupt, and his father was the same; the apple doesn't fall far from the tree. |
| People don't change | A leopard doesn't change its spots | A person's true character never changes. | Be careful trusting him again; a leopard doesn't change its spots. |
| Act quickly | Strike while the iron is hot | Take advantage of a good opportunity while it lasts. | The value will increase soon; we need to strike while the iron is hot. |
| Don't hurt your provider | Don't bite the hand that feeds you | Don't make someone angry if they are paying or helping you. | I want to yell at my boss, but I shouldn't bite the hand that feeds me. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake | Meaning / natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Word | WURD (rhymes with bird) | WARD / rounding lips | Do not round lips for the "O". "Actions speak louder than words." |
| Leopard | LEP-urd | LEO-pard / LAIR-pard | The "eo" is just an "eh" sound. "A leopard doesn't change its spots." |
| Iron | I-ern | I-ron / I-own | The "r" is pronounced before the "o". "Strike while the iron is hot." |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The grass is always greener on the other side of the fence.
2. Don't put all your eggs in one basket.
3. You can't judge a book by its cover.
4. The apple doesn't fall far from the tree.
5. Strike while the iron is hot!

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe a time when you realized "actions speak louder than words."
2. Practice pronouncing "word," "worm," "early," and "bird" in a single sentence aloud.

## Mini Quiz

1. Which proverb means you shouldn't judge something by its outward appearance?
2. If you act quickly to take advantage of an opportunity, you are striking while the what is hot?
3. What animal "doesn't change its spots"?

### Answer Key

1. You can't judge a book by its cover.
2. The iron.
3. A leopard.

<br/>

category: "english-writing"

mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"

# 10 Useful Idioms From LA Natives

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

By analyzing a real conversation with John, a man living on a boat in Marina del Rey, this lesson breaks down natural, native-level idioms and expressions related to living situations, choices, and money.

## Key Points

- Learn real-world conversational phrases like "24/7", "going on [number]", and "what's not to love".
- Understand how native speakers casually discuss large amounts of money ("seven figures," "drop $5 million").
- Notice the relaxed American pronunciation, such as dropping the 'T' in '24' (twenty-four).

## How to Review

Imagine a perfect living situation. Write a few sentences describing it using "what's not to love," "24/7," and "ended up."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| All the time | 24/7 | 24 hours a day, 7 days a week; constantly. | Living on a boat is like being on vacation 24/7. |
| Cut the grass | Mow the lawn | Cutting the grass in front/behind a house. | I don't have to mow the lawn anymore. |
| Almost | Going on | Approaching a certain age or length of time. | I've lived here going on six years. |
| From here | Originally from the area | Born or raised in this specific location. | Are you originally from the area? |
| Finish at | End up | To finish or find yourself in a place unexpectedly. | I stuck a pin in a map and ended up in Arizona. |
| Regret | Look back | To regret a past decision. | I moved from a small town and I've never looked back. |
| Perfect | What's not to love? | A rhetorical question meaning it is perfect; there's nothing to dislike. | It has a big kitchen and it's clean; what's not to love? |
| One million dollars | Seven figures | A number with 7 digits (e.g., $1,000,000+). | They can spend seven figures on a boat. |
| My social group | The circle I run in | The group of people you spend time with. | Rich celebrities? That's not a circle I run in. |
| Spend a lot of money | To drop (money) | Slang for spending a large amount of cash. | He dropped five million dollars to buy a boat. |
| Good income | Fair source of income | A good salary; a lot of money. | You've got to have a fair source of income for that. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. That pharmacy is open 24/7.
2. I've worked there going on two years.
3. If he doesn't stop his criminal behavior, he'll end up in jail.
4. I moved from a small town and I've never looked back.
5. She's beautiful, intelligent, kind, and funny—what's not to love?
6. He makes in the high five figures.
7. We went to the same university, but we didn't run in the same circles.

## Practice Prompts

1. How did you "end up" in your current city or job?
2. Write a sentence describing a time you "dropped" a lot of money on something.
3. Pronounce 24/7 the American way (twen-ee-four seven).

## Mini Quiz

1. If someone makes "six figures," roughly how much money is that?
2. What idiom describes the specific group of friends you socialize with?
3. What phrase means "almost" when referring to time or age?

### Answer Key

1. $100,000 or more.
2. The circle I run in.
3. Going on.

title: "12 Advanced English Words to Use Instead of 'Very'"
description: "Elevate your professional and descriptive English by replacing the simple word 'very' with elegant, advanced adverbs."
category: "english-writing"
theme: "vocabulary"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"
visibility: "public"
language: "en"
tags: ["english-writing", "vocabulary", "professional-english", "youtube", "review"]
---

# 12 Advanced Words Instead of "Very"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Stop relying on the word "very" for everything. This lesson provides 12 sophisticated adverbs that will instantly make your English sound more fluent, elegant, and professional, which is crucial for occupational success.

## Key Points

- Expanding vocabulary is the best single predictor of occupational success.
- Learn precise adverbs like *woefully*, *exceedingly*, and *utterly*.
- Master pronunciation tips, such as the flap "T" in *utterly* and reducing the "a" in *particularly*.

## How to Review

Take a piece of writing or an email you've written recently. Find the word "very" and replace it with one of the advanced words from this lesson.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Very bad/sad | Woefully | Used for something extremely negative or inadequate. | We were woefully unprepared for the test. |
| Very good/difficult | Exceedingly | Better or more than standard. | You did exceedingly well in that class. |
| Very obvious | Undeniably | Absolutely true; impossible to deny. | He is undeniably qualified for the job. |
| Completely | Utterly | Complete or entire (often with extreme feelings). | That is utterly absurd. |
| Very much/suddenly | Dramatically | In a big, sudden, or intense way. | Your English has dramatically improved. |
| Very hard to believe | Unimaginably | Hard to comprehend or imagine. | It is unimaginably hard to do this. |
| Very expensive/hard | Inconceivably | Cannot be conceived or imagined. | The tickets were inconceivably expensive. |
| Very much | Extremely | To a large extent. | The process was extremely straightforward. |
| Very/Especially | Particularly | More than usual (often negative: not particularly). | That's a particularly dangerous job. |
| Very large/different | Vastly | Large in size, amount, or degree. | Humans are vastly more social than other mammals. |
| Very helpful/popular | Tremendously | Great, excellent, or intense. | The new software is tremendously helpful. |
| Very clear | Unmistakably | Cannot be mistaken; obvious. | The change was unmistakably evident. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Utterly | UD-er-ly | Pronouncing hard T's. Use a fast flap "D" instead. |
| Dramatically | dru-MAT-ik-ly | Saying "dra-". The "dr" sounds like "jra" or "dru". |
| Particularly | par-TIC-yu-ler-ly | Saying "par-tic-u-LAR-ly". Reduce the "a" to "er". |
| Vastly | VAST-ly | Use a big open American "A" (like apple). |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The school's textbooks are woefully out-of-date.
2. The storage space in the cabins is exceedingly generous.
3. I find their writing utterly repulsive.
4. Knowing your goals will dramatically increase your productivity.
5. I wasn't particularly happy with that decision.
6. Foreign laws can be vastly different than our own.
7. He was a tremendously thoughtful leader.

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe a time you were "utterly confused."
2. Write a sentence about two things that are "vastly different."
3. Think of a movie that was "inconceivably expensive" to make.

## Mini Quiz

1. Which word means "impossible to deny"?
2. Which word is used for something very negative or sad, like being unprepared?
3. Which word means "completely" and uses a flap 'D' sound in American English?

### Answer Key

1. Undeniably.
2. Woefully.
3. Utterly.

<br/>

title: "13 Useful English Phrasal Verbs and Idioms with the Word 'TALK'"
description: "Expand your conversational fluency by learning 13 native phrasal verbs and idioms centered around the verb 'talk'."
category: "english-writing"

mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"

---

# 13 Expressions with "TALK"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson teaches 13 common phrasal verbs and idioms containing the word "talk". You will learn how to persuade people, handle interruptions, and identify manipulators, all while perfecting your American "ah" vowel sound.

## Key Points

- Master separable and inseparable phrasal verbs with "talk."
- Learn to distinguish between "sweet talker" and "smooth talker."
- Perfect the open American "ah" sound used in words like *talk*, *walk*, and *call*.

## How to Review

Create a short dialogue between a boss who "talks down to" employees and an employee who refuses to be "talked into" doing extra work.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Persuade to do | Talk into | To convince someone to do something. | The agent talked us into buying the house. |
| Persuade not to do | Talk out of | To convince someone NOT to do something. | His friends talked him out of jumping. |
| Be condescending | Talk down to | To speak to someone as if they are stupid or inferior. | He thinks he's smarter, so he talks down to me. |
| Interrupt / talk loudly | Talk over | To speak while someone else is still talking. | Please stop talking over me and let me finish. |
| Discuss a problem | Talk it over | To discuss a situation to get advice/opinion. | I'd like to talk it over with my wife first. |
| Casual chat | Small talk | Light, informal conversation with strangers. | We engaged in small talk before the interview. |
| Reply rudely | Talk back | To answer disrespectfully (usually children to adults). | I wasn't allowed to talk back to my parents. |
| Empty promises | All talk | Someone who says they'll do things but never does. | She doesn't keep her promises; she's all talk. |
| Words don't matter | Talk is cheap | It's easy to say things; actions matter more. | He said he'd finish, but talk is cheap. |
| Manipulator | Smooth talker | Someone who manipulates using clever words. | Don't trust him; he's a smooth talker. |
| Flatterer | Sweet talker | Someone who says nice things to get what they want. | He tells girls what they want to hear; he's a sweet talker. |
| Talk too much | Talk someone's ear off | To exhaust someone by talking continuously. | Don't ask about politics, he'll talk your ear off. |
| Tired of talking | Talked out | Exhausted from talking too much. | After giving the lecture, I'm all talked out. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Talk | TAHK | The 'L' is silent. Open mouth wide for an American 'AH' sound. |
| Sentences | SEN-ten-sez or SEN-en-sez | Dropping the 'T' to say 'sen-en-sez' is common in fast natural speech. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The real estate agent talked us into buying the more expensive house.
2. She's in love with him, so no one can talk her out of marrying him.
3. The employees don't like their boss because he talks down to them.
4. Before I give you my answer, I'd like to talk it over with my wife.
5. They engaged in small talk before starting the job interview.
6. The sweet talker talked her into going out with him.
7. We've been on the phone for two hours; I'm all talked out.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence about a time someone "talked you into" buying something.
2. Describe someone who is "all talk."
3. Say aloud: "I walked and talked and called." Make sure all vowels sound like "ah".

## Mini Quiz

1. Which expression means to discuss a problem with someone to get their advice?
2. If someone is speaking while you are trying to finish your sentence, what are they doing?
3. Which idiom means that making promises is easy, but actually doing the work is hard?

### Answer Key

1. Talk it over.
2. Talking over you.
3. Talk is cheap.

<br/>

title: "15 Common Idioms with an American Accent"
description: "Sound like a native speaker by mastering 15 common idiomatic expressions that use the word 'hit'."
category: "english-writing"
theme: "idioms-and-proverbs"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"
visibility: "public"
language: "en"

---

# 15 Idioms with "Hit"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson focuses on 15 everyday English idioms built around the verb "hit." You will also refine your American accent by mastering the short "ih" vowel sound and learning how native speakers connect words with the letter "T".

## Key Points

- Learn various uses of "hit," from *hit the books* to *hit the jackpot*.
- Distinguish between the relaxed, short "ih" sound (hit, bit, fit) and the tense, long "ee" sound (heat, beat, feed).
- Practice "holding" the final T when the next word starts with a consonant (e.g., *hit the*).

## How to Review

Create a short story about a difficult day at school or work using *hit the books*, *hit the ceiling*, and *hit the sack*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Exactly right | Hit the nail on the head | To have exactly the correct answer or assessment. | When you said the battery was dead, you hit the nail on the head. |
| Study hard | Hit the books | To begin studying intensely. | If I don't hit the books, I'll fail. |
| Big success | Hit the jackpot | To win money or have a massive, lucky success. | You got the job? You really hit the jackpot! |
| Leave | Hit the road | To depart or leave a place. | We'd better hit the road before traffic gets worse. |
| Get very angry | Hit the ceiling (or roof) | To explode with anger. | Your boss will hit the ceiling when he finds out. |
| Go to sleep | Hit the sack | To go to bed. | I have to wake up early, so I'd better hit the sack. |
| Drink alcohol | Hit the bottle | To start drinking heavily. | He was doing okay, but he started to hit the bottle again. |
| Unfair attack | Hit below the belt | To say something inappropriately personal/cruel. | When he's angry, he hits below the belt. |
| Become famous | Hit it big | To become a massive success. | He was popular here, but he hit it big in New York. |
| Flee an accident | Hit and run | Causing a car accident and driving away illegally. | The hit and run driver was in a blue car. |
| Stop / Slow down | Hit the brakes | To stop doing something. | Let's hit the brakes on this project. |
| Perform perfectly | Hit it out of the park | To do something exceptionally well. | Your presentation was amazing; you hit it out of the park. |
| Flirt | Hit on (someone) | To express romantic interest aggressively. | Was he friendly, or was he hitting on me? |
| Connect instantly | Hit it off | To become friends immediately upon meeting. | We met on a blind date and hit it off right away. |
| A huge success | A hit | Something extremely popular (song, movie, idea). | Your delicious cake will be a hit at the party. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word/Sound | Correct pronunciation | Rule |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Hit vs. Heat | HIT vs. HEET | "Hit" uses a relaxed throat and tongue. "Heat" is tense. |
| Hit the | HI-(stop)-the | If 'T' is followed by a consonant, hold the 'T' (don't release it). |
| Hit it | HID-it | If 'T' is followed by a vowel, it becomes a fast flap 'D'. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. When you said my car needed a new battery, you hit the nail on the head.
2. If I don't hit the books this week, I won't pass next week's exam.
3. Your boss is going to hit the ceiling when he finds out about your mistake.
4. I have to get up early tomorrow, so I'd better hit the sack.
5. The singer was popular in his hometown, but he really hit it big when he moved to New York.
6. Was my presentation good? You hit it out of the park!
7. They went on a blind date, but unfortunately, they didn't hit it off.

## Practice Prompts

1. Say "hit the road" out loud. Did you hold the 'T'?
2. Say "hit it big" out loud. Did you use a fast 'D' for the 'T' in "hit"?
3. Write a sentence about a time you had to "hit the brakes" on a bad idea.

## Mini Quiz

1. Which expression means to go to bed?
2. If you do an exceptionally good job on a task, you hit it out of the what?
3. Which expression means to say something deeply personal and unfair in an argument?

### Answer Key

1. Hit the sack.
2. Park (or ballpark).
3. Hit below the belt.

title: "15 Useful Words - How to Describe a Place (Getty Museum in LA)"
description: "Expand your vocabulary with 15 advanced adjectives to describe beautiful places, featuring the Getty Museum."
category: "english-writing"

mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"

# Describing a Beautiful Place

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson teaches 15 advanced adjectives for describing beautiful places, architecture, and scenery. Using the Getty Museum in Los Angeles as an example, you will learn how native speakers naturally describe locations that impress them.

## Key Points

- Move beyond the word "beautiful" by using precise adjectives like *stunning*, *captivating*, and *picturesque*.
- Learn the difference between feeling *exhilarated* (excited and alive) and feeling *calm/invited*.
- Master pronunciation challenges like the silent 'a' in *extraordinary* and the 'ch' sound in *ancient*.

## How to Review

Think of your favorite place in your city. Write a short paragraph describing it using at least 5 adjectives from this lesson (e.g., bustling, striking, warm and inviting).

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Very beautiful | Stunning | Extremely impressive or beautiful. | You look stunning in that dress. |
| Excitingly beautiful | Spectacular | Beautiful in a dramatic, impressive way. | We got a hotel room with a spectacular view. |
| Mixed styles | Eclectic | A combination of different styles/elements. | He has eclectic taste in music. |
| Very special | Extraordinary | Unusual, not ordinary, exceptional. | You're making extraordinary progress in English. |
| Beautiful views | Scenic | Having beautiful or impressive natural scenery. | Let's take the scenic road on the way home. |
| Balanced perfectly | Symmetrical | Both sides are exactly the same or balanced. | The four buildings are perfectly symmetrical. |
| Very detailed | Intricate | Very complicated with fine details. | Her wedding dress has intricate details. |
| Very exciting | Exhilarating | Making you feel happy, excited, and alive. | The roller coaster ride was exhilarating. |
| Welcoming | Warm and inviting | Making you feel happy and welcome to be there. | You decorated your living room in a warm and inviting way. |
| Exact | Precise | Very accurate and paying attention to detail. | Please make sure your measurement is precise. |
| Crowded & noisy | Bustling | Noisy and crowded with a lot of energetic people. | New York is a bustling city. |
| Immediately noticeable | Striking | Noticeable because it is so beautiful or amazing. | She is strikingly beautiful. |
| Holds your attention | Captivating | So interesting and charming you can't look away. | The architecture and views are truly captivating. |
| Like a picture | Picturesque | Visually attractive, especially in a quaint way. | We visited a picturesque little town. |
| Very old | Ancient | From a very long time ago in history. | Jerusalem is an ancient city. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Extraordinary | ex-TROR-di-ner-ee | Do not say "extra-ordinary". The first 'a' is completely silent. |
| Ancient | AYN-shent | The 'c' sounds like an 'sh', and the 'a' is a long 'ay'. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The museum is stunningly beautiful.
2. The architecture in that neighborhood is eclectic.
3. That was an extraordinarily difficult test.
4. I had an exhilarating run in the forest today.
5. I was struck by the beauty of the museum.
6. We have a picturesque view from our hotel.
7. That museum holds a lot of ancient art.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence describing a "bustling" place you have visited.
2. What is something you own that is "intricate"?
3. Record yourself saying "extraordinary" making sure the 'a' is silent.

## Mini Quiz

1. Which word means "a combination of many different styles"?
2. Which word means "noisy, crowded, and full of energy"?
3. If a roller coaster makes you feel incredibly excited and alive, it is what?

### Answer Key

1. Eclectic.
2. Bustling.
3. Exhilarating.

title: "17 Phrasal Verbs with 'PICK' - English Fluency Practice"

category: "english-writing"

mood: "study"

---

# 17 Expressions with "Pick"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson explores the highly versatile verb "pick." You'll learn the crucial differences between *pick on*, *pick at*, and *pick over*, as well as 12 entirely distinct meanings for the phrasal verb *pick up*.

## Key Points

- Master the short, relaxed "ih" vowel in *pick* versus the tense "ee" in *peak*.
- Understand specific prepositional changes: *pick at* (eat without interest), *pick on* (bully), *pick over* (examine carefully).
- *Pick up* can mean to pay a bill, learn a skill naturally, catch an illness, improve your mood, or resume an activity.

## How to Review

Think of a time you "picked up" a new skill (like a language or hobby) just by being exposed to it. Write three sentences describing how you "picked it up."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Bully / criticize | Pick on | To bully or treat someone unfairly. | Why do you always pick on me when something goes wrong? |
| Eat without interest | Pick at | To play with food without eating much. | She's not hungry; she's just picking at her food. |
| Examine carefully | Pick over | To examine items to reject the bad ones. | The customers had already picked over all the sale items. |
| Analyze flaws | Pick apart | To deeply analyze something looking for flaws. | The lawyer picked apart the witness's testimony. |
| Recover from failure | Pick yourself up | To recover emotionally/physically after a setback. | Life can knock you down, but you must pick yourself up. |
| Resume an activity | Pick back up | To resume something after stopping. | Let's pick back up where we left off. |
| Romantic intro line | Pickup line | A sentence used to flirt with a stranger. | She laughed at his funny pickup line. |
| Pay the bill | Pick up the tab / check | To pay the bill for everyone in a group. | I'm going to go ahead and pick up the check. |
| Tidy a room | Pick up (a room) | To organize and clean up a space. | Make sure you pick up your bedroom. |
| Give someone a ride | Pick up (a person) | To collect someone in your car. | I'll pick up the children after school. |
| Get arrested | Pick up (by police) | To be apprehended or caught by police. | He was picked up by the police for questioning. |
| Flirt / get a date | Pick up (someone) | To start a romantic conversation with a stranger. | He goes to that club to pick up girls. |
| Improve mood | Pick-me-up | Something that makes you feel energetic or happy. | My morning coffee is a great pick-me-up. |
| Learn by exposure | Pick up (a skill) | To learn something informally by being around it. | I picked up Spanish while traveling in Mexico. |
| Catch an illness | Pick up (a virus) | To catch an infection or illness. | I must have picked up the flu from my grandma's house. |
| Increase intensity | Pick up (wind/convo) | To become stronger or more intense. | The wind has really picked up. |
| Detect/notice | Pick up on | To notice a feeling or clue through observation. | I picked up on the tension in the room. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Pick vs. Peak | PIHK vs. PEEK | "Pick" requires a relaxed tongue (short 'ih'). "Peak" requires a tense tongue (long 'ee'). |
| Picked up | PIKT-up | The 'ed' sounds like a 'T', which connects directly to the 'u' in 'up'. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. He was constantly picked on because of his glasses.
2. Stop picking at your food and eat.
3. Don't pick apart every little mistake; focus on the big picture.
4. Let's pick back up where we left off in our discussion.
5. The company decided to pick up the expenses for our business trip.
6. This place looks messy; you need to pick up around here.
7. Your encouraging words really picked me up during a difficult time.
8. I picked up on his disappointment from the tone of his voice.

## Practice Prompts

1. Use "pick up on" to describe a time you noticed a friend was secretly sad.
2. Write a sentence explaining why you decided to "pick up the tab" at dinner.
3. What is a song or drink that acts as a good "pick-me-up" for you?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you are examining a basket of fruit to find the best ones, you are doing what to the fruit?
2. If you resume a conversation exactly where you stopped, what phrasal verb do you use?
3. What does it mean if you "pick up" Spanish while traveling?

### Answer Key

1. Picking it over.
2. Pick back up.
3. You learned it naturally through exposure, not formal study.

# 17 Expressions During a Crisis

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this socially-distanced interview, Drake shares his experience losing work during the pandemic. Through his natural speech, you will learn essential phrases regarding notice periods, workplace arrangements, unemployment, and emotional coping strategies.

## Key Points

- Master work-related terms: *give notice*, *file for unemployment*, *accrue PTO*, and *cash in*.
- Learn time-sequence phrases: *every other*, *in a row*, and *going on*.
- Understand phrases describing emotional states: *panic mode*, *spare time on my hands*, and *see the light at the end of the tunnel*.

## How to Review

Write a brief email to a fictional employer giving your "two-week notice" and requesting to "cash in" your "accrued PTO."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Tell in advance | Give notice | Informing an employer you are leaving (usually 2 weeks). | They gave us a one-day notice. |
| Eat at restaurant | Dine in | Eating food inside the restaurant. | We prefer to dine in, but we have to order takeout. |
| Look at | Check out | To look at, observe, or notice something. | Check out that beautiful sunset. |
| Alternating | Every other | Skipping one in between (e.g., Monday, Wed, Fri). | I exercise every other day. |
| Without stopping | In a row | Doing something consecutively without breaks. | I exercised six days in a row. |
| Customers/business | Traffic | The volume of people visiting a store or website. | Our restaurant traffic slowed down quite a bit. |
| Apply for benefits | File for unemployment | Submitting paperwork to get paid when you lose a job. | He lost his job, so he needs to file for unemployment. |
| Accumulate | Accrue | To grow or accumulate over time (usually money or time off). | I have 50 hours of accrued PTO. |
| Exchange for money | Cash in | To receive money for something you give/have saved. | If I cash that in, that's what I'll get. |
| Approximately | Roughly | About, approximately. | It takes roughly two hours to get there. |
| Dollars (slang) | Bucks | Casual slang for dollars. | They'll send you 500 bucks a week. |
| Not very | Not that [adjective] | A casual way to say "not so much" or "not very." | Is it painful? It's not that bad. |
| State of panic | In panic mode | The current condition or state of feeling intense fear. | Everyone is in panic mode right now. |
| Extra free time | Spare time on my hands | Having extra time available to you currently. | Everyone has a lot of spare time on their hands. |
| Have hope | See the light at the end of the tunnel | Having hope that a bad situation will soon end. | They finally see the light at the end of the tunnel. |
| Exactly the same | No exception / no different | A stronger way to say "not an exception." | This situation is no exception. |

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Two-week notice:** Do not say "two weeks notice." "Notice" is the noun, so "two-week" acts as an adjective and is singular.
- **Every other:** Do not say "every second day." Say "every other day."

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I gave my two-week notice to my employer.
2. They had us seating people every other table.
3. Last week, I exercised six days in a row.
4. A lot of companies are urging employees to file for unemployment.
5. If you don't pay your credit card bill on time, late fees will accrue.
6. He needed money, so he cashed in his gold coins.
7. I want to try to see the light at the end of the tunnel.

## Practice Prompts

1. Explain the difference between working "six days in a row" and "every other day."
2. Write a sentence explaining why someone might "file for unemployment."
3. Use "bucks" and "roughly" in a single sentence describing a purchase.

## Mini Quiz

1. What phrase describes exchanging saved vacation time (PTO) for money?
2. If a website gets a lot of visitors, it gets a lot of what?
3. What is the grammatically correct way to tell your boss you are leaving in 14 days?

### Answer Key

1. Cashing it in.
2. Traffic.
3. "I am giving my two-week notice."

<br/>

---

# Advanced Words From News Headlines

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Reading English news headlines can be confusing because journalists use advanced, punchy vocabulary. This lesson decodes 20 high-level verbs and adjectives commonly used in journalism, allowing you to discuss current events with true fluency.

## Key Points

- Differentiate easily confused words like *seize* (take by force) vs. *cease* (stop).
- Learn metaphorical verbs used in news: *derail*, *overshadow*, *echo*, and *cloud*.
- Master advanced adjectives to describe tone and situations: *fragile*, *somber*, and *scarce*.

## How to Review

Read a headline from an English news website today. Try to summarize the article aloud, purposefully incorporating words like *weighing options*, *looming*, or *overshadowed*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Take by force | Seize | To capture or control by force/law (pronounced with Z). | The police seized the stolen goods. |
| Stop | Cease | To stop doing something (pronounced with S). | The noise finally ceased. |
| Temporary stop of war | Ceasefire | A temporary pause in fighting. | Both sides agreed to a ceasefire. |
| Permanent stop of war | Truce | A longer or permanent agreement to stop fighting. | They called a truce after years of conflict. |
| Lessened / quieted | Muted | Made quieter, less intense, or less noticeable. | The joy is muted by doubt over long-term peace. |
| Thinking carefully | Weighing options | Thinking carefully about choices before deciding. | She weighed the pros and cons before buying. |
| Ruin a plan | Derail | To cause a plan or career to fail unexpectedly. | The bad weather derailed their travel plans. |
| Tax on imports | Tariff | A specific tax placed on imported/exported goods. | The government imposed new tariffs on steel. |
| Make less noticeable | Overshadow | To make something seem less important by comparison. | The scandal overshadowed his career success. |
| Repeat/Agree | Echo | To repeat or agree with someone's words or ideas. | Her concerns echoed those of the public. |
| Make unclear | Cloud | To make something difficult to understand or see. | Fear is clouding your judgment. |
| Exceed | Top | To go over a certain amount or expectation. | The damage topped $1 million. |
| Very serious/sad | Somber | Very serious, gloomy, or depressing. | The mood in the room was somber after the bad news. |
| Lack of something | Scarcity / Scarce | Not enough of something. | There is a scarcity of clean water. |
| Took a long time | Years in the making | Something that took years to develop or happen. | The new policy was years in the making. |
| Block / prevent | Stymie | To block or prevent the progress of something. | Health problems stymied his career growth. |
| Rule / control | Reign | To rule, dominate, or be in control (spelled r-e-i-g-n). | Confusion reigned during the emergency. |
| Threatening to happen | Loom | To appear large, threatening, and likely to happen soon. | An economic crisis is looming. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Seize vs Cease | SEEZ vs. SEES | The 'z' vibrates; the 's' does not. |
| Fragile | FRA-jul | In American English, it ends in "jul". In British English, it ends in "jile" (like mile). |
| Reign | RAYN | The 'g' is completely silent. |
| Uncertainty | un-SER-ten-tee | Hold the 'T's; do not drop them. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. His reaction was muted compared to hers.
2. I am weighing my options for a new job.
3. The project was derailed by funding issues.
4. Trump's talks about tariffs overshadowed the other topics.
5. His beliefs echoed my own.
6. Uncertainty clouded the team's decision.
7. Her performance topped everyone's expectations.
8. The teacher wore a somber expression.
9. Her success didn't happen overnight; it was years in the making.
10. The deadline is looming, and we still have a lot of work.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence about a bad event that "derailed" your plans.
2. Use "weighing my options" to describe a difficult decision you have to make.
3. Describe a situation where "confusion reigned."

## Mini Quiz

1. If you are comparing two choices carefully, you are doing what to your options?
2. What verb means to block or prevent progress?
3. If an event is threatening to happen very soon, it is doing what?

### Answer Key

1. Weighing.
2. Stymie.
3. Looming.

<br/>

title: "20 Business English Expressions for Fluent English"
description: "Test and review 20 vital corporate expressions, from 'subpar' and 'high grossing' to 'cut costs' and 'back out'."
category: "english-writing"

mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"

---

# 20 Business English Expressions

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson serves as a review and test of 20 high-level Business English expressions. Using these phrases instead of literal translations will make you sound highly professional and native-level in corporate environments.

## Key Points

- Replace basic descriptors with professional ones (e.g., *bad quality* -> *subpar*; *makes a lot of money* -> *high grossing*).
- Master project-management idioms like *sign off on*, *up in the air*, and *get going*.
- Understand specific prepositional choices in business, such as paying *up front* and moving *up in the company*.

## How to Review

Draft an imaginary email to a project manager using the phrases *time sensitive*, *cut costs*, *sign off on*, and *up in the air*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Below average | Subpar | Not as good as expected; poor quality. | The quality of that work is subpar. |
| Area of specialty | In the field | Working in a specific industry or profession. | How long have you been in the field? |
| Right skills | Skill set | A specific group of skills for a job. | He doesn't have the skill set for the job. |
| Get a promotion | Move one's way up | To get promoted within the same company. | He moved his way up in the company. |
| Urgent | Time sensitive | Needing to be done right away; cannot wait. | This document is time sensitive. |
| Make understood | Get my point across | To successfully communicate an idea to others. | Did I get my point across clearly? |
| Makes lots of money | High grossing | Generating significant financial revenue. | That is a high grossing store. |
| Approve | Sign off on | To officially approve a document or budget. | Did the boss sign off on the budget? |
| Pay in advance | Up front | Paying before the work begins. | You need to pay 50% up front. |
| Spend less | Cut costs | To figure out ways to reduce business expenses. | Let's figure out a way to cut costs. |
| Become successful | Taking off | Suddenly becoming very successful or popular. | Their business is really taking off. |
| Existed | Been around | Having existed for a certain amount of time. | Our company has been around for 30 years. |
| Good social skills | People skills | The ability to communicate well with others. | He's a good consultant because he has people skills. |
| Undecided | Up in the air | Uncertain; a plan that is not yet finalized. | The plan to start construction is up in the air. |
| Don't change plans | Stick to (our plans) | To continue with the original plan. | Let's stick to our original plans. |
| Cancel a commitment | Back out of | To withdraw from an agreement or project. | They backed out of the project. |
| Start | Get going | To begin doing something without delay. | Let's get going on the project. |
| Tell boss I'm quitting | Give notice | Informing an employer you will leave. | I gave my two-week notice yesterday. |
| Skip one | Every other | Alternating (e.g., working Monday, skipping Tuesday). | I work every other Saturday. |
| Making no progress | Not getting anywhere | Failing to improve or make progress. | We're arguing but we're not getting anywhere. |
| Making progress | Getting somewhere | Finally improving or making progress. | We are finally getting somewhere. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Point across | POIN-uh-cross | The 't' in 'point' is dropped when speaking quickly. |
| You'd cut costs | YOOD cut costs | "You would" contracts to "You'd". |
| Two-week notice | TWO-week notice | Do not say "two weeks". It acts as an adjective here. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I've worked in the field since my first job in college.
2. When we hire, we look at someone's resume and skill set.
3. Did I get my point across?
4. She needs her boss's approval to sign off on something.
5. Things really took off for me after the election.
6. The plan to start construction is still up in the air.
7. I hope they don't back out of the project.
8. We finally got going on the project yesterday.

## Practice Prompts

1. What is a "skill set" you possess that is valuable in your field?
2. Describe a time you had a plan, but a friend "backed out."
3. Why is it important to have good "people skills"?

## Mini Quiz

1. If a document must be signed immediately because it cannot wait, it is what?
2. What phrase means a project is currently uncertain and undecided?
3. If a business starts making a lot of money very quickly, you can say the business is what?

### Answer Key

1. Time sensitive.
2. Up in the air.
3. Taking off.

title: "20 Business Expressions You Should Know for Fluent English"
description: "Learn advanced business English expressions for communication, leadership, and professional situations from an executive coach."
category: "english-writing"
