---

# 20 Executive Business Expressions

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, you will learn high-level professional vocabulary from an interview with an executive business coach. These expressions focus on emotional intelligence, soft skills, and workplace dynamics, helping you sound more confident and articulate in professional environments.

## Key Points

- Master phrases for expressing your role: *go by*, *job title*, and *make a shift*.
- Learn terms related to emotional intelligence: *get triggered*, *get in the way of*, and *innately*.
- Understand key workplace dynamics: *get on the same page*, *going through the motions*, and *moving up through the company*.

## How to Review

Think of a time at work when you and a colleague had a misunderstanding. Write a brief paragraph explaining how you were able to "get on the same page" and avoid getting "emotionally triggered."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Job name | Job title | The official name of your job position. | What is your job title? |
| Also called | Go by | The name/title you use or prefer to be called. | I go by the term 'business coach'. |
| Prevent | Get in the way of | To prevent something from happening or succeeding. | Don't let emotions get in the way of your goals. |
| Naturally | Innately | Instinctively; something you are born knowing how to do. | People don't innately know how to do this. |
| Express carefully | Phrase (something) | To express something in a particular way. | Be careful how you phrase your criticism. |
| Become highly emotional | Get triggered | To have a strong emotional reaction (anger/fear) to something. | If I'm very direct, they might get triggered. |
| Take control | Take over | To assume control of a situation. | When we get emotional, the amygdala takes over. |
| Disconnected | Offline | Disconnected; not functioning or available. | It takes our executive brain function offline. |
| Good judgment | Common sense | The ability to think logically and practically. | All our common sense goes away when emotional. |
| Communicate | Deliver a message | To express yourself or communicate information. | He doesn't know how to deliver a message effectively. |
| Agree / align | Get on the same page | To have the same understanding or agreement. | It's important to get on the same page. |
| Interpersonal skills | Soft skills | Non-technical skills (communication, leadership). | This falls into the category of soft skills. |
| Working without care | Going through the motions | Doing a job without effort or interest. | He doesn't care; he's just going through the motions. |
| Getting promoted | Moving up | Getting promotions inside a company. | He is moving up through the company rapidly. |
| Change / transition | Make a shift | To change from one position or mindset to another. | You have to make the shift from expert to leader. |
| Get involved in | Get into | To enter a situation (often a difficult one). | They do not know what they are getting into. |
| Smart / intelligent | Sharp | Very intelligent or quick-witted. | You'll learn these skills quickly because you're sharp. |
| This is your role | Where you come in | The point where your specific role or help is needed. | It's not enough, and that's where you come in. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Entrepreneur | ahn-truh-pruh-NUR | Saying it with an "en" sound instead of the French "ahn". |
| How to | HOW-doo / HAR-oo | Fast speech turns the 't' into a soft 'd' or flap 'r'. |
| Conflict (noun) | CON-flict | Stress the first syllable for the noun. |
| Conflict (verb) | con-FLICT | Stress the second syllable for the verb. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Sometimes I just go by the term 'business coach'.
2. What kinds of things get in the way of them being effective leaders?
3. We don't innately know how to do that.
4. How do you phrase something so the other person can hear it?
5. They're going to get emotionally triggered.
6. All our good common sense goes away when we're too emotional.
7. It's really important to get on the same page.
8. He doesn't care about his job; he's just going through the motions.
9. They're happy about the promotion, but they don't know what they're getting into.
10. You'll learn them because you're sharp.

## Practice Prompts

1. What is a "soft skill" that is highly valued in your industry?
2. Write a sentence describing a time you had to "make a shift" in your career.
3. Have you ever started a project and realized you didn't know what you were "getting into"? Describe it.

## Mini Quiz

1. If you agree with your coworkers and understand the goal, you are all on what?
2. If someone does their job with zero enthusiasm or care, they are just going through the what?
3. If an event makes you suddenly very angry or fearful based on past experiences, you got what?

### Answer Key

1. On the same page.
2. Going through the motions.
3. Triggered.

title: "20 Common English Expressions with a Native Speaker"
description: "Learn casual, conversational expressions by listening to a native speaker who works as a boat captain."
category: "english-writing"

# 20 Conversational Expressions

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In an interview with a boat captain in Lake Tahoe, you will learn natural, casual expressions used to describe hobbies, careers, and social interactions. These phrases will make your everyday conversation sound much more authentic.

## Key Points

- Learn social expressions: *people person*, *hit it off*, and *make conversation*.
- Master hobby phrases: *big snowboarder*, *got into*, and *ended up*.
- Understand descriptive phrasing: *absolutely massive*, *choppy*, *year-round*, and *insane*.

## How to Review

Write a short paragraph about how you met your best friend. Use the expressions *hit it off*, *ended up*, and *make conversation*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Have the chance to | Get to | Having the opportunity to do something enjoyable. | I get to meet people from all over the country. |
| Very social person | People person | An extrovert who naturally enjoys talking to others. | You need to be a good people person. |
| Small talk | Make conversation | Speaking to strangers to be polite or friendly. | He has a talent for making conversation with strangers. |
| Finally | Lastly | Used to introduce a final point (instead of "in conclusion"). | And lastly, you need to know how to park a boat. |
| Connect immediately | Hit it off | Becoming friendly with someone the moment you meet. | Him and I hit it off immediately. |
| Huge fan/enthusiast | A big (noun) | Being very passionate or interested in an activity. | I'm a big snowboarder. |
| Resulted in | Ended up (+ing) | How a situation finished or resulted. | We ended up going out to lunch together. |
| Become interested | Get into | Becoming passionate or involved in a new hobby. | In college, I got really into cliff jumping. |
| Very large | Absolutely massive | Huge; incredibly large. | Lake Tahoe is absolutely massive. |
| Rough waves | Choppy | Having many small, rough waves (or disconnected speech). | The lake gets choppy in the afternoons. |
| Number two | The second (+est) | The item immediately following the absolute most/best. | It's the second deepest lake in the US. |
| Yes (casual) | Yup | A very common, casual way to say "yes". | Yup, we have the cleanest water. |
| All year | Year-round | Happening throughout the entire year. | The homes are lived in year-round. |
| Vacation | To vacation | Used as a verb: to spend a holiday somewhere. | They started wanting to vacation in Lake Tahoe. |
| Short trip | Getaway | A short, spontaneous trip or escape. | It's a close getaway for San Francisco residents. |
| Crazy / unbelievable | Insane | Crazy or unbelievable (often relating to price/events). | 75 million dollars? That's insane! |
| Negative aspect | Downside (to) | A bad or negative aspect of a situation. | The one downside to living there is the heat. |
| Many | Multiple | More than one; many. | We had multiple 100-degree days. |
| At least that | If not more | Meaning "at least that much, possibly more." | We had 100 days over 100 degrees, if not more. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Get to | GET-too | Make sure to link the words smoothly without pausing. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I get to travel a lot for my job.
2. She's a people person who likes to make conversation with customers.
3. We hit it off right away and ended up getting married six months later.
4. I tried watching that movie, but I just couldn't get into it.
5. This house is absolutely massive.
6. The sea is choppy today because of the wind.
7. Santa Barbara is a perfect weekend getaway.
8. That is an insane amount of money!
9. Are there any downsides to buying this house?

## Practice Prompts

1. What is a hobby that you are "really into"?
2. What is the one "downside to" living in your current city?
3. Think of a time your plans changed. What did you "end up doing" instead?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you start a new hobby and really enjoy it, you are really what?
2. If two people meet and immediately become great friends, they did what?
3. What is a more casual way to say "in conclusion" when giving a list of points?

### Answer Key

1. Getting into it.
2. Hit it off.
3. Lastly.

# 20 Modern American Slang Words

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Slang changes with every generation. In this lesson, you will learn modern internet and youth slang to help you understand American pop culture, YouTube videos, and casual conversations with younger generations. (Remember: avoid these in professional settings!)

## Key Points

- Learn social/vibe slang: *chillin'*, *lit*, and *woke*.
- Understand slang for negative feelings: *salty*, *shook*, and *done with it*.
- Master internet/texting slang: *ghosting*, *I'm dead*, and *canceled*.

## How to Review

Watch a casual YouTube vlog or read comments on social media. Try to spot at least three of these modern slang words being used in the wild.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Relaxing | Chillin' | Relaxing, hanging out, having a good vibe. | Everything's going good; I've just been chillin'. |
| Perfect / flawless | On fleek | Looking perfect (often used for eyebrows/hair/outfits). | Your eyebrows are on fleek. |
| Bitter / upset | Salty | Being upset or bitter over something minor. | He was salty because he lost the game. |
| Insult subtly | Throw shade | Making a subtle, indirect insult about someone. | He was throwing shade at me in front of the boss. |
| Amazing party | Lit | Exciting, fun, or amazing (usually describing an event). | Is that party going to be lit? |
| Show off | Flex | To show off your wealth, body, or belongings. | He's just flexing his new car. |
| Scared / shocked | Shook | Extremely shocked, surprised, or scared. | When the dog fell in the water, he was shook. |
| Give up | I'm done with it | To be completely finished or over a situation. | I can't take this anymore; I'm done with it. |
| Socially aware | Woke | Being aware of social injustices, lies, or truth. | He's very woke; he doesn't believe the media. |
| Ignore suddenly | Ghost (someone) | To abruptly cut off all communication with someone. | I was texting him, and then I got ghosted. |
| Laughing very hard | I'm dead / weak | Laughing so hard that you feel "dead" or "weak". | That meme was so funny; I'm dead. |
| Dismiss someone | Bye Felicia | A dismissive way to tell someone annoying to leave. | You're annoying me; Bye Felicia. |
| Do perfectly | Slay | To do something incredibly well; to look amazing. | You slayed that outfit! |
| Greatest Of All Time | GOAT | Acronym for Greatest Of All Time (sports/music). | Michael Jordan is the GOAT. |
| Boycott socially | Canceled | Publicly rejecting someone for doing something wrong. | He said something terrible, so he is canceled. |

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Shook:** This is grammatically incorrect (the proper word is "shaken"), but "shook" is the accepted slang term.
- **Ghost:** Usually a noun, but in modern slang, it is used as a verb ("I ghosted him" / "I was ghosted").

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. We're just chillin' at the house.
2. He's a really chill guy.
3. Don't be salty just because I won.
4. She was throwing shade at me all night.
5. That concert last night was so lit!
6. He's always flexing his expensive watches on Instagram.
7. I couldn't believe what happened; I was so shook.
8. That joke was hilarious, I'm dead.
9. You completely slayed that dance routine.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence describing an event that was "lit."
2. What does it mean if you are texting someone and suddenly they "ghost" you?
3. If someone tells a hilarious joke, what are two slang phrases you can say to show you are laughing hard?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you are showing off your new expensive shoes, what are you doing?
2. If someone is bitter because they lost a game, they are being what?
3. What acronym is used to describe the absolute best athlete in a sport?

### Answer Key

1. Flexing.
2. Salty.
3. GOAT (Greatest Of All Time).

# 20 Ways to Use "Deal"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

The word "deal" is incredibly versatile in English. It can mean a bargain, a negotiation, a problem, or an agreement. This lesson covers 20 different expressions using the word "deal," categorized for both business and daily life.

## Key Points

- Use "deal" for agreements: *it's a deal*, *close the deal*, and *done deal*.
- Use "deal" for bargains: *a good deal* and *sweeten the deal*.
- Use "deal" for situations/problems: *deal with it*, *deal breaker*, and *no big deal*.

## How to Review

Write a short dialogue where two people negotiate the price of a used car. Try to use *let's make a deal*, *sweeten the deal*, and *it's a deal*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| I agree | It's a deal | An expression used to finalize an agreement. | You want $5,000? Okay, it's a deal. |
| Let's negotiate | Let's make a deal | An invitation to negotiate and compromise. | Let's make a deal; how about $5,500? |
| A good price | A good/great deal | Buying something at a very favorable price. | You got a really good deal on that car. |
| A lot of | A great deal of | A large amount of something. | He has a great deal of money. |
| Finalized | A done deal | An agreement that is completely finalized. | After you sign the contract, it's a done deal. |
| Manipulate for profit | Wheel and deal | Dishonestly manipulating business for self-profit. | He made his money by wheeling and dealing. |
| Manipulative seller | Wheeler dealer | A salesperson who is dishonest and manipulative. | Stay away from him; he's a wheeler dealer. |
| Make an offer better | Sweeten the deal | Adding something extra to make an offer attractive. | The seller sweetened the deal by leaving the furniture. |
| Reason to cancel | Deal breaker | A specific issue that causes a negotiation/relationship to end. | Not wanting kids was a deal breaker for her. |
| Finalize the contract | Close the deal | To reach a final agreement in business. | We finally closed the deal after months of talks. |
| Suspicious agreement | Shady deal | An agreement that seems dishonest or illegal. | He was involved in many shady deals. |
| Not a problem | No big deal | Something that is not inconvenient or not serious. | It's no big deal; I can drive you to the airport. |
| Don't overreact | Don't make a big deal out of it | Advising someone not to turn a small issue into a crisis. | Relax, don't make a big deal out of it. |
| Handle a situation | Deal with (something) | To handle, manage, or accept a difficult situation. | You need to learn to deal with difficult people. |
| Distribute cards | Deal cards | Giving out playing cards in a game. | It's your turn to deal the cards. |
| Car seller | Car dealer / dealership | The person or place that sells cars. | There is a Toyota dealership near my house. |
| Authentic/excellent | The real deal | A person or thing that is genuine, authentic, and excellent. | That jazz musician is amazing; he's the real deal. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Deal | DEE-ul | Do not abruptly stop the tongue. Use a dark 'L' and a prolonged vowel. |
| Salesman | SAYLZ-mun | Reduce the "man" sound to "mun." |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I got a lot of good deals at the shopping mall last weekend.
2. After you sign the contract, it's a done deal.
3. Stay away from that salesman; he's a wheeler dealer.
4. The seller of the house sweetened the deal by offering to leave some furniture.
5. After many months of negotiation, we finally closed the deal.
6. Thank you for driving me; it's no big deal, it's on my way.
7. I can't deal with this traffic anymore.
8. That politician is honest and speaks from his heart; he's the real deal.

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe a time you got a "great deal" on a purchase.
2. What is a strict "deal breaker" for you when dating someone?
3. Write a sentence telling a friend that their minor mistake is "no big deal."

## Mini Quiz

1. If you add free floor mats to the sale of a car to make the buyer happy, you are doing what to the deal?
2. If someone is overreacting to a tiny scratch on their car, what should you tell them not to do?
3. What do you call a highly authentic, talented, and genuine person?

### Answer Key

1. Sweetening the deal.
2. Don't make a big deal out of it.
3. The real deal.

category: "english-writing"

mood: "study"

language: "en"

---

# 20 Advanced Pronunciation Fixes

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Even advanced English learners often carry over pronunciation mistakes from their early studies. This lesson identifies 20 commonly mispronounced words, highlighting silent letters, unexpected vowel sounds, and shifting syllable stress.

## Key Points

- Beware of silent letters: *salmon* (silent L), *bomb* (silent B), *extraordinary* (silent A).
- Note unexpected sounds: *chaos* / *choir* ('ch' sounds like 'k'), and *pizza* ('zz' sounds like 'ts').
- Differentiate American vs. British: *aluminum* (4 syllables) vs. *aluminium* (5 syllables).

## How to Review

Read the list of words in the vocabulary section aloud. Record yourself speaking them, then play the recording back to see if you accidentally pronounced the silent letters.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake / spelling trick |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Broccoli | BRAH-klee | The second 'o' is completely silent. |
| Salmon | SA-mun | The 'l' is completely silent. |
| Aluminum | uh-LOO-mi-num | American spelling ends in -um. British is -ium (aluminium). |
| Chaos | KAY-ahs | The 'ch' makes a 'K' sound. |
| Bombing | BAH-ming | The second 'b' is completely silent. |
| Choir | KWY-er | The 'ch' makes a 'K' sound, followed by a 'W' sound. |
| Close (verb) | CLOZE | Used as a verb, the 's' vibrates like a 'z'. |
| Close (adj) | CLOCE | Used as an adjective, the 's' is soft like a snake. |
| Extraordinary | ex-TROR-di-ner-ee | The first 'a' is completely silent. |
| Foreign | FOR-in | The 'g' is silent, and the ending is reduced to "in." |
| Heir | AIR | The 'h' is completely silent. (Female: heiress). |
| Hierarchy | HY-er-ar-kee | Pronounce the "r" clearly in the middle. |
| Lease | LEES | Rhymes with "peace," not "please." |
| Pizza | PEET-suh | The 'zz' makes a 'ts' sound. |
| Protein | PRO-teen | The "ei" makes a single "ee" sound. |
| Series | SEE-reez | Do not confuse with "serious." |
| Southern | SUH-thern | The "ou" makes an "uh" sound, unlike "South." |
| Thermometer | ther-MAH-mi-ter | The stress falls on the second syllable ("MOM"). |
| Of | UV | The 'f' sounds like a 'v'. |
| Receipt | re-SEET | The 'p' is completely silent. |
| Recipe | REH-si-pee | The 'e' at the end is pronounced as "ee." |

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Series vs Serious:** *Series* (SEE-reez) is a noun for a TV show. *Serious* (SEER-ee-us) is an adjective for a grave situation.
- **Of vs Off:** *Of* uses a soft 'v' sound (UV). *Off* uses a hard 'f' sound (AWF).

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I had broccoli with salmon.
2. I covered the salmon and broccoli with aluminum foil.
3. After the party, the house was in complete chaos.
4. She sings in a choir.
5. Please close the door; the supermarket is close to my house.
6. Prince Charles is the heir to the throne.
7. We signed a six-month lease for our new apartment.
8. Let's order a large pepperoni pizza.
9. Fish is a good source of protein.
10. That show is my favorite TV series.
11. He lives in the South, so he has a Southern accent.
12. I can't find my receipt.
13. Can you give me the recipe?

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying: "The heir to the throne eats salmon and broccoli for protein."
2. Explain the pronunciation difference between the noun "South" and the adjective "Southern."
3. Explain the pronunciation difference between "receipt" and "recipe."

## Mini Quiz

1. Which letter is silent in the word "salmon"?
2. Does the word "chaos" start with a "CH" sound or a "K" sound?
3. Which syllable is stressed in the word "thermometer"?

### Answer Key

1. The L.
2. A "K" sound.
3. The second syllable (ther-MAH-mi-ter).

title: "21 Professional English Expressions You Should Know"

# 21 Professional Expressions

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, you will learn 21 professional English expressions from an interview with Grant, a medical device sales representative. You will learn useful expressions for the workplace, networking, setting goals, and adapting to changes.

## Key Points

- Expressions for communication: *reach out blind*, *reiterate*, and *have a word with you*.
- Expressions for situations: *go with the flow*, *pan out*, and *take your breath away*.
- Expressions for careers/goals: *recession-proof*, *people skills*, *nest egg*, and *bright future ahead of you*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph describing your long-term career goals. In your paragraph, use the expressions *nest egg*, *people skills*, and *bright future ahead of you*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Contact without knowing | Reach out blind | To contact someone without any prior knowledge or connection to them. | One of the most difficult aspects is having to reach out blind. |
| Accept the situation | Go with the flow | To accept a situation without trying to change or control it. | You have to be flexible and go with the flow. |
| Economy-safe | Recession-proof | Not affected by a bad economy. | The medical field is almost recession-proof. |
| The absolute max | As [adj] as it gets | The maximum level of something. | It's about as stable as it can get. |
| Social abilities | People skills | The ability to communicate and interact well with others. | You need both technical background and people skills. |
| Be defensive | Have a guard up | To be cautious and protect oneself from getting hurt or manipulated. | They are skeptical and usually have a guard up. |
| Work out / happen | Pan out | To happen or develop in a certain way (often used negatively). | My plan to go to optometry school didn't pan out. |
| Plus extra | And then some | And even more; in addition to what was already mentioned. | Doctors go to medical school, and then some. |
| Between 30 and 40 | Thirty-something | Between the ages of 30 and 40. | I didn't want to be thirty-something when I graduated. |
| Somewhat small | Smallish | Quite small; kind of small. | I came from a smallish town in Ohio. |
| Except for | Apart from | Besides; other than that. | Apart from the weather, the biggest difference is the diversity. |
| Say again | Reiterate | To say something again to emphasize it. | Let me reiterate the importance of this meeting. |
| To such an extent | So much so that | To a very great extent. | There are beautiful views, so much so that I take pictures constantly. |
| Traffic light | Stoplight | A red, yellow, and green light that controls traffic. | I come to a stoplight and just appreciate the view. |
| Amaze someone | Take one's breath away | To be extremely beautiful, amazing, or spectacular. | The view took my breath away. |
| Imagine your future | See yourself doing | To imagine your future goals or career. | What do you see yourself doing in five years? |
| Successful future | Bright future ahead of you | Having a strong likelihood of success in the future. | You work hard, so you have a bright future ahead of you. |
| Savings for later | Nest egg | Money saved for the future, especially for retirement. | I want to build up a nest egg. |
| Smart people agree | Great minds think alike | A proverb used when two people have the same thought or idea. | We had the same idea—great minds think alike! |

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **-ish suffix:** The suffix `-ish` can be added to numbers, times, and adjectives to mean "approximately" or "kind of." Examples: *four-ish* (around 4 o'clock), *forty-ish* (around 40 years old), *brownish* (kind of brown).
- **Stoplight:** Interchangeable with *traffic light* and *red light*.

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. He had to reach out blind to potential clients.
2. We don't have plans tonight; we're just going to go with the flow.
3. This industry is generally considered recession-proof.
4. It's a great job, but you really need good people skills.
5. She always has her guard up when meeting new people.
6. My attempt to start a new business didn't pan out.
7. We paid a million dollars for that house, and then some.
8. Let me reiterate: you must submit the report by Friday.
9. She was so tired, so much so that she fell asleep at her desk.
10. The sunset over the ocean took my breath away.
11. What do you see yourself doing five years from now?
12. With your talent, you definitely have a bright future ahead of you.
13. I've been saving a nest egg for my retirement.

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe an industry that you believe is "recession-proof" and explain why.
2. What time do you usually wake up? Use the "-ish" suffix to estimate the time.
3. Talk about a plan or an idea you had that "didn't pan out."

## Mini Quiz

1. If you just relax and accept the situation without planning, what are you doing?
2. If you want someone to repeat their main point, you might ask them to do what?
3. What is the expression used when two people have the exact same idea at the same time?

### Answer Key

1. Going with the flow.
2. Reiterate it.
3. Great minds think alike.

title: "22 English Expressions You SHOULD Know - My Conversation With a Native Speaker"
description: "Learn advanced conversational expressions and phrasal verbs by listening to an interview with a native speaker in Los Angeles."
category: "english-writing"

# 22 Conversational Expressions in Context

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, you will listen to an interview with Michael, a musician and entrepreneur who moved to Los Angeles. You will learn useful, advanced expressions related to making big decisions, starting over, and getting around a city. 

## Key Points

- Expressions for starting over: *start from scratch*, *fend for myself*, and *trial and error*.
- Expressions for reactions: *freak out*, *wholeheartedly*, and *like none other*.
- Expressions for driving: *get out of your way*, *split lanes*, and *road rage*.

## How to Review

Write a short paragraph about a big decision you made recently. Use the expressions *power move*, *put my mind to it*, and *wholeheartedly*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Bold decision | Power move | An important, confident action that has a big impact. | I made a power move and moved to California. |
| Old, damaged | Beat up | Old, damaged, or in poor condition (often describing a car). | I bought an old, beat up minivan. |
| Start from nothing | Start from scratch | To start over completely from the beginning. | The business failed, so we had to start from scratch. |
| Take care of oneself | Fend for oneself | To take care of yourself without help or support. | Since my parents passed away, I've had to fend for myself. |
| Panic | Freak out | To panic, get scared, or lose emotional control. | Don't freak out, but we have a big problem. |
| Trying and failing | Trial and error | Finding a solution by trying methods and learning from mistakes. | I learned how to build websites through trial and error. |
| Almost entirely | Pretty much | Almost; nearly. | We're pretty much finished with the project. |
| Determine to do | Put one's mind to | To decide to do something and try very hard to achieve it. | You can do anything if you put your mind to it. |
| Completely | Wholeheartedly | Without hesitation; completely; with your whole heart. | I agree with you wholeheartedly. |
| Motivate / cause | Lead (led) to | To motivate or cause someone to do something. | What led you to move to Los Angeles? |
| Travel around | Get around | To travel or move from place to place. | It's hard to get around LA without a car. |
| Recreational Vehicle | RV | A large vehicle you can live or sleep in (camper/motorhome). | I sold my car and my RV. |
| Unique | Like none other | Very special; unique; incomparable. | The feeling of freedom on a motorcycle is like none other. |
| Move aside | Get out of your way | To move out of someone's path so they can pass. | In LA, drivers will actually get out of your way. |
| Driving lines | Lanes | The divisions on a highway/road for traffic. | It's legal for motorcycles to split lanes in California. |
| Hit lightly | Bump into | To hit something very lightly (like a car fender). | Someone bumped into my car in the parking lot. |
| Angry driving | Road rage | Extreme anger and aggressive behavior between drivers. | There's a lot of road rage in big cities like New York. |
| Continuously all year | All year long | Continuously throughout the entire year. | You can ride a motorcycle all year long in California. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Wholeheartedly | hole-HAR-tid-lee | Don't forget the 'ed' sound in the middle. |
| Cost (past tense) | COST | Do NOT say "costed." |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. He made a power move and asked for a promotion.
2. I drove a beat up old car in high school.
3. Our plan didn't work, so we had to start from scratch.
4. When he moved out, he finally had to fend for himself.
5. When she saw the spider, she totally freaked out.
6. I learned how to cook by trial and error.
7. The two proposals are pretty much the same.
8. She can achieve anything she puts her mind to.
9. I wholeheartedly support your decision.
10. What led you to study medicine?
11. How do you get around the city?
12. The performance tonight was like none other.
13. Please get out of my way; I'm in a hurry.
14. Make sure you use your blinker before you change lanes.
15. He bumped into my car, but there was no damage.
16. The traffic is terrible, and the drivers have terrible road rage.

## Practice Prompts

1. What is something you had to learn through "trial and error"?
2. What is the easiest way to "get around" in your city?
3. Describe a time when you or someone else completely "freaked out."

## Mini Quiz

1. If you start a brand new project from the very beginning with no resources, what are you doing?
2. If you completely and totally agree with someone without any doubt, how do you agree?
3. What is the phrase for the aggressive anger some drivers display on the highway?

### Answer Key

1. Starting from scratch.
2. Wholeheartedly.
3. Road rage.

<br/>

title: "25 Essential Business Idioms for Fluent English"

# 25 Essential Business Idioms

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

The business world is full of idioms. This lesson covers 25 highly common business idioms that native speakers use in meetings, emails, and professional conversations. Learning these will help you sound more natural and understand your colleagues better.

## Key Points

- Idioms for starting: *get the ball rolling*, *green light*, and *pull the trigger*.
- Idioms for problems: *elephant in the room*, *put out fires*, and *drop the ball*.
- Idioms for details: *bottom line*, *cross the t's and dot the i's*, and *big picture*.

## How to Review

Write an email to a fake colleague explaining a delay on a project. Use the idioms *playing catch up*, *put out fires*, and *the bottom line*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Think creatively | Think outside the box | To think in an original, creative way, not limited by rules. | We need to think outside the box to solve this. |
| Most advanced | Cutting edge | The newest, most advanced, or innovative technology/design. | They are on the cutting edge of new technology. |
| Estimate | Ballpark figure | An approximate estimate; not an exact number. | Just give me a ballpark figure of the cost. |
| Start | Get the ball rolling | To start a process or project. | Let's get the ball rolling on this new campaign. |
| Main point | The bottom line | The final result; the most important aspect or main point. | The bottom line is we don't have the budget. |
| Risk everything on one plan | Put all your eggs in one basket | To rely entirely on one option or strategy. | Apply to multiple jobs; don't put all your eggs in one basket. |
| In agreement | On the same page | To be in agreement and working towards the same goal. | I just want to make sure we're on the same page. |
| Do more than required | Go the extra mile | To put in extra effort beyond what is expected. | She always goes the extra mile for her clients. |
| Pay attention to details | Cross the t's and dot the i's | To pay careful attention to every small detail. | The lawyers are crossing the t's and dotting the i's. |
| Obvious ignored issue | Elephant in the room | An obvious problem or issue that everyone is ignoring. | We need to address the elephant in the room. |
| Exactly right | Hit the nail on the head | To identify something accurately; to be exactly right. | Your analysis really hit the nail on the head. |
| Check everything | Cover all the bases | To do everything necessary to ensure success. | We need to cover all the bases to avoid mistakes. |
| Decide as we go | Play it by ear | To make decisions as a situation develops, without a firm plan. | Let's just play it by ear tomorrow. |
| Try to keep up | Play catch up | To try to reach the same level as competitors, or meet deadlines. | We fell behind, so now we're playing catch up. |
| Handle urgent crises | Put out fires | To deal with urgent problems or crises constantly. | I spent the whole morning putting out fires. |
| Get permission | Get the green light | To get approval or permission to start something. | We finally got the green light to launch the app. |
| Undecided | Up in the air | Uncertain; not yet determined or decided. | Our vacation plans are still up in the air. |
| Get distracted | Go down the rabbit hole | To get lost in details or distractions (often online). | I went down a rabbit hole researching that topic. |
| Easy success | Pick low-hanging fruit | To pursue the easiest tasks or opportunities first. | Let's pick the low-hanging fruit to boost revenue. |
| Get fired | Get the axe | To lose your job; to be fired. | He was shocked when he got the axe yesterday. |
| Make a bad mistake | Drop the ball | To make a mistake or miss an opportunity at a crucial moment. | We really dropped the ball on that client presentation. |
| Very busy | Have a lot on my plate | To have many responsibilities, tasks, or problems. | I can't help you right now; I have a lot on my plate. |
| Take shortcuts | Cut corners | To reduce quality, time, or cost in a way that harms the result. | The builders cut corners, and now the roof leaks. |
| Overall situation | The big picture | The overall perspective or broad situation. | You need to step back and look at the big picture. |
| In the future | Going forward | From this point onward in the future. | Going forward, we will require weekly reports. |
| Make the final decision | Pull the trigger | To commit to a course of action; make a final decision. | It's time to pull the trigger on this investment. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The manager asked us to think outside the box for the new marketing campaign.
2. I don't need a specific quote, just a ballpark figure.
3. Let's schedule a meeting to get the ball rolling.
4. It's a great idea, but the bottom line is that we can't afford it.
5. Are we all on the same page regarding the new policy?
6. It's time to talk about the elephant in the room: the CEO's resignation.
7. You really hit the nail on the head with that comment.
8. We don't have a schedule yet; we'll just play it by ear.
9. After missing a week of work, I'm playing catch up.
10. I haven't done my actual job all day; I've just been putting out fires.
11. We are waiting for the boss to give us the green light.
12. The release date for the product is still up in the air.
13. Let's not go down a rabbit hole talking about minor details.
14. Target the existing customers first—they are low-hanging fruit.
15. He dropped the ball and forgot to email the client.
16. I can't take on another project; I have too much on my plate.
17. If you cut corners, the final product will be terrible.
18. You're focusing too much on details; look at the big picture.
19. Going forward, we will implement these new rules.
20. After months of debate, they finally pulled the trigger on the purchase.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever "dropped the ball" at work or school? What happened?
2. Write a sentence telling a friend that you have "a lot on your plate."
3. Think of a time when someone "went the extra mile" to help you. Describe it.

## Mini Quiz

1. If you are handling constant, urgent problems all day instead of your normal work, what are you doing?
2. If an issue is obvious but everyone is uncomfortable talking about it, what is it called?
3. If you decide not to make a strict plan and just handle the situation as it happens, you are doing what?

### Answer Key

1. Putting out fires.
2. The elephant in the room.
3. Playing it by ear.

<br/>

category: "english-writing"

# 25 "Out Of" Idioms

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this fast-paced lesson, you will learn 25 highly useful idioms that all begin with the phrase "out of." You will learn how to pronounce "out of" correctly in fast speech, and how to use these phrases to describe situations, people, and emotions.

## Key Points

- Learn pronunciation: The 'f' in "out of" sounds like a 'v' (out ov). In slang, it sounds like "outta".
- Understand common negative states: *out of order*, *out of breath*, *out of shape*.
- Master abstract phrases: *out of the blue*, *out of the loop*, *out of the picture*.

## How to Review

Group these idioms by category to help remember them. For example, group physical states (*out of shape*, *out of breath*) together, and abstract concepts (*out of the loop*, *out of my hands*) together.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Idiom | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Out of the question | Impossible; unacceptable. | Buying a new car is out of the question. |
| Out of the blue | Suddenly and unexpectedly. | He called me out of the blue after ten years. |
| Out of line | Behaving inappropriately or disrespectfully. | Your comments at the meeting were out of line. |
| Out of my league | Someone/something that is superior to you. | I can't date her; she is totally out of my league. |
| Out of nowhere | Suddenly appearing unexpectedly. | That truck came out of nowhere! |
| Out of order | Broken; not working correctly. | The elevator is out of order. |
| Out of place | Feeling uncomfortable or not fitting in. | I wore a suit to the beach and felt very out of place. |
| Out of shape | Not physically fit; unhealthy. | I need to go to the gym; I'm out of shape. |
| Out of the loop | Not informed; not having the information others have. | Nobody told me about the meeting; I'm out of the loop. |
| Out of breath | Difficulty breathing after heavy exercise. | I ran up the stairs and now I'm out of breath. |
| Out of character | Behaving differently than normal personality. | It was completely out of character for him to yell. |
| Out of the way | Inconvenient; far away. | That restaurant is a bit out of the way. |
| Out of this world | Amazing; fantastic. | Her mother's cooking is out of this world. |
| Out of touch | Not communicating anymore. | We've been out of touch since college. |
| Out of turn | Speaking when you have no right or permission to. | I'm sorry, I spoke out of turn. |
| Out of pocket | Paying with your own money (not insurance/company). | I had to pay $500 out of pocket for the repairs. |
| Out of the ordinary | Strange; unusual; different. | Did you notice anything out of the ordinary? |
| Out of your mind | Crazy; insane. | You must be out of your mind to quit that job. |
| Out of practice | Losing skill due to lack of recent practice. | I used to speak French well, but I'm out of practice. |
| Out of the picture | No longer involved in a situation or relationship. | Her ex-boyfriend is finally out of the picture. |
| Out of my budget | Too expensive. | That house is out of my budget. |
| Out of my hands | Beyond my control; I cannot change it. | I wish I could hire you, but the decision is out of my hands. |
| Out of the frying pan into the fire | Going from a bad situation to an even worse one. | Quitting now would be jumping out of the frying pan into the fire. |
| Out of control | Chaotic; unmanageable. | The wild party got completely out of control. |
| Out of [something] | Having no more left (time, gas, milk). | We need to go to the store; we're out of milk. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Correct pronunciation | Slang pronunciation |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Out of | OUT-uv (Fast 'D' sound for the T, 'V' sound for the F) | OW-duh (Outta) |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Asking for a raise right now is out of the question.
2. The promotion came completely out of the blue.
3. Don't speak to me like that; you are out of line.
4. The car broke down, and it's completely out of my hands.
5. She hasn't played piano in years, so she's really out of practice.
6. The cake you baked is absolutely out of this world!
7. Let me know what happened; I feel so out of the loop.
8. Sorry I'm late, the trains were completely out of order.
9. I felt very out of place at the fancy dinner party.
10. The teacher couldn't manage the students, and the class got out of control.

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe a time when something happened completely "out of the blue."
2. Write a sentence explaining why you can't buy an expensive watch (use "out of the question" or "out of my budget").
3. Think of a skill you used to have but don't anymore. Write a sentence using "out of practice."

## Mini Quiz

1. If you go from a bad job to a job that is much, much worse, what idiom describes that situation?
2. If you don't know the office gossip because no one told you, you are what?
3. If someone is acting crazy, you can tell them they are what?

### Answer Key

1. Out of the frying pan into the fire.
2. Out of the loop.
3. Out of your mind.

# 27 Expressions with the Word "Word"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa and her friend Drake teach you common, everyday expressions that all contain the word "word." You will also learn advanced vocabulary words that naturally arise in their conversation, such as *outlandish*, *rowdy*, and *legit*.

## Key Points

- Master phrases of trust: *give you my word*, *man of his word*, and *take it to the bank*.
- Learn negative phrases: *break your word*, *have a word with you*, and *put words in my mouth*.
- Understand advanced vocabulary: *connotation*, *outlandish*, *rowdy*, and *in awe*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph about a coworker who is very charismatic and reliable. Use the expressions *has a way with words*, *man of his word*, and *put in a good word*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| I promise | I give you my word | Promising someone that what you are saying is true. | I'll pay you back tomorrow, I give you my word. |
| Reliable man | Man of his word | A person who keeps their promises and can be trusted. | You can trust him; he is a man of his word. |
| Break a promise | Break your word | Failing to do what you promised to do. | You promised you'd help me; don't break your word. |
| Assume wrongly | Put words in my mouth | Claiming someone said something they didn't actually say. | I didn't say I hated the movie; don't put words in my mouth. |
| Negative meaning | Negative connotation | A negative feeling or idea associated with a word/phrase. | That phrase has a very negative connotation. |
| Strange / bizarre | Outlandish | Looking or sounding very strange, bizarre, or unusual. | The politician made some outlandish claims. |
| Final argument | Have the last word | Having to say the final thing in an argument to feel you won. | My sister is stubborn and always has to have the last word. |
| Speak privately | Have a word with you | Speaking in private, usually about something serious/negative. | My boss said, "Can I have a word with you in my office?" |
| Recommend | Put in a good word | Recommending someone positively to a person in power. | I'll put in a good word for you with the manager. |
| Retail store | Retail | The sale of goods to the public (like a clothing store). | I worked in clothing retail for three years. |
| Curse word | Four-letter word | A bad word, curse word, naughty word, or dirty word. | He got in trouble for using a four-letter word. |
| Believe me | Take my word for it | Trusting that what someone is saying is true. | Take my word for it, that restaurant is terrible. |
| Guaranteed | Take it to the bank | Meaning a statement is guaranteed, absolutely true, or legitimate. | He will win the election, you can take it to the bank. |
| Real / truthful | Legit | Short for legitimate; real, authentic, or legal. | Don't worry, the offer is completely legit. |
| Believe them | Take someone at their word | To believe someone based only on what they say. | I take him at his word that he didn't steal it. |
| Verbal recommendation | Word of mouth | Hearing about something from people talking, not advertising. | The restaurant became popular solely by word of mouth. |
| Haven't heard anything | I haven't heard a word | Having zero information, or hearing total silence. | Do you know what happened? I haven't heard a word. |
| Noisy / unruly | Rowdy | Noisy, disorderly, and difficult to control. | The kids were getting very rowdy. |
| Speechless | I have no words | Being so shocked or amazed that you cannot speak. | That sunset is so beautiful, I have no words. |
| Amazed | In awe | Having a strong feeling of amazement or respect. | I was in awe of his musical talent. |
| Charismatic speaker | Have a way with words | Being a smooth, charismatic speaker who says the right things. | He has a way with words and is very persuasive. |
| Charming | Smooth | Charming and having excellent social skills. | He's very smooth when talking to clients. |
| Pay attention | Mark my words | Used to tell someone to remember a prediction or warning. | Mark my words, that company will be bankrupt in a year. |

## Alternative Expressions

When Drake explained the meaning of **"break your word"**, he used three excellent alternatives that you can use interchangeably:
1. You didn't hold true to your promise.
2. You didn't follow through with it.
3. You didn't deliver on your word.

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Word | WERD | Eliminating the 'o' entirely. Do not say "w-OH-rd" with rounded lips. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I give you my word that I won't tell anyone your secret.
2. He said he would help us move, and he is a man of his word.
3. Please don't put words in my mouth; I never said I wanted to quit.
4. She made some very outlandish demands during the meeting.
5. In every argument, my husband always has to have the last word.
6. The principal would like to have a word with you.
7. I'll put in a good word for you regarding the open job position.
8. You can't use four-letter words in the classroom!
9. Take my word for it; you don't want to live in that neighborhood.
10. The plan will work, you can take it to the bank.
11. We don't spend money on advertising; all our business is word of mouth.
12. I haven't heard a word from her since she moved to Europe.
13. Mark my words, this will be the best decision you've ever made.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever known someone who always needed to "have the last word"? Describe them.
2. What is a business or product you discovered strictly through "word of mouth"?
3. Think of a time you were completely "in awe" of something. What was it?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you recommend your friend for a job to your boss, what did you do for your friend?
2. If your friend wears a massive, crazy, feathery purple hat to work, what word describes her outfit?
3. If someone incorrectly assumes what you said, you tell them not to put what in your mouth?

### Answer Key

1. Put in a good word.
2. Outlandish.
3. Words.

# 30 Idioms with a Native Speaker

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, you will learn practical expressions and idioms from an interview with Craig, an actor and former football player. He discusses the challenges of his career, how he deals with rejection, and the differences between acting and modeling.

## Key Points

- Career and work idioms: *nine-to-five job*, *downtime*, and *pros and cons*.
- Expressions of disappointment: *let down*, *cut down at the knees*, and *throw under the bus*.
- Lifestyle phrases: *day-to-day life*, *live it up*, and *lay low*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph about the "pros and cons" of your current "nine-to-five job" (or daily routine). Include a time when you experienced "downtime."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Most importantly | First and foremost | Most importantly; primarily; more than anything else. | First and foremost, it's definitely a tough job. |
| Regular job | Nine-to-five job | A typical, stable, full-time office job. | I didn't want a typical nine-to-five job. |
| Please explain | How so? | Asking someone to explain what they just said. | "It made me who I am." "How so?" |
| Have an advantage | Have an edge | To have an advantage over the competition. | His background in football gives him an edge. |
| Certainly | No doubt about it | Absolutely; certainly true. (The 'b' in doubt is silent). | He is the best player, no doubt about it. |
| Disappointed | Let down | Disappointed; or (as a noun) a big disappointment. | I used to leave auditions feeling very let down. |
| Devastated | Cut down at the knees | To be severely disappointed or abruptly stopped. | I was hopeful, only to be cut down at the knees. |
| Make an effort | Put in (work) | To make an effort or spend time doing something. | I'm proud of the hard work that I put in. |
| Get the role | Get the part | To be hired for an acting role in a play/film. | If I get a phone call saying I got the part, I'm happy. |
| Similarity | Crossover | Having similar elements or a shared connection. | There is definitely some crossover between acting and modeling. |
| Have the exact traits | Fit the mold | To have the exact characteristics that are desired. | I do modeling because I fit the mold they need. |
| Betray someone | Throw under the bus | To betray or sacrifice someone to save yourself. | I can't believe my coworker threw me under the bus. |
| Best word I have | For lack of a better term | The best word you can think of at the moment. | Just try to look cool, for lack of a better term. |
| Give an impression | Come across | To give a specific impression to others. | He comes across as a very friendly guy. |
| Have the chance to | Get to | To have the opportunity or privilege to do something. | I get to do things on camera I wouldn't normally do. |
| Daily routine | Day-to-day life | Your ordinary, everyday routine. | I wouldn't do that in my day-to-day life. |
| Party hard | Live it up | To party, spend money, and enjoy life extravagantly. | When he goes to Vegas, he likes to live it up. |
| Stay quiet/calm | Lay low | To stay quiet, avoid attention, and not party. | Tonight, I just want to stay home and lay low. |
| Bad habit | Vice | A destructive habit (smoking, gambling, drinking). | I'm not a guy who needs drinks or any other vice. |
| Negative consequences | Repercussions | Negative consequences or effects of an action. | You can act crazy without there being any repercussions. |
| Wait constantly | Hurry-up-and-wait | Being rushed to do something, only to end up waiting. | Acting is a lot of hurry-up-and-wait. |
| Undecided | Up in the air | Not yet decided; uncertain. | The schedule is always up in the air. |
| Follow the plan | Stick to it | To do what was planned without changing. | Even if they have a schedule, it's hard to stick to it. |
| Inactive time | Downtime | Free time; a period of inactivity. | There is a lot of downtime on a movie set. |
| Normal reality | Part of the game | An accepted, unavoidable reality of a situation. | Rejection is hard, but it's part of the game. |
| Positives and negatives | Pros and cons | The advantages and disadvantages of something. | What are the pros and cons of moving to LA? |
| Be more important | Outweigh | To be more important or significant than something else. | For me, the pros definitely outweigh the cons. |
| Continue doing | Stick with it | To continue doing something instead of quitting. | I love acting, so I'm going to stick with it. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Doubt | DOUT | The 'b' is completely silent. Do not say "dow-bt". |
| Pros and cons | PROZ and CAHNS | "Pros" uses a round 'O', but "cons" uses a wide 'ah' sound. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. First and foremost, I'd like to thank my family.
2. She was tired of her boring nine-to-five job.
3. His height gives him an edge in basketball.
4. It was a huge letdown when the concert was canceled.
5. I'm proud of the hours I put into this project.
6. The manager threw me under the bus to save his own job.
7. He comes across as very confident during interviews.
8. I don't like to live it up; I'd rather just lay low on the weekends.
9. If you break the rules, there will be serious repercussions.
10. The plan for next week is still up in the air.
11. Let's make a decision and stick to it.
12. I use my downtime at work to study English.
13. The pros of this car definitely outweigh the cons.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever been "thrown under the bus" by a colleague or friend? What happened?
2. Write a sentence describing your "day-to-day life."
3. Think of a hobby you enjoy. What is one of the "pros" and one of the "cons"?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you are betrayed by a coworker to save themselves, what did they do to you?
2. If the advantages of a decision are stronger than the disadvantages, the pros do what to the cons?
3. If you want to stay home, relax, and avoid attention this weekend, you are doing what?

### Answer Key

1. Threw you under the bus.
2. Outweigh the cons.
3. Laying low.

title: "3 English Grammar Rules Even Native Speakers Get Wrong"
description: "Understand common grammar mistakes native speakers make regarding 'lay vs. lie', 'less vs. fewer', and 'there is vs. there are'."
category: "english-writing"
theme: "grammar"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"
visibility: "public"
language: "en"
tags: ["english-writing", "grammar", "youtube", "review"]
---

# 3 Grammar Mistakes Native Speakers Make

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Should you correct native speakers when they make grammar mistakes? Probably not. In this lesson, Lisa explains three grammar rules that native English speakers frequently get wrong in spoken English. However, she warns that for professional writing, you must still follow the strict grammar rules!

## Key Points

- **Lay vs. Lie:** "Lie" is correct for resting, but native speakers almost always say "lay down."
- **Less vs. Fewer:** "Fewer" is for countable nouns, but native speakers often use "less" for everything.
- **There is vs. There are:** Native speakers frequently use "there's" for plural nouns because it's easier to say quickly.

## How to Review

Write three sentences using the grammatically *correct* forms of these rules. Then write the *casual, incorrect* versions that a native speaker might say in a relaxed setting.

## Grammar Rules And Exceptions

### 1. Lay vs. Lie

**The Rule:** 
- *Lay* is a transitive verb. It requires an object. (Example: I lay the book on the table).
- *Lie* is an intransitive verb. It does not take an object. (Example: I lie down on the sofa).

**The Native Speaker Mistake:** 
- Native speakers almost always use "lay" when they should use "lie."
- **Incorrect (but common):** "I want to lay down."
- **Why it happens:** The past tense of "lie" is "lay" (lie, lay, lain), which is incredibly confusing even for native speakers. The strict grammar rule is relaxing in spoken English.

### 2. Less vs. Fewer

**The Rule:**
- *Less* is used for non-countable nouns (less money, less water, less time).
- *Fewer* is used for countable nouns (fewer people, fewer cars, fewer friends).

**The Native Speaker Mistake:**
- Native speakers frequently use "less" for countable nouns. 
- **Incorrect (but common):** "There were less people at the party." / "10 items or less."
- **Why it happens:** People simply prefer the sound of "less." The "grammar police" will correct this, but the average person does not care.

### 3. There is vs. There are

**The Rule:**
- *There is* is for singular nouns (There is a car).
- *There are* is for plural nouns (There are five cars).

**The Native Speaker Mistake:**
- In fast spoken English, native speakers use "There's" for plural nouns.
- **Incorrect (but common):** "There's a lot of people here."
- **Why it happens:** Saying "There are" requires pronouncing two 'R' sounds in a row, which is difficult. Saying "There's" is faster and easier for the mouth. However, in *written* English, native speakers will properly write "There are."

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Professional Environment:** Do not use these native-speaker mistakes in formal writing or job interviews. Companies want to hire people who write properly.
- **The Grammar Police:** "The grammar police" are people who aggressively correct other people's grammar mistakes. Do not be the grammar police; people find it very annoying.

## Good Sentences To Imitate (Correct Grammar)

1. I am tired; I want to lie down on the bed.
2. I will lay the blanket on the floor.
3. There are fewer cars on the road today.
4. I have less time than I did yesterday.
5. There are a lot of people waiting in line.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence correctly using "fewer."
2. Write a sentence correctly using "lay" (transitive).
3. Why do native speakers often incorrectly say "there's a lot of people" instead of "there are a lot of people"?

## Mini Quiz

1. Which verb requires a direct object: lay or lie?
2. Which word should you strictly use for countable nouns: less or fewer?
3. If you constantly correct your friends' English mistakes, you are acting like the what?

### Answer Key

1. Lay.
2. Fewer.
3. Grammar police.

<br/>

# 4 Accent Tips for Professionals

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa provides four essential tips for non-native professionals to improve their spoken English. The goal is not to eliminate your accent completely, but to ensure you are clearly understood the first time you speak, especially when using important career-related vocabulary.

## Key Points

- Master your career vocabulary: Ensure you pronounce the words you use every day perfectly.
- Consonant clusters: Pronounce all consonants clearly (e.g., the 'ct' in *expectations*).
- Syllable stress: English relies heavily on stressed syllables for comprehension (e.g., *career* vs. *carrier*).
- International words: Do not assume words that look similar in your native language sound the same in English.

## How to Review

Make a list of 5-10 technical words you use every day at work. Look them up in an online dictionary or on YouGlish, and practice pronouncing them out loud, focusing on syllable stress.

## Four Rules For Clear Professional Speech

### 1. Master Words Used Regularly at Work
If you use the same 20 words every day at your job, you must make sure they are pronounced flawlessly. Mispronouncing common words leads to confusion and a loss of professional confidence. 
- *Example:* "Real estate agent" should link the L and the E: *real-estate*.

### 2. Pay Attention to Consonant Clusters
English has many words with multiple consonants back-to-back (clusters). Do not skip consonants or add extra vowel sounds between them.
- *Example:* "Expectations" has a strict 'ct' sound. Do not drop the hard 'c' (k sound).
- *Example:* "Doctor" and "Architect" both have clear 'ct' sounds in English.

### 3. Pay Attention to Syllable Stress
In English, stressing the wrong syllable can make a word completely incomprehensible to a native listener. 
- *Accuracy:* Stress the first syllable (AC-cu-ra-cy).
- *Develop:* Stress the second syllable (de-VEL-op).
- *Career vs. Carrier:* *Career* stresses the second syllable (ca-REER). *Carrier* stresses the first syllable (CAR-ri-er).
- *Eligible vs. Illegible:* *Eligible* stresses the first (EL-i-gi-ble). *Illegible* stresses the second (il-LEG-i-ble).
- *Record (Noun vs. Verb):* Noun stresses the first (REC-ord). Verb stresses the second (re-CORD).

### 4. Be Careful With International Words
Many academic, medical, and scientific words are spelled similarly across multiple languages. Do not assume the pronunciation is the same as your native language.
- *Example:* The brand "Toyota" or "Porsche" is pronounced differently in the US than in Japan or Germany.
- *Example:* The "-ism" suffix (tourism, capitalism). In English, an extra vowel sound is inserted between the 'z' and 'm' sound (*tour-iz-um*).

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Syllable stress rule |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Accuracy | AC-cu-ra-cy | First syllable stress |
| Develop | de-VEL-op | Second syllable stress |
| Career | ca-REER | Second syllable stress |
| Eligible | EL-i-gi-ble | First syllable stress |
| Illegible | il-LEG-i-ble | Second syllable stress |
| Record (Noun) | REC-ord | First syllable stress |
| Record (Verb) | re-CORD | Second syllable stress |
| Tourism | TOUR-iz-um | Don't forget the hidden vowel sound before the 'm' |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The doctor gave orders for you to be discharged.
2. I am a tourism ambassador for the city.
3. Please be direct about your exact expectations.
4. The student was expelled for plagiarism.
5. Don't reject the practical advice from your doctor.
6. My goal is to develop new treatments for pancreatic cancer.
7. I love my career in real estate.
8. His handwriting on the medical record was illegible.

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying: "Please be direct about your exact expectations." Did you pronounce the 'c' before the 't'?
2. Write a sentence using the noun "record" and another using the verb "record".
3. Write down 5 words you use every day in your profession. Go to YouGlish and listen to native speakers pronounce them.

## Mini Quiz

1. If you stress the first syllable of the word "career," what word does it sound like?
2. In English, what hidden sound exists between the 's' and 'm' in words ending in "-ism"?
3. True or False: You must eliminate your accent completely to be a successful professional in the US.

### Answer Key

1. Carrier.
2. A vowel sound (an "uh" sound).
3. False. Clear speech and comprehension is the goal, not perfection.

<br/>

# 4 Rules for "OF" vs "OFF"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Many non-native English speakers mispronounce the simple prepositions "of" and "off." This quick lesson clarifies the exact phonetic differences between the two words, ensuring you don't confuse native listeners.

## Key Points

- **OF:** The 'f' is pronounced as a soft 'V' sound (uv).
- **OFF:** The 'f' is a strong, hard 'F' sound, and the vowel is an open 'aw' sound (awf).

## How to Review

Read aloud phrases like "A cup of coffee" and "Turn off the light" to physically feel the difference in your teeth and lips. 

## Vocabulary And Expressions

### Rule 1: The word "OF" uses a 'V' sound.
In the entire English language, the word "of" is the *only* time the letter 'F' is pronounced with a 'V' sound. 
- It is pronounced like "UV" or "AHV".
- Your vocal cords must vibrate. 
- *Example:* A piece of cake. A cup of coffee.

### Rule 2: The word "OFF" uses a strong 'F' sound.
The word "off" uses a traditional, voiceless 'F' sound. The vowel sound is a wider "AW" sound.
- It is pronounced like "AWF".
- Your vocal cords do not vibrate on the 'F'.
- *Example:* Turn off the light. Take off your shoes.

### Rule 3: Reducing "OF" in fast speech.
When native speakers speak quickly, the word "of" often reduces to just a schwa sound ("uh").
- "A cup of coffee" sounds like "A cuppa coffee."
- "A lot of people" sounds like "A lotta people."

### Rule 4: "OFF" is never reduced.
Unlike "of", the word "off" is an important stress word (often part of a phrasal verb). It is never reduced to an "uh" sound. It must always be pronounced fully as "AWF".
- "Turn it off" is always fully pronounced.

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Of | UV / AHV / UH | Pronouncing it with a hard 'F' sound. |
| Off | AWF | Pronouncing the vowel too short. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Can I get a cup of coffee?
2. I have a lot of work to do.
3. Please turn off the television.
4. We need to get off the bus at the next stop.
5. He took a piece of the pie right off the table.

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying: "Take your shoes off and have a cup of tea."
2. Write down three phrasal verbs that use the word "off" (e.g., turn off).
3. Explain the physical difference in your mouth when making a 'V' sound versus an 'F' sound.

## Mini Quiz

1. In which word does your voice box vibrate during the final consonant: "of" or "off"?
2. Which word can be reduced to just an "uh" sound in fast speech?
3. What is the only word in English where the letter 'F' is pronounced as a 'V'?

### Answer Key

1. Of.
2. Of.
3. Of.

<br/>

# 4 Tips for Clear Communication

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa coaches Jacobo, a Spanish author and screenwriter. She provides four essential tips for non-native speakers to speak clearly. She emphasizes that clear communication and proper pronunciation of core vocabulary is far more important than completely hiding your foreign accent.

## Key Points

- Master your specific vocabulary. 
- Beware of words that look similar in your native language; the pronunciation is likely different in English.
- Practice words in full thought-groups, not just individually.
- Do not obsess over your accent. Confidence and clarity are the true goals.

## How to Review

Pick five English words that are spelled exactly the same in your native language. Look up their English pronunciation on YouGlish, and practice the specific vowel and stress differences.

## Four Tips For Clear Speech

### 1. Master Words Related to Your Career/Passion
Make a list of the 30-50 words you use constantly in your field. Ensure your pronunciation of these specific words is flawless. If you mispronounce core words, people will struggle to understand your overall point. 
- Use tools like YouGlish to listen to native speakers pronounce these specific words in context.
- *Example:* For Jacobo (an author of a historical novel about Rome), he had to master the pronunciation of *Romance*, *Romans*, and *novel*.

### 2. Be Careful With Similar-Looking Words
Words that are spelled similarly in English and your native language are dangerous. You might subconsciously use your native pronunciation. 
- Pay special attention to the **TU** and **TI** sounds. In English, they often make a "CH" or "SH" sound, whereas in Latin languages, they make a hard "T" sound.
- *Spanish vs English (Multi):* Spanish uses an "OO" sound. English uses an "UH" sound (*MULL-tie*).
- *Spanish vs English (Culture):* Spanish uses a hard T. English uses a "CH" sound (*CUL-chur*).
- *Spanish vs English (Situation):* Spanish uses a hard T. English uses a "CH" sound (*sit-yoo-A-shun*).
- *Spanish vs English (Virtual):* English uses a "CH" sound (*VIR-choo-ul*).

### 3. Practice in Thought Groups and Sentences
Do not just practice a word in isolation. Once you learn the correct pronunciation, immediately practice it in a full sentence. Link the words together naturally. 
- *Instead of:* "I wrote... a... novel."
- *Link them:* "I wrote a pilot based on a novel." (Link the words: *wrote-a*, *based-on-a*).

### 4. Don't Obsess About Your Accent
Unless you are an actor trying to play an American character, you do not need a perfect American accent. Obsessing over every vowel sound will make you nervous, and your speech will become stiff and unnatural. 
- Remember: **"It's no good unless you're understood."**
- Professional speech is clear speech. Your confidence should come from your knowledge of your subject, not the perfection of your accent. 

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct English pronunciation | Common mistake for Latin-language speakers |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Romans | RO-minz | Stressing the second syllable |
| Romance | ro-MANCE | Stressing the first syllable |
| Novel | NAH-vul | Pronouncing the 'o' like "no", or the 'v' like a 'b' |
| Multi | MULL-tie | Pronouncing the 'u' like "moo" |
| Culture | CUL-chur | Pronouncing a hard 'T' instead of 'CH' |
| Situation | sit-yoo-A-shun | Pronouncing a hard 'T' instead of 'CH' |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. My novel is about Romans.
2. I wrote a pilot based on a novel.
3. I am a multi-award-winning screenwriter.
4. I love discovering new cultures.
5. We have a difficult situation at work.
6. The meetings will be completely virtual.
7. There are many statues in that ancient city.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence linking two words together naturally, like "based on a."
2. Say the word "Situation" out loud. Did you use a "CH" sound or a hard "T" sound?
3. Why is it dangerous to assume that a word spelled the same in your language sounds the same in English?

## Mini Quiz

1. In English, the "TU" in words like "virtual" and "culture" makes what sound?
2. What happens to your speech if you obsess too much over having a perfect American accent?
3. True or False: You should practice new words in isolation before trying them in sentences.

### Answer Key

1. A "CH" sound.
2. You become nervous and your speech becomes stiff/unnatural.
3. False. You should practice them in full sentences and thought groups to learn the natural linking.

<br/>

# 4 Tips for Understanding Fast Speech

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Do you struggle to understand native speakers when they talk fast? It often feels like they are swallowing words. In this lesson, Lisa explains the concept of "linking"—connecting multiple words together so they sound like one long word. You will learn four rules for identifying these patterns to improve your listening comprehension.

## Key Points

- Master linking final consonants to initial vowels (e.g., *first of all*).
- Understand how identical consonant sounds merge (e.g., *good dog* becomes *goo-dog*).
- Learn how pronouns are reduced (e.g., *his*, *he*, and *her* often lose the 'H' sound).
- Use captions strategically to train your ear to catch these missing sounds.

## How to Review

Watch a short YouTube video without captions. When you fail to understand a phrase, rewind it, turn the captions on, and see how the native speaker linked the words together.

## Four Rules For Understanding Fast Speech

### 1. Listen for the Key Words in Thought Groups
When native speakers talk quickly, they reduce minor words (prepositions, pronouns, articles) and emphasize only the important words (nouns, verbs, adjectives). 
- *Example:* "If you **told** me about the **issue**, I would have **helped** you **fix** it."
- If you only hear "told", "issue", "helped", and "fix", you can still understand the sentence perfectly. Do not stress over the small, reduced words.

### 2. Link Final Consonants to Initial Vowels
When a word ends in a consonant and the next word begins with a vowel, native speakers drag the consonant onto the next word.
- *First of all* becomes *Firs-tov-all*. (Note: "of" sounds like "uv").
- *Get up late* sounds identical to *Get a plate*.
- *Picked out* becomes *Pick-tout*. (The 'ed' sounds like a 't').
- *Worked out* becomes *Work-tout*.

### 3. Merge Identical Consonant Sounds
When one word ends with a consonant and the next word begins with the *exact same* consonant sound, native speakers do not pause to say it twice. They pronounce it once, but hold the sound slightly longer.
- *Good dog* becomes *Goo-dog*. (Do not say the 'd' twice).
- *She's sad* becomes *She-sad*.
- *Let's stop* becomes *Let-stop*.
- *Black cat* becomes *Bla-cat*.

### 4. Watch for Dropped Consonants (Reduced Pronouns)
In fast, connected speech, native speakers often completely drop the 'H' sound in pronouns like *he*, *his*, and *her*. 
- *It's his house* becomes *It's-is-house*.
- *What did he say?* becomes *What-diddy-say?* (or even *Wuddy-say?*).
- *I love her* becomes *I-love-er*.
- *Did you talk to her?* becomes *Did-you-talk-to-er?*

## Usage Notes And Common Mistakes

- **Listening practice:** Listen to podcasts at a slightly slower speed (0.75x) to train your ear to hear the reduced sounds and merged consonants. Once you recognize the pattern, speed it back up to 1.0x.
- **Phrasal verb stress:** When using phrasal verbs, the second word (the preposition) is usually stressed more than the verb. (e.g., I *worked OUT* yesterday).

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. First of all, we need to get a plate.
2. I would read the books that I had just picked out.
3. You should have told me; we could have figured it out.
4. You should have warned me; I would have turned it off.
5. It's a special occasion. (Link the 's': *It-special*).
6. What did he find out? (*Wuddy find out?*)

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying "I picked out a new dress" using the consonant-to-vowel linking rule.
2. What happens to the 'H' sound in the word "her" when a native speaker says "I love her" quickly?
3. Write a sentence containing two identical consonant sounds back-to-back (like "good dog").

## Mini Quiz

1. If someone speaks quickly, "get up late" sounds exactly like what other phrase?
2. In the sentence "You should have told me", which word carries the most stress?
3. True or False: You should pause between every single word to ensure you are understood.

### Answer Key

1. Get a plate.
2. Told.
3. False. Native speakers connect words in "thought groups."

<br/>

# 5 Commonly Mispronounced Words

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Advanced English students often make pronunciation mistakes on very common words due to the confusing and irregular rules of English spelling. This lesson highlights five specific words where the spelling tricks non-native speakers into pronouncing the wrong vowel sounds.

## Key Points

- Do not pronounce the 'A' in *says* or *said*. They rhyme with *bed*.
- The word *foreign* sounds like the number four plus the letter N (for-N).
- The 'AI' spelling in *mountain* and *plaid* is highly irregular.

## How to Review

Read the five target words aloud in front of a mirror or record yourself on your phone. Ensure you are completely dropping the "A" sound in *says* and *said*.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

### 1. Says
- **Common Mistake:** Pronouncing the "ay" sound, like "say-z".
- **Correct Pronunciation:** "SEZ". It rhymes with *fez* or *pez*. The "A" sound completely disappears.
- *Example:* She says she is tired. (She *sez*).

### 2. Said
- **Common Mistake:** Pronouncing the "ai" sound, like "say-d".
- **Correct Pronunciation:** "SED". It rhymes with *bed* and *red*.
- *Example:* Yesterday, he said hello. (He *sed*).

### 3. Foreign / Foreigner
- **Common Mistake:** Pronouncing the "eign" like "rain" (for-rain).
- **Correct Pronunciation:** "FOR-in". Think of the number 4 (four) and the letter N (en).
- *Example:* I want to learn a foreign language. 

### 4. Mountain
- **Common Mistake:** Pronouncing the "ai" sound in the second syllable (moun-tayn).
- **Correct Pronunciation:** "MOUN-tin" or "MOUN-tn". The second syllable is heavily reduced, and the "T" is often held or swallowed by native speakers. The "ai" sound is completely lost.
- *Example:* He climbed the mountain.

### 5. Plaid
- **Common Mistake:** Pronouncing the "ai" sound, like "played".
- **Correct Pronunciation:** "PLAD". It rhymes with *sad* and *mad*.
- *Definition:* A clothing pattern consisting of crisscrossed horizontal and vertical bands in multiple colors (often seen on flannel shirts).
- *Example:* He is wearing a plaid shirt. 

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Listen to what your teacher says. (Sez)
2. He said that he would climb the mountain. (Sed / Moun-tin)
3. I studied two foreign languages in high school. (For-in)
4. A lot of foreigners visit my city. (For-in-erz)
5. I'm shopping for a new plaid shirt. (Plad)

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying: "She says she bought a plaid shirt in a foreign country."
2. Explain the difference in vowel sounds between the present tense verb "say" and the third-person verb "says".
3. Write a sentence describing an outfit that contains the word "plaid".

## Mini Quiz

1. Which color rhymes with the past tense verb "said"?
2. What number helps you remember how to pronounce the first syllable of the word "foreign"?
3. True or False: The word "plaid" rhymes with the word "played."

### Answer Key

1. Red.
2. Four (4).
3. False. It rhymes with "sad."

<br/>
