# 6 Tips for Job Interviews with an Accent

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Many highly qualified professionals fail to apply for their dream jobs because they are terrified that their strong foreign accent will hold them back during the interview. In this lesson, Lisa provides six practical tips to help you prepare, build confidence, and ensure you are clearly understood by your interviewers.

## Key Points

- Master the exact pronunciation of your interviewers' names.
- Perfectly pronounce the technical vocabulary of your industry and job title.
- Practice answering questions out loud to hear your own pacing.
- Confidently acknowledge your accent and your dedication to improving it.

## How to Review

Write down your exact job title and three technical words related to your industry. Look them up online and practice pronouncing them until they are absolutely perfect.

## Six Interview Preparation Tips

### 1. Master the Interviewers' Names
Find out who will be interviewing you in advance. English names can be tricky (e.g., *Joan* vs. *Joanne*, *Craig* vs. *Greg*). Practice pronouncing their names perfectly. Walking into the room and saying their name correctly immediately builds confidence.

### 2. Master Your Industry Vocabulary
Make a list of 20 to 40 words specific to your field, including your exact job title. If you mispronounce your own job title, the interviewers will struggle to understand you. 
- *Example:* A doctor must pronounce "pain specialist" clearly, not "pen specialist."
- *Example:* A manager must pronounce the "J" in "general manager," not "heneral manager."
- *Action:* Have a native speaker or a coach record these words for you so you can listen and repeat.

### 3. Practice Answering Out Loud
Write down all the possible questions you might be asked. Do not just think about the answers in your head—you must practice saying them **out loud**. Record yourself. If you stumble or sound unclear, repeat the answer until it is smooth.

### 4. Pay Attention to Your Speed
When we get nervous, we naturally speed up. If you speak too quickly with a strong accent, the interviewers will not understand you. Force yourself to slow down, especially when delivering key words and important information.

### 5. Be Confident and Positive
Behave as if you are the absolute right person for the job. Do not let insecurity about your accent ruin your posture or your energy. If you believe in your qualifications, the interviewers will believe in them too.

### 6. Mention Your Dedication to Improvement
If you feel your accent is an issue, confidently bring it up. Tell them that you are actively working on your English and pronunciation. This shows the interviewers that you are ambitious, driven, and committed to self-improvement. It makes a fantastic impression.

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. "I am applying for the position of General Manager."
2. "I am very passionate about this industry and have ten years of experience."
3. "I am continually working to improve my English and professional communication skills."

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself answering the common interview question: "Why do you want to work for this company?" Pay attention to your speed.
2. What are two English names that you find difficult to pronounce?
3. Write a short script of how you would confidently tell an interviewer that you are working on your English pronunciation.

## Mini Quiz

1. When people get nervous in an interview, what do they usually do with their speaking speed?
2. Why is it important to practice your answers out loud instead of just in your head?
3. True or False: You should hide the fact that you are taking English lessons from your interviewer.

### Answer Key

1. They speak too fast.
2. To hear yourself, check your pacing, and ensure your pronunciation is clear.
3. False. Telling them shows ambition and dedication to self-improvement.

# 7 Advanced Grammar Mistakes

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Even advanced English learners who score perfectly on written tests often make grammatical errors when speaking naturally. In this lesson, Lisa highlights seven common mistakes involving the verb "suggest," compound adjectives, non-countable nouns, and conditionals. 

## Key Points

- Master the verb *suggest*: It does not take a direct object pronoun (e.g., "suggest him").
- Understand compound adjectives: Hyphenated adjectives are singular (e.g., *two-bedroom apartment*).
- Never pluralize non-countable nouns: *Advice*, *homework*, and *information* never take an 'S'.
- Learn conditional rules: Do not use *will* or *would* twice in an "if/when" sentence.

## How to Review

Review the "Mini Quiz" section and try to correct the mistakes in your head before checking the answer key. Repeat this exercise until the correct grammar sounds more natural to you than the incorrect grammar.

## Grammar Rules And Corrections

### 1. Using "Suggest" with Pronouns
- **Incorrect:** I suggested him to buy the other one.
- **Rule:** You cannot use a direct object pronoun immediately after "suggest."
- **Correct:** I suggested *that he buy* the other one. (Or: I suggested *buying* the other one).

### 2. Using "Suggest" with Infinitives
- **Incorrect:** I suggest you to do it.
- **Rule:** Do not use "to" after suggest. "Suggest" requires the subjunctive form (the base infinitive form without 'to' or 's').
- **Correct:** I suggest *that you do it*. / I suggest *that he go* there. (Not "goes").

### 3. Using "Suggest" with Negatives
- **Incorrect:** I suggest you don't be late anymore.
- **Rule:** For negative suggestions, use "not" instead of "don't."
- **Correct:** I suggest you *not be* late anymore. (Or: I suggest *not being* late).

### 4. Compound Adjectives with Numbers
- **Incorrect:** I'm looking for a two-bedrooms apartment. / I have a six years old son.
- **Rule:** When a number and a noun act as an adjective before another noun, the describing noun must be singular and hyphenated.
- **Correct:** I have a *two-bedroom* apartment. / I have a *six-year-old* son. / It is a *million-dollar* house.

### 5. Non-Countable Nouns
- **Incorrect:** I would appreciate getting some advices from you.
- **Rule:** Non-countable nouns (advice, homework, information, vocabulary) never take an 'S'.
- **Correct:** I would appreciate getting some *advice* from you.

### 6. "Looking Forward To"
- **Incorrect:** I'm really looking forward to go there.
- **Rule:** The phrase "looking forward to" must be followed by a gerund (an -ing verb).
- **Correct:** I'm really looking forward to *going* there. / I'm looking forward to *meeting* you.

### 7. "Used To" vs. "Am Used To"
- **Incorrect:** In my country, we are used to eat rice for breakfast.
- **Rule:** 
  - Past habit: *I used to* + base verb (I used to eat rice).
  - Current habit: *I am used to* + gerund verb (I am used to eating rice).
- **Correct:** In my country, we *are used to eating* rice. (It is our current habit).

### 8. Conditionals (Will and Would)
- **Incorrect:** I will give it to you when we will see each other. / If you'll go, I'll go too. / If you would have tried it, you would have liked it.
- **Rule:** Do not use "will" or "would" twice in conditional sentences. The "if/when" clause takes the present or past perfect tense.
- **Correct:** I will give it to you *when we see* each other. / *If you go*, I will go too. / *If you had tried it*, you would have liked it.

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I suggested that he fix it soon.
2. I suggest not driving so fast.
3. My car has four doors; it's a four-door car.
4. I have a seven-year-old and a nine-year-old.
5. Thank you for your advice.
6. I am looking forward to hearing from you.
7. If I had studied, I would have passed the test.
8. She would have come to your party if you'd invited her.

## Practice Prompts

1. Correct this sentence: "I suggest you to take the job."
2. Write a sentence describing the age of a family member using a compound adjective (e.g., 50-year-old).
3. Correct this sentence: "I am used to wake up early."

## Mini Quiz (Find the Mistake)

1. I need to finish my homeworks before I can go out.
2. If you will help me, I will pay you.
3. I am looking forward to see you tomorrow.

### Answer Key

1. Homeworks -> homework. (Non-countable noun).
2. If you will help me -> If you help me. (Don't use will in the if-clause).
3. to see -> to seeing. (Must use an -ing verb).

<br/>

# 8 Business Phrasal Verbs & Linking

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Speaking quickly does not make you sound fluent; linking words smoothly does. In this lesson, you will learn a critical rule for sounding like a native American speaker: linking final consonants to initial vowels. You will then practice this rule using 8 highly common phrasal verbs used in business.

## Key Points

- **The Linking Rule:** When a word ends with a consonant (especially T or D) and the next word starts with a vowel, connect them.
- When you link words, it forces you to think about word stress, giving your speech a natural rhythm.
- In phrasal verbs, the second word (the preposition) carries the stress.

## How to Review

Read the eight phrasal verbs below out loud. Exaggerate the connection between the final consonant of the verb and the "O" or "U" of the preposition. (e.g., *pai-doff*, *burn-dout*).

## Pronunciation and Linking Rules

### Why Linking is Important
1. It makes you sound advanced and fluent.
2. It forces you to use natural rhythm and melody by stressing the key words.
3. It actually makes difficult consonants easier to say because moving a final consonant to the beginning of the next word is physically easier for your mouth.

### The T/D Linking Rule
When a word finishes with a T or D sound, and the next word begins with a vowel, drag the consonant to the start of the vowel word.
- *Most of* becomes *mo-stov*.
- *Turned off* becomes *turn-doff*.

*Note on Phrasal Verbs:* Always stress the preposition (the second word) more than the verb itself.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Phrasal Verb | Past Tense (Linked) | Meaning | Natural Example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Pay off | Paid off (pai-doff) | 1. To have good results from effort. 2. To finish paying a loan completely. | 1. Our hard work paid off. 2. We finally paid off our car loan. |
| Lay off | Laid off (lai-doff) | To lose your job (usually due to budget cuts, not performance). | Many people were laid off due to budget cuts. |
| Wrap up | Wrapped up (wrap-tup) | To finish something completely (a meeting, a project). | We will wrap up the meeting in a few minutes. |
| Burn out | Burned out (burn-dout) | To become physically or mentally exhausted from working too hard. | He burned out from working 60 hours a week. |
| Miss out | Missed out (miss-tout) | To lose a good opportunity because you didn't act quickly enough. | We didn't take action, so we missed out on the investment. |
| Price out | Priced out (price-tout) | To be unable to buy something because the price has increased too much. | If we don't buy a house now, we will be priced out of the market. |
| Call in | Called in (call-din) | To call your workplace to tell them you cannot come in (usually "call in sick"). | Several employees called in sick today. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. How much does it cost?
2. Our investment really paid off.
3. Is your house paid off yet?
4. After the meeting wrapped up, everybody went home.
5. Our department is short-staffed today because three people called in sick.

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying "wrapped up". Ensure the 'ed' sounds like a 'T', and link it to the 'U' (wrap-tup).
2. Write a sentence describing a time you "missed out" on a fun event or opportunity.
3. What is the difference between getting fired and getting "laid off"?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you work 80 hours a week for a year, you are in danger of doing what?
2. If you finish paying the bank for your car, the car is officially what?
3. In a phrasal verb, which word usually receives the vocal stress?

### Answer Key

1. Burning out.
2. Paid off.
3. The second word (the preposition).

<br/>

category: "english-writing"

# 8 Tips for Understanding Native Speakers

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Many advanced English learners get frustrated when they cannot understand fast native speakers. In this lesson, Lisa explains why native speakers are harder to understand than English teachers, and she shares 8 practical tips to improve your listening comprehension.

## Key Points

- Teachers speak slowly and avoid slang; regular native speakers link words and use heavy idiomatic expressions.
- Native speakers speak in "thought groups" rather than individual words.
- You do not need to understand every single word. Focus on the stressed "content words" (nouns and verbs).

## How to Review

Find a YouTube video of a native speaker discussing a topic you love. Turn off the captions. Try to identify the "content words" they are stressing in each sentence.

## Eight Listening Tips

### 1. Listen for Thought Groups, Not Individual Words
Native speakers do not pause between every word. They group words together grammatically and speak them as one continuous breath. 
- *Example:* "What are you talking about" sounds like "Whadareyou talking about."

### 2. Focus on Content Words
You do not need to hear every single preposition or pronoun. Focus on the words that hold meaning (nouns, verbs, adjectives). The "function words" (the, a, of, to) are naturally swallowed and reduced in fast speech.
- *Example:* "If you hadn't **told** me, I wouldn't have **known** about it." (Only "told" and "known" are heavily stressed).

### 3. Don't Try to Understand Everyone
Different ages, professions, and regions use different slang and speaking speeds. Choose the type of speaker that is most relevant to your goals (e.g., business professionals) and focus on understanding them first.

### 4. Focus on One Accent
Don't frustrate yourself by trying to understand standard American, deep Southern, British, Australian, and Scottish all at once. Focus your training on the standard accent you need most.

### 5. Keep Learning Expressions and Idioms
If you hear a sentence where you know every single word, but the sentence still makes no sense, it is an idiom. You must memorize these.
- *Cold turkey:* To quit a bad habit abruptly and completely. ("I decided to go cold turkey.")
- *Hold your horses:* Wait a moment; slow down.

### 6. Listen More Than Once
The first time you listen, you will miss things. The second and third times, your brain will start recognizing the linked words and reduced sounds.

### 7. Listen to Topics You Enjoy
If you are passionate about a topic (like cooking, sports, or tech), your brain is more motivated to focus, learn the vocabulary, and retain the information.

### 8. Use Audio with Transcripts
Listen to a podcast or audiobook while reading the text. Then, try listening without the text to see how much your ear has trained itself to hear the linked words.

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. What are you talking about?
2. I don't know what he's talking about.
3. If you hadn't told me, I wouldn't have known about it.
4. I decided to quit smoking and just go cold turkey.
5. Whoa, hold your horses! Let's think about this for a minute.

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying "If you hadn't told me." Stress the word "told" and say the rest very quickly.
2. What does it mean to quit a bad habit "cold turkey"?
3. Why is an English teacher usually easier to understand than a regular native speaker?

## Mini Quiz

1. What do we call the important words (nouns and verbs) that receive the most stress in a sentence?
2. True or False: If you can't understand every native speaker you meet, your English is bad.
3. If someone tells you to "hold your horses," what do they want you to do?

### Answer Key

1. Content words.
2. False. Even native speakers struggle to understand different dialects or heavy slang from other regions.
3. Wait a moment and think before acting.

<br/>

# About Lisa Mojsin

## Summary

Lisa Mojsin introduces herself and her channel, Accurate English. She has over 25 years of experience teaching pronunciation and accent reduction in Los Angeles, working with Hollywood actors and corporate professionals.

- Her training focuses on ensuring professionals and actors are clearly understood every time they speak.
- She emphasizes that standard English classes rarely teach the actual rules of the American accent (linking, reduction, and word stress).

# Leadership Idioms with an Executive Coach

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this interview with executive coach Gene Sullivan, you will learn advanced expressions related to leadership, career development, and business dynamics. 

## Key Points

- Career and leadership vocabulary: *peers*, *dictatorial*, and *prodigy*.
- Idioms of difficulty: *run into a wall*, *at stake*.
- Conversational verbs: *lend itself to*, *subscribe to*, and *pick up*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph describing your career goals. Try to use at least three expressions from this lesson, such as "make a name for myself," "put in the work," or "run into a wall."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Bossy / demanding | Dictatorial | Expecting people to obey orders without question; bossy. | You can't be dictatorial if you want people to respect you. |
| Coworkers / equals | Peers | People who are at the same level, age, or social class as you. | He is highly respected by all of his peers. |
| Emphasize strongly | Impress upon | To emphasize a point so someone understands how important it is. | I need to impress upon you the seriousness of this situation. |
| Put away prejudice | Set biases aside | To make an effort not to judge a situation based on personal prejudice. | We need to set our biases aside to make a fair decision. |
| Benefit someone | Do someone good | To be helpful or beneficial for someone's well-being or reputation. | Taking a long vacation will do you good. |
| Agree with / support | Subscribe to | To agree with a belief, philosophy, or idea. | I don't subscribe to the idea of a "natural-born" leader. |
| Be suitable for | Lend itself to | To be suitable for something, or to cause something to happen. | This system lends itself to internal conflict. |
| Hit a dead end | Run into a wall | To reach a point where a problem seems impossible to solve. | We were making progress, but then we ran into a wall. |
| Large collection | Body of (research) | A large amount of collected information or work. | A growing body of evidence suggests this is true. |
| Child genius | Prodigy | A child who is exceptionally talented at a young age. | She was a musical prodigy at the age of seven. |
| Based on own merit | In one's own right | Successful because of one's own effort, not because of connections. | His father was an accomplished musician in his own right. |
| Spend effort | Put in | The amount of effort or time spent doing something. | It's the amount of practice you put in that matters. |
| At risk of losing | At stake | When there is a risk that something valuable might be lost. | He works hard because his whole career is at stake. |
| Learn | Pick up | To learn a new concept or skill (often quickly). | I'd love to pick up the guitar. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Dictatorial | dic-ta-TOR-i-al | Stressing the first 'a' |
| Buddies | BUH-deez | Pronouncing it with an 'ah' sound like "bodies" |
| Peer | PEER | Pronouncing it with an 'a' sound like "pear" or "bear" |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The manager was too dictatorial, so the employees quit.
2. She is an amazing scientist, and she is respected by all of her peers.
3. I want to impress upon you how important this project is.
4. He needs to set his biases aside and listen to the team.
5. I do not subscribe to that pessimistic philosophy.
6. We ran into a brick wall when the client cut our budget.
7. She became famous in her own right.
8. I'm putting in ten hours a day at the office.
9. There is a lot at stake with this new contract.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever "run into a wall" while working on a project? What happened?
2. What is something you "do not subscribe to" (e.g., a common belief or habit)?
3. Why do people work harder when they have something important "at stake"?

## Mini Quiz

1. If someone is an equal to you at work, they are your what?
2. If a child is exceptionally talented at playing the piano, they are a musical what?
3. If you learn a new skill quickly, you _________ it up.

### Answer Key

1. Peer.
2. Prodigy.
3. Picked.

# Acting and Enthusiasm Idioms

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this interview with Megan, an actress living in Los Angeles, you will learn expressions related to the entertainment industry, enthusiasm, and diving into new projects. Lisa also explains how native speakers use the word "slash" in conversation and how they pronounce the word "school."

## Key Points

- Expressions of enthusiasm: *caught the bug*, *dive head first*, and *jump right in*.
- LA-specific phrases: *The industry* means the film industry; *out here* refers to the West Coast.
- Pronunciation rules: The dark 'L' in words like "school" and "cool."

## How to Review

Write a sentence about a new hobby you started using the expression "I caught the bug," and explain how you "jumped right in."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Become passionate | Catch the bug | To suddenly develop a strong interest or passion for something. | I took one photography class and immediately caught the bug. |
| Start eagerly | Dive head first | To start doing something eagerly without overthinking it. | I didn't plan it; I just dived head first into the project. |
| Film business | The industry | In Los Angeles, this specifically means the entertainment industry. | Many of my neighbors work in the industry. |
| Extremely successful | Booming | Exploding with success; doing really well financially. | The tourism industry is booming right now. |
| In the West | Out here | Refers to living on the West Coast of the US (California). | I moved out here two years ago for acting. |
| In the East | Back east | Refers to living on the East Coast of the US (New York, etc.). | My family still lives back east. |
| Start immediately | Jump right in | To quickly get involved in something without delaying. | We have a lot to cover, so let's jump right in. |
| The absolute center | The heart of it | The core, center, or most important part of something. | Let's get right to the heart of the matter. |
| I think (uncertain) | I want to say... | Used when you are trying to remember a fact but aren't 100% sure. | "When did we meet?" "I want to say... six months ago." |
| Continuing a long time | Long-running | Being shown or continuing for a long period of time (like a TV show). | I would love to get cast on a long-running dramatic series. |
| Slowly begin to like | Grow to (love/like) | To develop a liking for something over time that you didn't like at first. | I hated sushi at first, but I grew to like it. |
| Both / And | Slash | Used to describe someone/something with a dual function. | She is an actress slash model. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| School | SKOO-ul | Pronouncing a hard, flat 'L'. Native speakers use a "dark L" that creates an extra syllable sound. |
| Theater | THEE-uh-ter | Pronouncing the 'a' strongly. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. She took one ballet lesson and immediately caught the bug.
2. I wanted to dive head first into starting my own business.
3. The real estate market is booming out here in California.
4. I grew up back east, but I moved out here for college.
5. If you have any questions during the presentation, feel free to jump right in.
6. The restaurant is located right in the heart of the city.
7. I want to say it cost about fifty dollars, but I'm not sure.
8. This room is my office slash guest bedroom.

## Practice Prompts

1. What is something you "grew to like" as you got older?
2. If you live in Los Angeles and someone asks if you work in "the industry," what do they mean?
3. Write a sentence using "slash" to describe a person with two jobs.

## Mini Quiz

1. If a business is making a ton of money and expanding rapidly, it is ____________.
2. If you start a new project immediately with massive enthusiasm, you dived ____________.
3. If someone says "I want to say it was Tuesday," are they absolutely certain it was Tuesday?

### Answer Key

1. Booming.
2. Head first (or dived in).
3. No, they are guessing or trying to remember.

# Idioms using the word "Name"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, native speaker Drake teaches a variety of highly common English idioms and expressions that all revolve around the word "name." 

## Key Points

- Expressions for remembering (or forgetting) people: *what's-his-name*, *I didn't catch your name*, *bad with names*.
- Expressions for reputation: *make a name for yourself*, *name dropper*.
- Expressions for reality and accusations: *the name of the game*, *name names*.

## How to Review

Write a short paragraph about someone you met "the other day." Use the phrases "what's-his-name" and "I'm bad with names."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Anything you can think of | You name it | Used to say there is a huge variety or everything is available. | They sell laptops, phones, TVs—you name it, they have it. |
| That guy (forgotten name) | What's-his-name | Used when you casually forget a man's name. (What's-her-name for women). | I was talking to what's-his-name the other day. |
| A few days ago | The other day | Recently; a few days ago. | I ran into my old boss the other day. |
| I assumed / I thought | I figured | I thought, I believed, or it was my guess. | She was crying, so I figured she was upset. |
| I didn't hear your name | I didn't catch your name | A polite way to ask someone to repeat their name because you forgot or didn't hear it. | I'm sorry, I didn't catch your name. |
| That's how it is | The name of the game | The accepted reality or essential nature of a specific situation. | In this business, working late is the name of the game. |
| Trick / cheat | Dupe / Scam | To deceive someone into believing a lie or giving you money. | The car salesman duped me into paying too much. |
| Insult someone | Call someone names | To insult someone using disrespectful words (stupid, idiot). | The teacher said there is no name-calling allowed in class. |
| Brag about connections | Name dropping | Mentioning famous/important people you know to impress others. | He's such a name dropper; he always mentions the celebrities he met. |
| Informal relationship | On a first-name basis | Knowing someone well enough to use their first name instead of "Mr." or "Mrs." | My boss and I are on a first-name basis. |
| Completely broke | Not have a penny to one's name | Having absolutely zero money; being completely broke. | He lost his job and doesn't have a penny to his name. |
| Forgetful with names | Bad with names | A way to admit you have trouble remembering people's names. | Please tell me again, I am really bad with names. |
| Identify guilty people | Name names | To specifically identify people involved in something wrong or illegal. | The witness went to the police and started naming names. |
| Telltale | Tattletale | A child (or childish person) who tells authority figures when someone breaks a rule. | Don't be a tattletale; mind your own business. |
| Become famous/respected | Make a name for oneself | To build a good reputation and become well-known for your skills. | She really made a name for herself as a lawyer. |
| Perfect for you | Has your name written all over it | When an item or opportunity is absolutely perfectly suited for someone. | You have to buy that jacket; it has your name written all over it! |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Correct pronunciation |
| --- | --- |
| What's his name | WHATS-iz-name (drop the 'H' sound on his) |
| The other day | THEE-uther-day (link the 'E' of 'the' to the 'O' of 'other') |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. He can sing, dance, act—you name it, he can do it.
2. I was talking to what's-her-name the other day about the project.
3. I figured you wouldn't want to come since you were sick.
4. I'm sorry, I didn't catch your name; I am really bad with names.
5. In sales, rejection is just the name of the game.
6. The scammer duped the old lady into giving him her credit card.
7. Stop calling your brother names!
8. I don't like him; he's always name-dropping to sound important.
9. I'm on a first-name basis with the CEO.
10. The detective demanded that the suspect start naming names.
11. He worked hard and made a name for himself in the restaurant industry.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever been "duped" or "scammed" into buying something? What happened?
2. Write a sentence describing a career where rejection is "the name of the game."
3. Think of a friend. What kind of gift would have "their name written all over it"?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you forget someone's name right after meeting them, what polite phrase can you use?
2. If you have absolutely zero money in your bank account, you don't have a _________ to your name.
3. If someone constantly brags about meeting celebrities, they are a ____________.

### Answer Key

1. "I'm sorry, I didn't catch your name."
2. Penny.
3. Name dropper.

<br/>

# Emergency Services Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this interview with Tom, a former firefighter and paramedic, you will learn the advanced vocabulary and expressions used in emergency services, as well as general conversational phrases used to discuss career changes, teasing, and teamwork.

## Key Points

- Career vocabulary: *Firefighter/paramedic*, *shift*, *chain of command*.
- Expressions of camaraderie: *Tease*, *camaraderie*, *for lack of a better term*.
- Extreme situations: *Line of duty*, *at all hours*, *inebriated*, *onslaught*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph describing the difficult parts of a challenging job using the phrases "chain of command," "at all hours," and "camaraderie."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Baby/small child | Toddler | A young child, usually between one and three years old. | That toy is not suitable for toddlers. |
| Move | Shift | To move from one place or position to another. | He shifted from a small town to a big city. |
| Make fun of / joke | Tease | To playfully (or sometimes unkindly) laugh at or joke with someone. | When he was a child, he was teased about his weight. |
| Friendship / trust | Camaraderie | Mutual trust and friendship among people who spend a lot of time together. | I miss the camaraderie of my former coworkers. |
| Used to (past habit) | Would | Used to describe things done repeatedly as a regular habit in the past. | When I was a child, we would swim in the lake every summer. |
| I don't have the exact word | For lack of a better term | Used when you cannot think of the precise word you need, so you use an approximate one. | We would, for lack of a better term, share our feelings. |
| Unselfishness | Selflessness | Caring about other people more than you care about yourself. | The firefighters showed great selflessness. |
| Hierarchy of authority | Chain of command | The official ranking system that defines who gives orders to whom. | You must report to the chain of command. |
| While working | Line of duty (death/injury) | Happening while officially doing your job, usually in the military or emergency services. | A policeman was injured in the line of duty. |
| All the time / late | At all hours | Happening constantly, often at inappropriate times (like late at night). | My neighbor plays loud music at all hours of the night. |
| Drunk (formal) | Inebriated | A formal or medical way to say that someone is drunk. | The patient was heavily inebriated. |
| Fierce attack | Onslaught | A fierce physical attack or an overwhelming amount of criticism. | The president changed his mind after an onslaught of criticism. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Inebriated | in-EE-bree-ay-ted | Ensure you link it correctly when speaking fast: "in an inebriated state" (in-an-in-ee-bree-ay-ted). |
| Criticism | CRI-ti-si-zm | All three 'i's are short 'i' sounds, not 'ee'. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The toddlers were playing in the park.
2. I was a small-town boy who shifted into a large international city.
3. Good friends often tease each other playfully.
4. It is good to have a source of camaraderie with your team.
5. In the summer, we would sit outside and talk.
6. It was a perfect storm, for lack of a better term.
7. You cannot skip the boss; you must follow the chain of command.
8. The baby cries at all hours of the night.
9. You can tell he is inebriated by the way he is walking.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence about something you "would" do every day when you were a child.
2. What does it mean if someone makes noise "at all hours"?
3. Use the phrase "for lack of a better term" in a sentence describing a confusing situation.

## Mini Quiz

1. What is a more polite, formal word for "drunk"?
2. What is the word for the mutual trust and friendship that develops among teammates?
3. True or False: "I would go to the beach" can mean "I used to go to the beach regularly."

### Answer Key

1. Inebriated.
2. Camaraderie.
3. True.

<br/>

# Real Estate and Business Idioms

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this interview with Lauren, a professional home stager in Los Angeles, you will learn the vocabulary and expressions used in the real estate industry. A home stager prepares a house for sale by filling it with beautiful furniture, and Lauren's business vocabulary is excellent for anyone working in a fast-paced corporate environment.

## Key Points

- Sales terminology: *Appeal to a broader audience*, *multiple offers*, *over asking*, *high volume*.
- Business flow: *Fast-paced*, *move forward*, *seamlessly*.
- Description words: *Luxurious*, *high-rise condo*, *blown away*.

## How to Review

Describe a time when you were "blown away" by a product or a service, and explain how the business handled everything "seamlessly."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Lots of people like it | Appeal to a broader audience | To be attractive or interesting to a wider variety of people. | We designed the product to appeal to a broader audience. |
| More than one offer | Multiple offers | Receiving several different offers or bids to buy something. | The house received multiple offers on the first day. |
| Moving very fast | Fast-paced | Happening very quickly; high-energy. | I work in a fast-paced environment. |
| Very expensive/fancy | Luxury / Luxurious | Extremely comfortable, elegant, or expensive. | They filled the room with luxury furniture. |
| Proceed as planned | Move forward | To continue with a plan or action. | They decided to move forward with selling the home. |
| Existed | Been around | To have existed for a certain amount of time. | Our company has been around for 13 years. |
| Tall apartment building | High-rise condo | A condominium (an apartment you own) located in a tall building with many stories. | They live in a high-rise condo in West Hollywood. |
| Partly / not completely | To an extent | Used to say you agree or something is true, but only up to a certain point. | I agree with you to an extent. |
| Provide food/drink | Cater | To professionally provide food and drinks for an event. | The business meeting was catered by a local restaurant. |
| Extremely impressed | Blown away | To be deeply impressed, overwhelmed, or amazed by something. | The homeowner was completely blown away by the design. |
| Very important to me | Meant a lot | Used to express that an action was deeply appreciated or meaningful. | Thank you for the gift; it meant a lot to me. |
| Large quantity | High volume | A large amount or quantity of something. | We have a high volume of sales in the summer. |
| Smoothly | Seamlessly | When things flow perfectly without any problems or interruptions. | We planned everything so the event would go seamlessly. |
| The requested price | Asking / Asking price | The original price the seller requested for an item or property. | We sold the house for $50,000 over asking. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Luxury | LUG-zhur-ee | Pronouncing the 'x' as a sharp 'ks'. It is a soft 'g' sound. |
| Luxurious | lug-ZHUR-ee-us | |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The new design appeals to a broader audience.
2. The house is beautiful, and we expect to receive multiple offers.
3. This is a very fast-paced industry.
4. They decided to move forward with the project.
5. That restaurant has been around for 50 years.
6. She lives in a high-rise condo downtown.
7. I agree with his theory to an extent.
8. We need to hire someone to cater the wedding.
9. I was totally blown away by his performance.
10. Your support really meant a lot to me.
11. We process a high volume of orders every day.
12. The transition to the new software went seamlessly.
13. They offered twenty thousand dollars over asking.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever been "blown away" by someone's talent? Describe it.
2. What is a polite way to say you only agree with a colleague's idea up to a certain point?
3. Write a sentence describing a business that does a "high volume" of sales.

## Mini Quiz

1. If you own an apartment in a very tall building, what do you own?
2. If an event went perfectly with zero mistakes, it went ____________.
3. What is the difference between an apartment and a condo?

### Answer Key

1. A high-rise condo.
2. Seamlessly.
3. You rent an apartment, but you own a condo.

# Fine Dining and Restaurant Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this interview with Kendra, a server at a top-tier restaurant, you will learn the specific terminology used in the American restaurant industry. You will also learn advanced adjectives for describing people and buildings, such as *flashy* and *unassuming*.

## Key Points

- Restaurant terminology: *Fine dining*, *pairings*, *server* (vs. *waiter/waitress*), *diner*, *dish*.
- Descriptive adjectives: *Flashy*, *unassuming*, *sub-par*, *outlandish*, *disheartening*.
- Expressions of effort: *Bring your A-game*, *push and pull*, *abide by*.

## How to Review

Pretend you are reviewing an expensive restaurant. Use the words "fine dining," "sub-par," and "disheartening" to describe a negative experience.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Expensive restaurant | Fine dining | High-end, expensive, and elegant restaurants. | She works in a fine dining establishment. |
| Drink matching | Pairings | Knowing the appropriate wine to serve with a particular meal. | The server has great knowledge of wine pairings. |
| Showy / bright | Flashy | Bright, noticeable, and designed to impress others with wealth. | Los Angeles is much more flashy than New York. |
| Show off | Flaunt | To show off your money, success, or beauty so others notice. | He doesn't like to flaunt his wealth. |
| Hidden | Concealed | Hidden from view. | The restaurant is concealed in an unassuming building. |
| Modest / humble | Unassuming | Not trying to appear important; modest; quiet. | Despite his great power, he had an unassuming personality. |
| Waiter / Waitress | Server | The modern, professional, gender-neutral term for a restaurant worker. | The word 'server' sounds more professional than 'waiter'. |
| Casual restaurant | Diner | A casual, often 24-hour restaurant that serves American food. (Also means a person eating dinner). | We went to a diner for pancakes at 2 AM. |
| Disappointing | Disheartening | Causing you to lose hope, courage, or enthusiasm. | It is disheartening when customers are rude. |
| Follow the rules | Abide by | To obey a rule or accept a decision, even if you disagree. | We must abide by the rule that the customer is always right. |
| Strange / weird | Outlandish | Looking or sounding strange, unusual, or highly inappropriate. | His outlandish behavior got him kicked out. |
| Discuss logically | Reason with | To discuss a problem logically to reach an agreement. | The angry customer was impossible to reason with. |
| A specific meal | Dish | Food prepared in a particular way (e.g., a seafood dish). | My grandmother cooks my favorite dish. |
| Noticeable difference | Remarkable difference | A very large, surprising, or noticeable difference. | There is a remarkable difference in salaries between states. |
| My hometown | Back home | The city or state where you were born or grew up. | I am going back home for the holidays. |
| Best effort | A-game | Your absolute best effort or highest level of performance. | There is a lot of competition, so bring your A-game. |
| Struggle/balance | Push and pull | A constant struggle to find the right balance between two things. | Life is a constant push and pull between work and family. |
| Below average | Sub-par | Not high quality; worse than expected. | The food at that restaurant was sub-par. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The wine pairings for this dinner are excellent.
2. She bought a flashy new sports car.
3. They like to flaunt their wealth by buying expensive clothes.
4. The entrance was concealed behind an unassuming door.
5. I prefer to be called a server rather than a waitress.
6. We are going to the diner to get some food.
7. It was very disheartening to fail the test again.
8. You must abide by the terms of the contract.
9. She wore an outlandish outfit to the office.
10. When he gets angry, he is difficult to reason with.
11. You have made remarkable progress in your English!
12. I don't think the people back home know about this law.
13. If you want the job, you need to bring your A-game to the interview.

## Practice Prompts

1. What does it mean if someone's work is "sub-par"?
2. What is the difference between a "dinner" and a "diner"?
3. Write a sentence about a rule you must "abide by" at your school or workplace.

## Mini Quiz

1. If a famous actor dresses very modestly and drives a cheap car, he is being ____________.
2. If you do your absolute best on a test, you brought your ____________.
3. True or False: "Server" is the gender-neutral, professional term for a waiter or waitress.

### Answer Key

1. Unassuming.
2. A-game.
3. True.

# VIP and Hospitality Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

Megan works at Spago, one of the most famous fine-dining restaurants in Beverly Hills. In this interview, she discusses serving celebrities and the VIP treatment they receive. Lisa explains several advanced expressions related to elite clientele, influence, and casual storytelling.

## Key Points

- Celebrity vocabulary: *Spot*, *A-lister*, *VIP*, *top-tier clientele*.
- Casual storytelling: Using "and I go" or "and they're like" instead of "and I said."
- Expressions of influence and service: *Pull strings*, *accommodate*, *set aside*.

## How to Review

Write a short story about trying to get a table at a famous restaurant. Use the phrases "VIP," "accommodate," and "pull strings."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Notice | Spot | To notice someone or something, especially when it is difficult. | Spago is a great restaurant for celebrity spotting. |
| And I said | And I go / And I'm like | Highly casual slang used in storytelling to mean "and I said." | He goes, "How are you?" and I go, "I'm fine." |
| Huge movie star | A-lister | The most famous, popular, and highly-paid movie stars. | Tom Cruise and Leonardo DiCaprio are A-listers. |
| Highest level | Top-tier | At the highest level of quality, importance, or prestige. | He works for a top-tier consulting firm. |
| Customer base | Clientele | A group of clients or the specific type of clients a business attracts. | That hotel attracts a wealthy clientele. |
| As you might say | If you will | Used when describing something in a slightly unusual way or searching for the right phrase. | We serve top-tier clientele, if you will. |
| Use your influence | Pull strings | To use your influence or connections to get something done secretly or unfairly. | She was able to pull strings to get him the job. |
| Save for later | Set aside | To keep something (money, a table, time) available for a special purpose. | We set aside some money for a vacation. |
| Without planning | Last minute | Happening at the last possible time; not planned in advance. | I booked a last-minute flight to New York. |
| Very Important Person | VIP | A very important or influential person who receives special treatment. | We received the VIP treatment at the hotel. |
| Honestly / Truly | Genuinely | Used to show that a feeling is honest, real, and authentic. | He seems genuinely interested in the project. |
| Serve their needs | Accommodate | To do what someone wants you to do, often adjusting your own plans to help them. | The restaurant will do everything possible to accommodate Tom Cruise. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Usage Note |
| --- | --- |
| If Tom Cruise called | Used for hypothetical situations (If he did this...). |
| If Tom Cruise were to call | Also used for hypothetical situations; slightly more formal but means the exact same thing. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I spotted two mistakes in my essay.
2. Tom Cruise is a Hollywood A-lister.
3. This firm attracts top-tier clientele.
4. I don't know the owner, so I can't pull any strings to get you a reservation.
5. Is there a table set aside for the VIPs?
6. Why do you always do your homework at the last minute?
7. I am genuinely happy for your success.
8. The hotel staff was very accommodating to our requests.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever done something "last minute"? What was it?
2. What does it mean if an actor is on the "B-list"?
3. Rewrite this casual sentence into formal English: "And he goes, 'I can't believe it!' and I'm like, 'Me neither!'"

## Mini Quiz

1. If a restaurant is the absolute best in the city, it is a _________ restaurant.
2. If you use secret connections to get a favor, you are pulling ___________.
3. If someone tells you the truth and is very sincere, they are being ____________.

### Answer Key

1. Top-tier.
2. Strings.
3. Genuine.

<br/>

title: "Advanced Vocabulary - How Good is your English?"
description: "Test your advanced English vocabulary with this 12-question multiple-choice quiz covering words like mitigate, disparage, and equivocate."
category: "english-writing"
theme: "vocabulary"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"

language: "en"

---

# Advanced Vocabulary Quiz

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson features a 12-word multiple-choice quiz designed to test your knowledge of highly advanced English vocabulary verbs. Lisa explains the definition of each word, provides sample sentences, and gives crucial pronunciation tips for tricky consonants and vowels.

## Key Points

- Verbs of impact: *Mitigate*, *undermine*, *underpin*, *withstand*.
- Verbs of communication: *Gush*, *disparage*, *admonish*, *equivocate*.
- Verbs of control: *Harness*, *inculcate*, *rally*, *wield*.

## How to Review

Take the 12 words listed below and try to write one cohesive paragraph that uses at least five of them correctly (e.g., a story about a leader *rallying* a team to *withstand* an *onslaught*).

## Vocabulary Words

| Word | Meaning | Example Sentence |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Mitigate | To make something less severe, serious, or painful. | The doctor mitigated the patient's pain with medication. |
| Gush | 1. To flow out suddenly. 2. To speak with excessive, overly emotional enthusiasm. | The grandparents gushed over their beautiful new grandchild. |
| Harness | To control and make use of something (like energy or power). | Scientists are finding new ways to harness solar energy. |
| Inculcate | To teach or instill an idea/habit through repetition. | Parents should inculcate good manners in their children. |
| Disparage | To belittle or criticize someone unfairly. | It is unfair to disparage someone just because they have a different opinion. |
| Rally | To come together for a common purpose or to support a cause. | The community rallied to support the victims of the disaster. |
| Admonish | To warn or reprimand someone gently but firmly. | The teacher had to admonish the students for talking. |
| Equivocate | To avoid speaking directly or avoid giving a clear answer. | Politicians often equivocate to avoid criticism. |
| Wield | To hold or control a tool, weapon, or power/influence. | The CEO wields a lot of influence over the company's decisions. |
| Withstand | To resist or endure something successfully. | The bridge was built to withstand strong earthquakes. |
| Undermine | To damage or weaken something gradually (like confidence or trust). | The constant criticism began to undermine her confidence. |
| Underpin | To serve as a foundation or support for something. | Solid evidence underpinned his claim in court. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Rule/Note |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Gushed | GUSHT | If a verb ends in a voiceless consonant (sh, ch, k, p), the 'ed' is pronounced as a 'T'. |
| Admonished | ad-MON-isht | Same rule as above. |
| Equivocate | ee-QUIV-oh-cate | The 'qu' makes a 'kw' sound. |
| Wield | WEE-uld | When a long vowel is followed by an 'L', add a tiny schwa (uh) sound before the L. (e.g., feel -> fee-ul). |
| Withstand | with-STAND | The tongue must move quickly from the 'th' to the 's' without dropping either sound. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. What can be done to mitigate the risks?
2. She began to gush about her favorite movie.
3. He learned to harness his inner strength during the competition.
4. The teacher worked hard to inculcate a love of reading in her students.
5. Critics disparaged the movie for its lack of originality.
6. The team rallied and won the game.
7. The librarian admonished the children for making too much noise.
8. Instead of giving a clear answer, the lawyer equivocated.
9. The knight wielded his sword. (Silent W in sword: SORD).
10. The ancient architecture has withstood the test of time.
11. Corruption can undermine the stability of a government.
12. Trust and communication underpin every healthy relationship.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence about a building that was built to "withstand" a natural disaster.
2. What does a politician do when they "equivocate" during a debate?
3. Describe a time you had to "rally" together with your friends or coworkers to solve a problem.

## Mini Quiz

1. If you gently tell a child to stop running, you _________ them.
2. If you slowly damage someone's confidence, you _________ it.
3. If you speak with intense, overwhelming excitement about a celebrity, you are _________ over them.

### Answer Key

1. Admonish.
2. Undermine.
3. Gushing.

<br/>

---

# Business and News Vocabulary: Meta Layoffs

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa uses an article from The New York Times about Meta's recent layoffs to teach advanced business vocabulary. The lesson also covers the pronunciation of popular social media platforms and tricky words like "accumulate."

## Key Points

- Business changes: *Workforce*, *hiring freeze*, *slash*, *reversal of fortune*.
- Describing success and excess: *High-flying*, *lavish*, *perk*, *soar*.
- Difficulties: *Scrutiny*, *dent*, *grapple with*, *play out*.
- Pronunciation rules: Facebook (uh sound, not ooh), Instagram/WhatsApp (schwa and short 'a' sounds).

## How to Review

Write a paragraph describing a struggling business using the words "grapple with," "slash," "workforce," and "reversal of fortune."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Employees | Workforce | The total group of people working in an organization or a country. | Women represent almost 50% of the workforce. |
| Stop hiring | Hiring freeze | When a company officially stops hiring new employees to save money. | The company has extended its hiring freeze. |
| Greatly reduce / cut | Slash | To drastically reduce a price, amount, or number of jobs. | The store is slashing prices on all items. |
| Shocking | Stunning | Extremely shocking, surprising, or beautiful. | I was absolutely stunned by the news. |
| Major change in luck | Reversal of fortune | A complete change in a situation, often from very good to very bad. | The company experienced a stunning reversal of fortune. |
| Very successful | High-flying | Highly successful, especially in business or academics. | She quit her high-flying corporate job. |
| Expensive / luxurious | Lavish | Large, impressive, and very expensive. | They had a lavish wedding. |
| Desirable / wanted | Enviable | Highly desirable; making other people jealous or wanting it. | He has an enviable job with great hours. |
| Job benefit | Perk | An extra benefit given to employees in addition to their salary. | Free gym membership is a great perk. |
| Close examination | Scrutiny | Careful, thorough investigation or observation. | His actions came under intense public scrutiny. |
| Damage slightly | Dent | To slightly damage or harm something (like a car or confidence). | The failure dented his confidence. |
| Income | Revenue | The money a business makes from its normal operations. | The company's revenue increased this year. |
| Rise rapidly | Soar | To increase very quickly to a high level. | The price of gasoline has soared. |
| Struggle to solve | Grapple with | To attempt to deal with a difficult problem or situation. | We are grappling with the issue of homelessness. |
| Develop / end up | Play out | How a situation develops, unfolds, or ends over time. | Let's wait and see how this plays out. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Facebook | FAYS-buk | The double 'oo' is an 'uh' sound (like 'look' or 'book'), not an 'ooh' sound. |
| WhatsApp | WUTS-app | The first 'a' is an 'uh' sound. The second 'a' is short 'a' (like apple). |
| Accumulate | uh-KYOO-myoo-layt | The two 'u's are pronounced as 'yoo', not 'ooh'. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The parent company reduced its workforce by 13 percent.
2. We had to implement a hiring freeze for the rest of the year.
3. The store slashed its prices by 50%.
4. You look absolutely stunning in that dress.
5. The once high-flying company is now in ruins.
6. The room was lavishly decorated.
7. My friends showered me with gifts on my birthday.
8. One of the perks of the job is free travel.
9. Celebrities constantly deal with intense media scrutiny.
10. The bad news didn't dent his optimism.
11. Housing prices have soared in the last few years.
12. The government is grappling with a severe economic slowdown.
13. I wonder how this situation is going to play out.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever received a "perk" at a job? What was it?
2. What does it mean if an actor experiences a "reversal of fortune"?
3. Use the phrasal verb "play out" in a sentence about an unpredictable sports game.

## Mini Quiz

1. If you cut jobs or prices drastically, you _________ them.
2. If your confidence is slightly damaged, it has been _________.
3. If an issue is very complicated and you are struggling to fix it, you are _________ with it.

### Answer Key

1. Slash.
2. Dented.
3. Grappling.

<br/>

# Economic and Political Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa uses an article from Time Magazine about high gas prices to teach vocabulary related to economic difficulty and government action. The lesson also highlights the critical difference between "little" (negative) and "a little" (positive).

## Key Points

- Grammar focus: *Little* (not enough/lack of) vs. *A little* (some/positive).
- Extreme adjectives and verbs: *Whopping*, *rage on*, *scramble*, *slam*, *triple*.
- Economic problems: *Crunch*, *curb*, *pain at the pumps*.
- Solutions: *Ramp up*, *near-term*, *address*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph about an economic "crunch" using the words "ramp up," "curb," "scramble," and "whopping."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Not enough (negative) | Little | Meaning almost none; a lack of something. | I have little hope that things will improve. |
| Some (positive) | A little | Meaning a small amount, but viewing it positively. | I have a little time, so we can talk now. |
| High gas prices | Pain at the pumps | The financial stress felt when buying expensive gasoline. | Drivers are feeling the pain at the pumps. |
| Worst affected | Hardest hit | The people or places that suffer the most damage or difficulty. | California was the hardest hit by the storm. |
| Huge / Very large | Whopping | Used before a number or amount to emphasize that it is extremely large. | The house sold for a whopping 20 million dollars. |
| Continue violently | Rage / Rage on | To continue with great force or violence. | The battle still rages on. |
| Rush to do something | Scramble | To try very hard to do something difficult quickly or in a panic. | Lawmakers are scrambling to find a quick solution. |
| Increase / Boost | Ramp up | To increase the speed, power, or production of something. | We need to ramp up production of gasoline. |
| Criticize harshly | Slam | To attack someone verbally; to criticize severely. | The president slammed the oil companies. |
| Become three times larger | Triple | To increase by three times the original amount. | Our profits have tripled this year. |
| Severe shortage / crisis | Crunch | A difficult economic situation caused by a lack of money or time. | Families are facing a financial crunch. |
| Short-term | Near-term | Happening in the near future; short-term. | We need concrete, near-term solutions. |
| Deal with | Address | To start trying to solve or deal with a problem. | We must address this issue immediately. |
| Limit / Control | Curb | To control or limit the growth of something harmful. | The government is trying to curb inflation. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Usage Note / Pronunciation |
| --- | --- |
| Address | Noun: ADD-ress (First syllable stressed: I need your address). Verb: uh-DRESS (Second syllable stressed: We must address the crisis). |
| Ramp up | Make sure to pronounce the 'p' clearly before the 'u': ramp-pup. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. There is little sign that relief is coming soon.
2. I have a little money saved, so I can buy lunch.
3. They charged a whopping $50 for parking.
4. The storm raged all night long.
5. I had to scramble to finish my essay before the deadline.
6. The factory ramped up production to meet the high demand.
7. The movie was slammed by critics for being too boring.
8. The population of the city has tripled over the last 20 years.
9. Small businesses are feeling the credit crunch.
10. We expect prices to drop in the near-term.
11. Thank you for addressing this important issue.
12. You need to learn how to curb your anger.

## Practice Prompts

1. Explain the difference in meaning between "I have little time" and "I have a little time."
2. Write a sentence about a company trying to "ramp up" sales.
3. What is a "whopping" amount of money to pay for a cup of coffee?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you criticize someone extremely harshly in public, you _________ them.
2. To control or limit a harmful behavior is to _________ it.
3. A severe shortage of money or time is called a _________.

### Answer Key

1. Slam.
2. Curb.
3. Crunch.

<br/>

# Career and Education Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa uses an article from the Los Angeles Times to discuss why some students from community colleges end up making more money than students from elite universities. The vocabulary focuses on careers, wages, and professional comparisons.

## Key Points

- Career success: *Lucrative*, *out earn*, *propel*, *wage earner*.
- Education terminology: *Community college*, *elite university*, *grad*, *yearlong*.
- Professional standing: *Peers*, *poised to*, *stand to*, *let go of*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph about someone changing careers. Use the phrases "let go of," "lucrative," "peers," and "out earn."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Highly profitable | Lucrative | Producing a large amount of money; highly profitable. | He has a very lucrative business. |
| Make more money | Out earn | To earn more money than someone else. | She out earns all of her male colleagues. |
| Prestigious / top-tier | Elite | Representing the most successful, powerful, or respected group. | Harvard is an elite university. |
| Equals / colleagues | Peers | People who are the same age or have the same social/professional status as you. | He is highly respected by his academic peers. |
| Lasting one year | Yearlong | Lasting for the duration of a full year. | It was a yearlong training program. |
| Ready to / about to | Poised to | Ready, prepared, or about to do something. | She is poised to take on a leadership role. |
| Likely to | Stand to | To be likely to do or get something (usually money or a benefit). | If you invest wisely, you stand to make a profit. |
| Extra work hours | Overtime | Time spent working beyond your normal scheduled hours. | I earned extra money by working overtime. |
| Stop holding on to | Let go of | To stop clinging to a feeling, an idea, or a past event. | You need to let go of your anger. |
| Push forward | Propel | To push or drive someone or something forward. | His ambition propelled him to success. |
| Worker | Wage earner | A person who works for wages/salary, usually to support a family. | He is the primary wage earner in his family. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Rule/Note |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Lucrative | LOO-kruh-tiv | Make sure the 't' is a true 't' sound, not an American flap T (don't say loo-cra-div). |
| Graduate | Noun: GRAD-joo-it | The 'a' at the end of the noun form is reduced to an 'ih' or 'uh' sound. |
| Graduate | Verb: GRAD-joo-ayt | The 'a' at the end of the verb form is a full long 'a' sound. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Real estate can be a very lucrative major.
2. I can't believe he out earns me!
3. That ten-year-old is much taller than his peers.
4. We are planning a yearlong trip around the world.
5. The company is poised to launch its new product.
6. If the deal fails, we stand to lose a lot of money.
7. My boss asked me to work overtime this weekend.
8. He had to let go of his high school dream.
9. Her new degree propelled her to the top 5% of wage earners.
10. The community college grads are out earning many who attended prestigious universities.

## Practice Prompts

1. What does it mean if someone "stands to" make a lot of money?
2. Write a sentence about a job that requires you to work "overtime."
3. Name a career path that is generally considered "lucrative."

## Mini Quiz

1. People who are your exact age or professional level are your _________.
2. If you push someone quickly toward success, you _________ them.
3. If an action takes an entire year, it is a _________ effort.

### Answer Key

1. Peers.
2. Propel.
3. Yearlong.

<br/>

# Real Estate and Housing Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa reads an article from The New York Times discussing the disappearance of the affordable "starter home" in America. You will learn specific vocabulary related to housing, real estate, and government regulations.

## Key Points

- Housing types: *Starter home*, *entry-level*, *no-frills*, *detached house*, *mcmansion*.
- Real estate terms: *Build equity*, *go for*, *in demand*, *booming*.
- Grammar: *Few* (negative/not enough) vs. *A few* (positive/some).
- Action verbs: *Squeeze out*, *ban*, *give a foothold*.

## How to Review

Explain why a young person might struggle to buy a "starter home" today. Use the phrases "squeezed out," "mcmansion," "go for," and "build equity."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| I wonder where X is | Whatever happened to X | Used when wondering about someone/something from the past that you haven't seen lately. | Whatever happened to that actor? |
| Forced out | Squeeze out | To force someone or something out of a situation, usually due to competition/cost. | Small businesses are being squeezed out by large corporations. |
| Most basic / cheapest | Entry-level | The cheapest or most basic version of a product, intended for beginners. | We bought an entry-level car. |
| First house | Starter home | A small, relatively cheap house suitable for people buying their first home. | They bought a starter home in the suburbs. |
| Highly popular | In demand | Greatly desired or sought after by many people. | Nurses are currently in tremendous demand. |
| Sell for (price) | Go for | Used informally to say how much money something costs or sells for. | What do houses in this neighborhood go for? |
| Basic / without luxury | No-frills | Offering basic services or features without any expensive extras. | We flew on a cheap, no-frills airline. |
| An initial opportunity | Foothold | A secure starting position that provides a base for further advancement. | The internship gave him a foothold in the industry. |
| Grow financial value | Build equity | To increase the portion of your home's value that you actually own (value minus debt). | Owning a house allows you to build equity. |
| Growing rapidly | Booming | Experiencing rapid economic growth, success, and prosperity. | The tech industry is absolutely booming right now. |
| Making strict rules | Prescriptive | Giving exact instructions or rules about how things should be done. | The building regulations are very prescriptive. |
| Officially forbid | Ban | To officially prohibit or forbid something. | The school banned the use of cell phones. |
| Stand-alone house | Detached house | A house that does not share a wall with any other building. | They live in a small detached house. |
| Cheap, massive house | Mcmansion | A slang, negative term for a very large, generic, cheaply built house. | Developers are replacing small homes with mcmansions. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Usage / Pronunciation |
| --- | --- |
| What do they go for? | Spoken naturally, it sounds like: Whut-do-they GO-fer? |
| Steep / Steeply | Meaning a sharp angle (a steep hill) OR very expensive (a steep price). |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Whatever happened to Elegance?
2. Entry-level homes have been squeezed out of the market.
3. They started off in a small starter home.
4. That specific model of phone is in high demand.
5. Those shoes go for about two hundred dollars.
6. The scholarship gave him a foothold to start his career.
7. Land costs have risen steeply in booming parts of the country.
8. The new government rules are far more prescriptive.
9. They banned smoking inside the restaurant.
10. The small detached house has all but vanished.

## Practice Prompts

1. What is the difference between "I have a few ideas" and "I have few ideas"?
2. What does it mean if a house is described as "no-frills"?
3. Why does the word "mcmansion" have a negative connotation?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you buy a house to increase your overall wealth, you are trying to build _________.
2. If an area's economy is growing very rapidly, the area is _________.
3. A large house built quickly with low-quality materials is pejoratively called a _________.

### Answer Key

1. Equity.
2. Booming.
3. Mcmansion.

<br/>

# Political and Debate Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa uses an article from The New York Times about a presidential debate to teach highly advanced political and argumentative vocabulary. The language covered is excellent for discussing conflicts, debates, and intense situations.

## Key Points

- Debate and insult: *Hurl*, *invective*, *striking*.
- Political actions: *Retribution*, *give rise to*, *take on*, *step aside*.
- Characterization: *Figure*, *cast as*, *enmity*.
- Pronunciation: Dropping the T after an N (e.g., Atlanta -> "Atlanna").

## How to Review

Write a short paragraph about two rival companies. Use the words "enmity," "hurl," "invective," and "take on."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Throw forcefully / shout | Hurl | To throw something with great force, or to aggressively shout insults. | They hurled insults at each other. |
| Insulting language | Invective | Highly critical, abusive, or insulting language (formal/uncountable). | His speech was filled with invective. |
| Noticeable / shocking | Striking | Attracting attention because it is extreme, unusual, or very noticeable. | The resemblance between the two sisters is striking. |
| Vengeance / punishment | Retribution | Severe punishment inflicted as revenge for a wrong or criminal act. | The victims are demanding retribution. |
| Cause / lead to | Give rise to | To cause something to happen or exist. | His performance gave rise to urgent questions. |
| Challenge / fight | Take on | To challenge someone to a fight, argument, or competition. | I am ready to take you on in a game of chess. |
| Important person | Figure | A person, especially one who is important, notable, or controversial. | He is a controversial figure in politics. |
| Portray as | Cast as | To describe or portray someone in a particular way. | The media cast him as the villain of the story. |
| Intense hatred | Enmity | Deep-seated hatred or intense hostility between enemies. | There is deep enmity between the two groups. |
| Resign / withdraw | Step aside | To withdraw from an important job or position, letting someone else take over. | The CEO decided to step aside for health reasons. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word / Phrase | Correct pronunciation | Rule/Note |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Hurl | HERL | The vowels in hurl, girl, pearl, and curl all make the exact same "er" sound. |
| Atlanta | at-LAN-nuh | When a 't' is between an 'n' and a vowel, Americans often drop the 't' (e.g., in-ner-net for internet). |
| I want to / I want it | I wahn-nuh / I wahn-nit | Dropping the 't' after the 'n' in casual speech. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. They hurled insults and invective in strikingly personal terms.
2. The coach's invective demoralized the entire team.
3. He feared retribution from his political opponents.
4. The heavy rains gave rise to severe flooding.
5. She decided to take on the reigning champion.
6. Shakespeare is an iconic figure in literature.
7. She felt unfairly cast as the troublemaker.
8. The deep enmity on display damaged both of their reputations.
9. They called for the leader to step aside and let someone new take charge.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever known someone to "hurl" insults when they were angry?
2. What does it mean if a politician is asked to "step aside"?
3. Use the phrase "gave rise to" to describe how a mistake caused a big problem.

## Mini Quiz

1. Highly abusive or critical language is called _________.
2. Deep hatred or hostility toward someone is known as _________.
3. If you want to challenge someone in a debate, you are preparing to _________ them _________.

### Answer Key

1. Invective.
2. Enmity.
3. Take (them) on.

<br/>

# AI and the Job Market Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa uses an article from the Los Angeles Times about Artificial Intelligence replacing jobs. You will learn excellent professional vocabulary to describe work tasks, business decisions, and emotional reactions to the changing economy.

## Key Points

- Describing tasks: *Mundane*, *tedious*.
- Action verbs: *Lean on*, *chime in*, *wipe out*, *roll out*.
- Reactions and impact: *Ripple*, *frenzy*, *fuel*, *heightened*.
- Expressions: *Rewrite the rule book*, *in the long run*, *roughly*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph about a new technology replacing an old one. Use the phrases "wipe out," "roll out," "mundane," and "rewrite the rule book."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Boring / ordinary | Mundane | Lacking interest or excitement; dull and ordinary. | Washing dishes can feel like a very mundane task. |
| Boring and long | Tedious | Too long, slow, or dull; tiresome and monotonous. | Copying data by hand is a tedious job. |
| Depend on | Lean on | To depend on or rely on something/someone for support. | I can always lean on my friends for support. |
| Add a comment | Chime in | To join or interrupt a conversation by adding your opinion. | He chimed in with his thoughts during the meeting. |
| Destroy completely | Wipe out | To completely remove, destroy, or eliminate something. | One bad investment wiped out all of his savings. |
| Spread slowly | Ripple | To spread through a group or area slowly, like waves in water. | The news rippled through the company. |
| Panic / excitement | Frenzy | A state or period of uncontrolled excitement or wild behavior. | The holiday sales caused a shopping frenzy. |
| Make stronger | Fuel | To make something (like an emotion or situation) stronger or worse. | His struggles fueled his ambition to succeed. |
| Approximately | Roughly | Approximately; about. | Roughly 30 people came to the meeting. |
| Over a long time | In the long run | Over a long period of time; eventually. | Saving money is smart in the long run. |
| Introduce / launch | Roll out | To officially launch or introduce a new product or service. | The company rolled out a new software program. |
| Change everything | Rewrite the rule book | To completely change the normal, accepted way of doing things. | Her new way of teaching is rewriting the rule book. |
| Increased | Heightened | Made stronger, more intense, or more alert. | The news caused heightened anxiety. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Usage Note / Pronunciation |
| --- | --- |
| Heightened | HY-tend | Make sure the 'h' is strong and the 't' is present. |
| Few vs. A few | "I have few problems" = Good (almost zero). "I have a few problems" = Bad (some problems). |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. AI can easily automate mundane and tedious tasks.
2. The company is leaning heavily on artificial intelligence.
3. If you have an opinion, please feel free to chime in.
4. The storm completely wiped out the small village.
5. The impact of the new law is rippling across multiple sectors.
6. The new video game caused a buying frenzy.
7. High prices are fueling anxiety among workers.
8. The trip will cost roughly five hundred dollars.
9. This decision will benefit us in the long run.
10. The company will roll out the new service next week.
11. AI is really rewriting the rule book on what work looks like.

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe a task you consider "tedious."
2. What does it mean if someone tells you to "chime in"?
3. Name a company that completely "rewrote the rule book" for its industry.

## Mini Quiz

1. To completely destroy or eliminate something is to _________ it _________.
2. If an emotion or rumor spreads slowly through a group, it is _________ through the group.
3. If you make a fire or an emotion stronger, you _________ it.

### Answer Key

1. Wipe (it) out.
2. Rippling.
3. Fuel.

<br/>
