# Job Seeking and Professional Idioms

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa reads an LA Times article about candidates using AI to help them get jobs. Native speakers use specific expressions to talk about careers, trust, and interviews. Learning these will make your business English sound incredibly natural.

## Key Points

- Seeking jobs: *Land a job*, *secure*, *competitive edge*.
- Deception and mistakes: *Fishy*, *embellish*, *misrepresent*, *breach of trust*, *no-no*.
- Outcomes: *Backfire*, *yield*, *rub the wrong way*.
- Dealing with difficulty: *Navigate*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph about a disastrous job interview. Use the words "embellish," "misrepresent," "backfire," and "rub the wrong way."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| An advantage | Competitive edge | An advantage that makes someone more successful than others. | Fluent English will give you a competitive edge. |
| Have the opposite effect | Backfire | When a plan has an unintended, opposite, or negative result. | His attempt to impress the boss completely backfired. |
| Suspicious | Fishy | Seeming suspicious, questionable, or not quite right. | I noticed something fishy about his explanation. |
| Exaggerate | Embellish | To make a story or resume look better by adding untrue details. | He embellished his resume to look more experienced. |
| Annoy unintentionally | Rub the wrong way | To irritate or annoy someone without meaning to. | His arrogance tends to rub people the wrong way. |
| Find a way through | Navigate | To carefully find a way through a difficult or complex situation. | She is trying to navigate the terrible job market. |
| Breaking a promise | Breach of trust | A serious failure to act in a trustworthy or confidential manner. | Stealing company data is a total breach of trust. |
| Unacceptable thing | No-no | Something that is strictly forbidden or highly inappropriate. | Lying on your resume is a big no-no. |
| Get (a job/deal) | Land | To successfully acquire something important, like a job or deal. | She finally landed her dream job. |
| Produce (results) | Yield | To produce or result in something, usually positive. | Hard work will eventually yield success. |
| Succeed in getting | Secure | To successfully obtain or lock down a job, loan, or contract. | He secured a senior role at the bank. |
| Give false info | Misrepresent | To give false or misleading information about yourself or a fact. | You should never misrepresent yourself in an interview. |
| A strong statement | Bold claim | A strong statement that is said with confidence, often lacking proof. | Saying they are the best company in the world is a bold claim. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Phrase | Usage Note |
| --- | --- |
| Big no-no | Used very commonly in both casual and professional settings for something you absolutely should not do. |
| Rub the wrong way | The full idiom is "to rub someone the wrong way." (e.g., He rubbed me the wrong way). |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Learning advanced Excel will give you a competitive edge.
2. Lying on your resume might backfire during the interview.
3. The recruiter noticed something fishy about the applicants.
4. Try not to embellish your work experience.
5. His comment rubbed me the wrong way.
6. Many young professionals are struggling to navigate office politics.
7. Reading answers off a screen is a total breach of trust.
8. Using slang in a formal interview is a big no-no.
9. After applying to 50 places, he finally landed an interview.
10. The new marketing strategy didn't yield the expected growth.
11. She secured an internship at a major law firm.
12. He misrepresented his education on his application.
13. That is a very bold claim to make without any proof.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever had a plan that "backfired"? What happened?
2. What is an example of a "big no-no" at your workplace or school?
3. What is the difference between "lying" and "embellishing"?

## Mini Quiz

1. If a situation seems suspicious and not quite right, it feels _________.
2. To successfully get a job or a deal is to _________ it.
3. If you accidentally annoy someone, you _________ them the _________ _________.

### Answer Key

1. Fishy.
2. Land / Secure.
3. Rub (them the) wrong way.

<br/>

# Corporate Leadership Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa reads an article about Susan Wojcicki, the former CEO of YouTube, stepping down from her position. The article is packed with high-level vocabulary used to describe corporate transitions, struggles, and massive success.

## Key Points

- Career moves: *Step down*, *tenure*, *wear many hats*.
- Describing companies/situations: *Powerhouse*, *amateur*, *moment of truth*, *tough landscape*.
- Difficulties: *Flounder*, *wave of layoffs*, *cost-cutting measures*, *brewing*.
- Communication and strategy: *Elaborate*, *prompt*, *court*.

## How to Review

Write a short paragraph about a company facing difficulty. Use the words "flounder," "step down," "cost-cutting measures," and "wave of layoffs."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Resign / quit | Step down | To resign from an important position or job. | The CEO stepped down after the scandal. |
| Time in a job | Tenure | The period of time someone holds an important position. | During his tenure as president, he changed the laws. |
| Add details | Elaborate | To explain or discuss something in greater detail. | Could you please elaborate on that subject? |
| Cause to act / reason | Prompt | To cause someone to make a decision or take an action. | What prompted you to say that to me? |
| Developing (usually bad) | Brewing | Used to describe a difficult or unpleasant situation that is slowly developing. | I think trouble is brewing at the office. |
| Beginner / not pro | Amateur | Someone who does something as a hobby, not a professional. | YouTube started as an amateur video site. |
| Extremely strong group | Powerhouse | A person, group, or organization with great energy, strength, or power. | The city has become an economic powerhouse. |
| Crucial test | Moment of truth | A critical moment when someone/something is tested and a decision must be made. | Finally, the moment of truth has arrived. |
| Struggle severely | Flounder | To struggle clumsily or to be in serious difficulty (business/personal). | The economy is floundering due to the recession. |
| Many firings at once | Wave of layoffs | When a company fires a large number of employees at the same time. | The tech industry is experiencing a wave of layoffs. |
| Actions to save money | Cost-cutting measures | Specific plans or actions taken by a company to reduce its expenses. | They implemented cost-cutting measures to survive. |
| Difficult situation | Tough landscape | A difficult general situation or environment (e.g., economic landscape). | Startups are navigating a tough macroeconomic landscape. |
| Try to attract | Court | To try hard to please or attract someone because you want something from them. | TikTok is courting a youthful audience. |
| Have many jobs/roles | Wear many hats | To have many different roles, jobs, or responsibilities within an organization. | Working at a startup means you have to wear many hats. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Amateur | AM-uh-cher | Do not pronounce the "teur" as "toor". It is a "cher" sound. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The president is stepping down after 16 years.
2. During her tenure, she held multiple positions.
3. The representatives declined to elaborate on the issue.
4. What prompted her sudden departure?
5. There is a storm brewing between the two companies.
6. We can't take him seriously; he's just an amateur.
7. That company is a massive advertising powerhouse.
8. This is the moment of truth for the struggling business.
9. His marriage was floundering despite his financial success.
10. The company announced a new wave of layoffs.
11. While I understand what you're saying, I disagree.
12. The politician was skilled at courting the media.
13. Over the years, she has worn many hats at the company.

## Practice Prompts

1. What does it mean if an employee is asked to "wear many hats"?
2. Use the word "amateur" in a sentence about sports or art.
3. What is the difference between a company that is a "powerhouse" and one that is "floundering"?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you resign from your position as CEO, you _________ _________.
2. A critical time when a major test or decision occurs is the _________ of _________.
3. If trouble is slowly developing in secret, we say trouble is _________.

### Answer Key

1. Step down.
2. Moment (of) truth.
3. Brewing.

# Hybrid Work and Office Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

This lesson uses a New York Times article about the struggle companies face when asking employees to return to the office (RTO) after the pandemic. Lisa explains excellent vocabulary related to business logic, confusing situations, and office culture.

## Key Points

- Business concepts: *Bottom line*, *hybrid work*, *bulk*, *scale back*.
- Confusion and difficulty: *Conundrum*, *gibberish*, *daunting*, *lurch*.
- Social interaction: *RSVP*, *banter*, *pants that don't button*.
- Beliefs and truths: *Gospel*, *outweigh*, *set in*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph about a difficult business problem. Use the words "conundrum," "bottom line," "scale back," and "outweigh."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Confusing problem | Conundrum | A confusing, difficult problem or question. | We haven't found a way to resolve this conundrum. |
| Please reply | RSVP | A request to reply to an invitation (from French: Répondez s'il vous plaît). | Please RSVP to the wedding by Friday. |
| Mixed location work | Hybrid work | A working arrangement where employees split their time between the office and home. | Hybrid work has become very popular since the pandemic. |
| All over the country | Nationwide | Happening or existing in all parts of the country. | Police have launched a nationwide search. |
| Profit / total income | Bottom line | The final profit or loss a company makes; the most important factor. | That decision will have a big effect on the bottom line. |
| Sudden increase | Surge | A sudden and powerful upward movement or increase. | Oil prices have surged recently. |
| Move irregularly | Lurch | To make a sudden, unsteady, or irregular movement backward, forward, or sideways. | The train suddenly lurched forward. |
| Reduce | Scale back | To reduce the size, amount, or extent of something. | We need to scale back on our spending. |
| The majority | Bulk | The majority or greater part of something. | The bulk of the work has already been completed. |
| Friendly joking | Banter | Friendly, teasing conversation or joking around with colleagues/friends. | I enjoy bantering with my coworkers during lunch. |
| Absolute truth | Gospel | Something believed to be absolutely true and unquestionable. | Don't take his word as gospel. |
| Meaningless nonsense | Gibberish | Meaningless, confusing nonsense; words that don't make sense. | This computer manual is written in complete gibberish. |
| Intimidating | Daunting | Frightening in a way that makes you feel less confident; intimidating. | The job interview process can be daunting. |
| Begin and stay | Set in | When something (often unpleasant) begins and seems likely to continue. | The rainy season has finally set in. |
| Be more important than | Outweigh | To be greater or more important than something else. | My desire for success outweighs my fear of failure. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Usage Note |
| --- | --- |
| Complex | Noun: COM-plex (I live in an apartment complex). Adjective: com-PLEX (This is a complex problem). |
| Banter | Can be used as a noun (cafeteria banter) or a verb (we bantered). |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The executives are facing a difficult conundrum.
2. It's hard to plan a party if everyone RSVPs "maybe."
3. The new software will definitely improve our bottom line.
4. COVID cases surged in the winter.
5. The company is scaling its office requirements back to two days.
6. The bulk of the population lives in cities.
7. I miss the cafeteria banter with my friends.
8. Two days working from home means wearing pants that don't button.
9. You shouldn't treat his opinions like gospel.
10. After a while, I stopped listening to his gibberish.
11. Starting a whole new career can feel very daunting.
12. A sense of comfort has set in for employees working from home.
13. We believe the benefits far outweigh the negatives.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever had to "scale back" your budget? What did you cut?
2. Use the word "gibberish" to describe someone speaking a language you don't know.
3. What is an example of a "daunting" task?

## Mini Quiz

1. The total profit a company makes is known as its _________ _________.
2. If you joke around in a friendly way with your coworkers, you are _________.
3. A difficult, confusing problem is called a _________.

### Answer Key

1. Bottom line.
2. Bantering.
3. Conundrum.

# Corporate Struggles and Economics Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa covers a Los Angeles Times article about Netflix losing subscribers and cutting jobs. The vocabulary focuses on financial losses, employee anxiety, and sudden negative changes in a corporate environment.

## Key Points

- Loss and dropping: *Shed*, *plummet*, *cut*, *steep slide*.
- Anxiety and negativity: *Bleak*, *stir*, *angst*, *uncertainty*.
- Corporate terms: *Rank and file*, *heavyweight*, *severance*, *undisclosed*.
- Surprises and trends: *Curveball*, *skyrocket*, *grow complacent*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph describing a company losing money. Use the words "plummet," "shed," "bleak," and "severance."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Get rid of / drop | Shed | To lose, drop, or get rid of something (like jobs, weight, or hair). | The company is shedding a large number of jobs. |
| Drop rapidly | Plummet | To decrease sharply and very quickly. | The stock market plummeted to its lowest point. |
| Depressing / hopeless | Bleak | Not hopeful, encouraging, or promising; depressing. | The medical prognosis was bleak. |
| Mix / cause | Stir | To mix something, or to cause a movement, change, or emotion. | The news stirred a mix of anger and confusion. |
| Anxiety | Angst | Deep anxiety, worry, or dread. | I could hear the angst in your voice. |
| Not sure | Uncertainty | The state of being doubtful or not knowing what will happen. | The future is full of uncertainty. |
| Ordinary workers | Rank and file | The ordinary workers in an organization, excluding the leaders/managers. | The rank and file workers are worried about layoffs. |
| Major leader/player | Heavyweight | A very important, successful, or influential person/company. | Netflix is a heavyweight in the streaming industry. |
| Become lazy/satisfied | Grow complacent | To become so satisfied with your situation that you stop trying to improve. | They grew complacent after their massive success. |
| Rise rapidly | Skyrocket | To increase very quickly by a large amount (prices, numbers). | Housing prices have skyrocketed recently. |
| Most important | Overriding | Dominant; more important than any other considerations. | Our overriding concern is customer safety. |
| Secret / unrevealed | Undisclosed | Information kept secret; not made known publicly. | The company was sold for an undisclosed amount. |
| Unexpected surprise | Curveball | Something highly unexpected or disruptive that surprises you. | Life really threw me a curveball this year. |
| Make worse/stronger | Fuel | To make a situation, emotion, or argument more intense. | His angry words fueled the argument further. |
| Sharp / expensive | Steep | Set at a sharp angle (steep hill), or unreasonably high/expensive (steep price). | There was a steep decline in the stock's value. |
| Pay given when fired | Severance | Money paid to an employee when they are let go from a company without cause. | She received two months of severance pay. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation | Common mistake |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Decade | DECK-ayd | Do not stress the second syllable. It is not de-CAYD (that means rotten). |
| Stir vs. Steer | STUR vs. STEER | Stir (mix) has an 'er' sound. Steer (drive) has an 'ee' sound. |
| Certainty | SER-tin-tee | The 't' in the middle is often held, and the 'a' is reduced to an 'ih'. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The dog is shedding hair all over the couch.
2. His reputation plummeted after he was accused of the crime.
3. The future looks bleak for that specific industry.
4. The bad numbers stirred a mix of angst and uncertainty.
5. The rank and file workers deserve better pay.
6. He is a political heavyweight in the local government.
7. We must not grow complacent about the quality of our product.
8. The unemployment rate has skyrocketed.
9. It is of overriding importance to finish the project today.
10. They met at an undisclosed location.
11. When life throws you a curveball, you can't give up.
12. The steep drop in sales fueled anxiety among the staff.
13. They were told it was their last day and they would receive severance.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever been "thrown a curveball" in life? What happened?
2. What does it mean if a company pays an employee "severance"?
3. Use the word "plummet" in a sentence about the temperature in winter.

## Mini Quiz

1. To decrease sharply and rapidly is to _________.
2. The ordinary workers at a company (not the managers) are called the _________ and _________.
3. An unexpected, disruptive surprise is called a _________.

### Answer Key

1. Plummet.
2. Rank and file.
3. Curveball.

# Emotional Resilience Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa talks to Joanne, a psychotherapist in Los Angeles, about dealing with rejection, criticism, and emotional struggles. You will learn excellent expressions to talk about emotional strength, personal identity, and handling difficult times.

## Key Points

- Expressions of similarity: *Along the same lines*.
- Popular phrases: *Catchphrase*.
- Emotional strength: *Grounded*, *rooted*, *sense of oneself*.
- Dealing with difficulty: *Face storms*, *knock down*, *thrive*, *emotional landscape*.
- Interactions/Roles: *Look the part*, *caregivers*, *look the other way*, *matter*.

## How to Review

Write a short paragraph about how you stay emotionally strong during difficult times. Try to use the words "grounded," "rooted," "face storms," and "thrive."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Similarly / on that topic | Along the same lines | In a similar way, or continuing on the same topic of conversation. | Along the same lines, how have you changed recently? |
| To do very well | Thrive | To be happy, successful, and to grow or develop well. | He is really thriving in his new job. |
| Emotional environment | Emotional landscape | The general emotional state or situation of a specific environment. | The emotional landscape of the entertainment industry is brutal. |
| Popular saying | Catchphrase | A short, popular phrase that you hear people say very often. | "Time is money" is a common catchphrase. |
| Emotionally stable | Grounded | Well-balanced, practical, and in control of your emotions. | My family keeps me very grounded despite my success. |
| Appear suitable | Look the part | To have an appearance that is appropriate for a specific role or job. | He didn't get the acting job because he didn't look the part. |
| Deeply established | Rooted | Established deeply and firmly; standing strong like a tree. | Even on a hard day, I remain deeply rooted in who I am. |
| Cause to fall | Knock down | When something hits someone or something and causes it to fall. | The wind was so strong it almost knocked me down. |
| Very important | Critical | Extremely important or essential. | It is absolutely critical that you learn this skill. |
| Deal with difficulties | Face storms | To confront and deal with difficult times or challenges in life. | People facing storms need to have strong relationships. |
| Knowing who you are | Sense of oneself | Knowing your own character, strengths, weaknesses, and identity. | She has a very strong sense of herself. |
| At the same time | At once | Simultaneously; happening at the exact same time. | You can't be in two places at once. |
| People who take care | Caregivers | People (parents, nurses, etc.) who take care of children or the sick. | His grandmother was his primary caregiver. |
| Ignore a bad thing | Look the other way | To ignore what is happening and choose not to help or take action. | When I asked for help, everybody looked the other way. |
| Be important | Matter | To be important, to have meaning, or to have an effect. | Your opinion matters to me. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Usage Note / Pronunciation |
| --- | --- |
| At once | Can mean "immediately" (Do it at once!) or "simultaneously" (Two things at once). |
| Grounded | GROWN-did (Used to describe someone who is mentally and emotionally stable). |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Let's ask another question along the same lines.
2. She takes unique emotional skills to thrive in this industry.
3. "You have to know yourself" is a popular catchphrase.
4. Despite his huge success, he remains surprisingly grounded.
5. If you want to work at the law firm, you have to dress the part.
6. This was a hard day, but I am rooted.
7. It doesn't matter how fast the wind blows; it will not knock me down.
8. Changing our energy sources is absolutely critical.
9. Everyone will face storms at some point in their life.
10. He has a very good sense of himself.
11. The computer can do many things at once.
12. When he was a child, his grandmother was his caregiver.
13. When the crime was committed, the authorities looked the other way.
14. What you say and what you do in the world matters.

## Practice Prompts

1. Who is someone in your life who keeps you "grounded"?
2. What is a common "catchphrase" in your native language or in English?
3. Describe a time you had to "face a storm." What helped you get through it?

## Mini Quiz

1. If you are emotionally stable and practical, you are a _________ person.
2. To ignore a bad situation instead of helping is to _________ the _________ _________.
3. If you want to change the topic to something similar, you can say, "_________ the _________ _________..."

### Answer Key

1. Grounded.
2. Look (the) other way.
3. Along (the) same lines.

# Wall Street Journal Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa reviews recent headlines from the Wall Street Journal. She explains advanced verbs, nouns, and expressions related to politics, international business, loyalty, and economics.

## Key Points

- Suggesting ideas: *Float an idea*.
- Utilizing connections: *Work your ties*, *leverage a bond*, *go back decades*.
- Protecting: *Spare*, *fend off*.
- Loyalty and support: *Check in on someone*, *stick with someone through thick and thin*.
- Urgent actions: *Scramble*, *loom*.

## How to Review

Write a paragraph describing a business partnership. Try to use the expressions "go back decades," "through thick and thin," and "leverage that bond."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Suggest an idea | Float (an idea) | To suggest or propose a plan/idea to see if people accept it. | The manager floated a new plan to restructure the department. |
| Use connections | Work (one's) ties | To actively use your relationships or connections to achieve a goal. | He is working his ties in the industry to find a new job. |
| Save from harm | Spare | To protect or save someone/something from harm or negative impacts. | The government intervened to spare the town from flooding. |
| Perfect combination | Match made in heaven | Two people or things that are perfectly suited for each other. | You and your fiancé are a match made in heaven. |
| Know for a long time | Go back decades | To have known someone or existed for many years/decades. | My best friend and I go back decades. |
| Use a relationship | Leverage (a bond) | To use a strong relationship or connection to gain an advantage. | She leveraged her friendship with the CEO to get a promotion. |
| Defend against | Fend off | To defend yourself against an attack, threat, or something annoying. | The country is trying to fend off economic sanctions. |
| See how someone is | Check in on (someone) | To contact someone to see how they are doing and offer support. | I check in on my elderly neighbor every day. |
| A powerful person | Titan / Heavyweight | A person or organization with great strength, power, or importance. | He is a titan of the luxury goods industry. |
| Remain loyal | Stick with (someone) | To remain loyal, supportive, or committed to someone/something. | I will stick with my workout routine. |
| Through good and bad | Through thick and thin | To stay with someone through good times and very difficult times. | She stood by me through thick and thin. |
| Rush urgently | Scramble | To move or act quickly and urgently to get something done. | We were scrambling to book a flight after ours was canceled. |
| Threaten to happen | Loom | When something threatening, important, or bad is coming close. | The deadline is looming and I'm feeling stressed. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct pronunciation |
| --- | --- |
| Pharmaceuticals | far-muh-SOO-ti-kuhls (Stress the "SOO"). |
| Aluminum (US) | uh-LOO-mi-num (British: al-yoo-MIN-ee-um). |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The scientists floated a theory about the origins of the virus.
2. She worked her ties in the media to get good publicity.
3. She wore sunscreen to spare her skin from the sun.
4. Fried chicken and dill pickles are a match made in heaven.
5. The family goes back generations in this town.
6. He tried to fend off the uncomfortable questions.
7. I wanted to check in on you after your big presentation.
8. My friends and I support each other through thick and thin.
9. Fans scrambled to buy tickets as soon as they went on sale.
10. The threat of a recession continues to loom.

## Practice Prompts

1. Who is someone you have "stuck with through thick and thin"?
2. What is an idea you recently "floated" at work or to a friend?
3. Describe a time you had to "scramble" to finish something quickly.

## Mini Quiz

1. If you suggest a new plan or idea for consideration, you _________ an idea.
2. To remain loyal to someone during good times and bad times is to stay with them _________ _________ and _________.
3. If an uncomfortable deadline is getting very close, the deadline is _________.

### Answer Key

1. Float.
2. Through thick (and) thin.
3. Looming.

description: "Master the difference between the tense 'ee' and neutral 'ih' vowel sounds in American English."
category: "english-pronunciation"
theme: "vowels"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"

---

# Tense vs. Neutral Vowels (EE vs. IH)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa teaches the difference between two critical English vowel sounds: the tense "ee" (as in *feel*, *seat*, *eat*) and the neutral "ih" (as in *fill*, *sit*, *it*). Mixing these up can lead to embarrassing mistakes. The difference is not just length; it's about tension in the lips and tongue.

## Key Rules

1. **Tense Vowel (EE)**: 
   - Lips are slightly wide and tense. Tongue is high and tense in the back of the mouth.
   - Example: *Eat, feel, seat*.
   - Spelling hint: Usually spelled with two vowels (EE, EA, IE) like in *see, please, believe*.

2. **Neutral Vowel (IH)**: 
   - Lips and tongue are completely relaxed and neutral.
   - Example: *It, fill, sit*.
   - Spelling hint: Usually spelled with a single letter "I" like in *if, this, different*.

3. **The Big Exception**: 
   - The word "been" (b-e-e-n) is spelled with two vowels, but in American English, it is pronounced with the neutral, relaxed vowel "ih" (bin).

## Pronunciation Practice

| Tense Vowel (EE) | Neutral Vowel (IH) |
| --- | --- |
| Eat | It |
| Beat | Bit |
| Feel | Fill |
| Seat | Sit |
| Leave | Live |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Can you feel it?
2. Did you hit it?
3. He doesn't want to leave.
4. He doesn't want to live.
5. How have you been? (Pronounced "bin" in American English).

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice saying these pairs back to back: Heel/Hill, Sheep/Ship, Sleep/Slip. Focus on relaxing your tongue and lips completely for the second word.
2. Notice how your fingers under your jaw move slightly when you say the tense "EE" sound, but stay still when you say the relaxed "IH" sound.

<br/>

# The T + N Sound in American English

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

This is an advanced American pronunciation lesson. Lisa teaches how Americans pronounce words that end with a T followed immediately by an N sound (like *written*, *cotton*, *button*). The vowel between them disappears, and the 'T' is held without releasing the tongue.

## Key Rules

When a word has a T and an N (with an unstressed vowel between them that disappears):
1. Place your tongue up to the roof of your mouth to make the 'T' sound.
2. **Do not release your tongue** to let the air out of your mouth.
3. Instead, hold your tongue in place and release the air forcefully through your **nose** to make the 'N' sound.
4. There is a sudden, abrupt stop of air between the two sounds.

## Pronunciation Practice

| Word | Pronunciation Notes |
| --- | --- |
| Written | wri- (stop) -n |
| Cotton | co- (stop) -n |
| Button | bu- (stop) -n |
| Kitten | ki- (stop) -n |
| Mountain | moun- (stop) -n |
| Curtain | cur- (stop) -n |
| Clinton | clin- (stop) -n |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. My cotton shirt has buttons.
2. I have forgotten when the book was written.
3. I am certain that's the tallest mountain.

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice holding your tongue against the roof of your mouth for the 'T' and simply shifting the airflow to your nose for the 'N'.
2. Be careful not to say "moun-TIN" or "cot-TON" (which sounds more British or overly enunciated). The transition should be an abrupt stop in the throat/mouth.

<br/>

category: "english-writing"

# American Politics and Pronunciation

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-writing

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa uses vocabulary related to US Presidential elections to teach both American political concepts and advanced pronunciation rules. You will learn about political parties, how to pronounce politicians' names, and the different vowel sounds for the letter 'O'.

## Key Points

- Political Parties: *Democrats* (donkey symbol), *Republicans* (elephant symbol).
- Election Geography: *Blue states* (Democrat), *Red states* (Republican), *Swing states / battleground states* (Undecided).
- Taking office: *Hold office*, *sworn into office*, *oath of office*.
- Acronyms: *POTUS*, *FLOTUS*, *SCOTUS*, *GOP*.
- The letter O: Can be pronounced as "oh" (*hold*), "ah" (*office*, *Donald*), or "uh" (*done*).

## How to Review

Write a paragraph about an election process. Try to use the terms "hold office," "sworn in," "voter turnout," and "swing states."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Undecided state | Swing state / Battleground state | A US state where both major political parties have similar levels of support. | Florida is a very important swing state. |
| Have a govt job | Hold office / Hold public office | To have a large and powerful job, especially in government. | Some people think he is too old to hold office. |
| Take the oath | Sworn into office | To officially start a government job by taking a formal promise (oath). | The president is sworn into office on January 20th. |
| President | POTUS | Acronym: President of the United States. | POTUS is expected to be in attendance. |
| President's wife | FLOTUS | Acronym: First Lady of the United States. | FLOTUS will give a speech today. |
| Top US court | SCOTUS | Acronym: Supreme Court of the United States. | SCOTUS will make a decision next week. |
| % who vote | Voter turnout | The percentage of eligible voters who actually cast a vote. | The voter turnout was very high this year. |
| Republican Party | GOP | Acronym: Grand Old Party (traditional nickname for the Republicans). | He is the GOP candidate for the election. |
| Presidency / Office | The Oval Office | The president's physical office, or a term meaning the presidency itself. | They will compete for the Oval Office. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Usage Note / Pronunciation |
| --- | --- |
| Democrat / Democracy | DEM-uh-crat (stress 1st). De-MOC-ra-cy (stress 2nd). Dem-o-CRAT-ic (stress 3rd). |
| Biden | BY-din (Hold the 'd', reduce the 'e', don't say by-DEN. Similar to *hidden*, *written*, *sudden*). |
| Donald / Ronald | DAH-nild / RAH-nild (The 'o' is an 'ah' sound. The 'a' is reduced to 'ih' or 'uh'). |
| Hold / Office | H-OH-ld (round lips). AH-ffice (open mouth, 'ah' sound). |
| The Oval | Thee-Oval (Use "thee" because the next word starts with a vowel. Reduce the 'a' in oval). |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. The red states are likely to vote for the Republican candidate.
2. He held public office for eight years.
3. When will the new prime minister be sworn into office?
4. Every 1 percent increase in voter turnout matters.
5. Democrats oppose the GOP stimulus plan.
6. The next occupant of the Oval Office will deal with that issue.
7. Have you ever ridden a horse?
8. The document is hidden from view.

## Practice Prompts

1. Explain the difference between a "red state," a "blue state," and a "swing state."
2. Practice pronouncing "Donald" and "Ronald" using the "AH" sound for the O, and reducing the A at the end.
3. What does "POTUS" stand for?

## Mini Quiz

1. The percentage of eligible people who actually vote is called the _________ _________.
2. When a president formally takes their position, they are _________ _________ office.
3. "GOP" stands for _________ _________ _________.

### Answer Key

1. Voter turnout.
2. Sworn into.
3. Grand Old Party.

# Real-Life Idioms and the Fast 'D' Sound

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-speaking

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa talks to a man at a car wash in Los Angeles who owns an 18-year-old parrot named Buster. Through their natural conversation, you will learn several common idiomatic expressions and practice the American "fast D" (or flap T) sound that connects words together smoothly.

## Key Points

- Making choices: *Pick and choose your battles*, *take it from there*.
- Replacements: *Trade in*.
- Exceptions: *Everything but*, *other than that*.
- Existence/Time: *Be around*, *go way past*.
- Price ranges: *Start at about*, *go all the way up to*.
- Pronunciation: The "fast D" sound (e.g., *a lot easier* sounds like *a lod easier*).

## How to Review

Write a short paragraph about buying a new phone. Try to use the expressions "trade in," "start at about," and "go all the way up to."

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Choose what to fight | Pick (your) battles | To choose which things are worth arguing about and which are not. | You shouldn't argue over every little thing; learn to pick your battles. |
| Exchange old for new | Trade in | To give something old (like a car or phone) to get a discount on something new. | I traded in my old phone to buy a new iPhone. |
| Except for | Everything but | All things except for a specific item. | He eats everything but broccoli. |
| Except for that | Other than that | Except for the thing just mentioned. | He talks a lot, but other than that, he's a great student. |
| Be alive / be present | Be around | To be present in a place, or to still be alive. | I won't be around tomorrow. (or) Too bad my grandfather wasn't around to see me graduate. |
| Continue much longer | Go way past | To continue existing or happening long after someone/something else is gone. | The parrot will outlive me; he'll go way past me. |
| Very much / far | Way | Used to emphasize an adjective or adverb (much more). | His English is way better than mine. |
| Decide the next step | Take it from there | To do the first step and then decide what to do next based on the result. | Let's find out how much the repairs cost, and we'll take it from there. |
| Lowest price | Start at about | The minimum or starting price of an item or service. | The lessons start at about twenty dollars an hour. |
| Maximum price | Go all the way up to | The maximum limit or highest price. | The prices go all the way up to $150 an hour. |

## Pronunciation Guide: The "Fast D" (Flap T)

When an American 'T' is between two vowel sounds, it often sounds like a fast 'D' (or a lightly rolled 'R' in some languages). 
Notice how native speakers link these words together using this sound:

| Written | Pronounced as |
| --- | --- |
| Shut up | shud-up |
| A lot easier | a lod-easier |
| Cat or dog | cad-er dog |
| Get another | ged-another |
| Eating | ea-ding |
| Started about | star-did-about |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. He doesn't say "shut up" immediately; he picks and chooses his battles.
2. The advantage of a parrot is that there is no trading in.
3. His diet is everything but avocado, chocolate, alcohol, and onions.
4. He can't eat chocolate. Other than that, he eats whatever he feels like.
5. Will you be around tomorrow?
6. I won't be around; he'll live way past me.
7. You should find a good breeder and take it from there.
8. The prices start at about $4,000 and go all the way up to $15,000.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever "traded in" an electronic device or a car?
2. Practice saying the following phrase smoothly using the fast D sound: "A lot of people get another cat or dog."
3. Describe a restaurant's prices using "start at about" and "go all the way up to."

## Mini Quiz

1. If you give the store your old car to buy a new one, you _________ it _________.
2. If you decide the next steps after finishing the first step, you say, "We'll _________ it _________ _________."
3. If you want to say someone is much older than someone else, you can say they are _________ older.

### Answer Key

1. Trade (it) in.
2. Take it from there.
3. Way.

<br/>

# The Professional "What Factor"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-speaking

## Summary

In this interview, Lisa speaks with Frank, an Adobe employee from Spain working in "Silicon Beach" (Los Angeles). They discuss how improving pronunciation and word stress is critical for career advancement, specifically for reaching management or director-level positions.

## Key Points

- **The "What Factor"**: The frequency with which native speakers say "What?" or ask you to repeat yourself because they didn't understand your pronunciation.
- **Word Stress vs. Syllable Timing**: Many languages (like Spanish) pronounce words syllable by syllable with equal weight. English requires stressing important words and reducing unimportant words.
- **Career Growth**: Being a manager or director is entirely about communication. If you cannot smoothly express complex ideas, your career options will be limited.
- **Practice Tip**: Don't just read; listen to the melody of English. Connect words rather than speaking "word by word."

## How to Review

Reflect on your own "What Factor." How often do people ask you to repeat yourself? Practice recording yourself reading a paragraph, focusing specifically on linking words together rather than reading word-by-word.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Ambitious | Driven | Highly motivated and determined to succeed. | He is a very driven and ambitious young professional. |
| Make me repeat | The "What" factor | A casual term for how often people say "What?" to you because of your accent. | My goal is to reduce the "what factor" at work. |
| Emphasis | Word stress | Making certain syllables or words louder, longer, and higher in pitch. | Spanish speakers struggle with English word stress. |

## Pronunciation Focus: Linking and Stress

Frank demonstrated how Spanish speakers naturally read an English sentence versus how an American reads it.

**Incorrect (Word by Word / Robotic):**
"I... love... work-ing... for... A-do-be."

**Correct (Linked and Stressed):**
"I **love** working for A**do**be." (The words flow together smoothly, with emphasis on "love" and the middle syllable of "Adobe").

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. One of my goals is to reduce the "what factor."
2. As soon as you want to explain something complicated, it becomes much harder.
3. Being a manager is all about communication.
4. I love working there.

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying: "I love working for this company." Listen to see if you pause between every word, or if it flows like a single song.
2. Why does Frank say pronunciation is essential for becoming a manager?

# 10 Commonly Mispronounced Words

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa teaches the correct American English pronunciation of 10 words that even native speakers sometimes mispronounce. Using the correct pronunciation ensures you sound educated and professional in business settings.

## Key Pronunciation Corrections

1. **Silicone vs. Silicon**
   - *Silicone* (SILL-ih-cone): A liquid rubber-like material used for sealing. "I used silicone to seal the sink."
   - *Silicon* (SILL-ih-con): A chemical used in computer chips. "He got a job in Silicon Valley." (Don't say Sili-cone Valley).

2. **Mischievous**
   - Correct: MIS-chuh-vus.
   - Incorrect: mis-CHEE-vee-us.
   - Meaning: Behaving badly but usually in an innocent way (often used for children).

3. **Et cetera (etc.)**
   - Correct: et-SET-er-uh.
   - Incorrect: ek-SET-er-uh (There is no "X" sound).

4. **Nuptial / Prenuptial**
   - Correct: NUP-shul.
   - Meaning: Related to marriage or weddings. "They signed a prenuptial agreement."

5. **Pronunciation**
   - Correct: pro-NUN-see-a-shun.
   - Incorrect: pro-NOUN-see-a-shun. (The verb is *pronounce*, but the noun is *pronunciation*).

6. **Parenthesis vs. Parentheses**
   - Singular: pa-REN-the-sis (Ends with an "is" sound).
   - Plural: pa-REN-the-sees (Ends with an "eez" sound). "I put the sentence in parentheses."

7. **Strengths**
   - Correct: STRENGTHS (You must pronounce the 'G' in the back of your throat).
   - Incorrect: STRENTHS (Skipping the G). "Tell me about your strengths."

8. **Jewelry**
   - Correct: JOOL-ree.
   - Incorrect: JOOL-er-ee.

9. **Anyway**
   - Correct: Anyway. (Never add an "s" to the end).
   - Incorrect: Anyways. "I didn't want to go, but I went anyway."

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. He got a job with a tech company in Silicon Valley.
2. The mischievous boys were disciplined by their parents.
3. I need to buy paper, a pen, a pencil, etc.
4. Many people watched the royal nuptials on television.
5. I would like to improve my English pronunciation.
6. Tell me about your three greatest strengths.
7. She is wearing beautiful jewelry.
8. I didn't want to go, but I went anyway.

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice saying: "Etc." Make sure you start with an "E-T" sound, not an "E-X" sound.
2. Practice the word "Strengths." Make sure you feel the back of your tongue hit the roof of your mouth for the 'G' sound before saying the 'TH' and 'S'.

# Recommended Grammar Books for Advanced Learners

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-grammar

## Summary

Even advanced, fluent speakers often struggle with specific grammar rules like prepositions, articles (a, an, the), and gerunds vs. infinitives. In this video, Lisa recommends four books she uses with her private students to fix these final, persistent mistakes.

## Key Grammar Challenges

1. **Prepositions (in, on, at, for, by, to)**
   - Example: "Her birthday is *in* April" vs. "Her birthday is *on* April 10th."
2. **Articles (a, an, the)**
   - Example: "It was *last* week" (one week ago) vs. "It was *the last* week" (the final week).
   - Example: "I have *a few* friends" (positive: several) vs. "I have *few* friends" (negative: not enough).
3. **Gerunds vs. Infinitives (doing vs. to do)**
   - Example: "I can't afford *to buy* it" (Correct) vs. "I can't afford *buying* it" (Incorrect).
   - Example: "I can't resist *eating* it" (Correct) vs. "I can't resist *to eat* it" (Incorrect).

## Recommended Books

1. **The Ins and Outs of Prepositions**
   - Best for: Mastering difficult preposition rules (in, on, at, etc.).
2. **Three Little Words**
   - Best for: A small, quick reference book for learning the rules of articles (a, an, the) with immediate answers at the bottom of the page.
3. **The Article Book**
   - Best for: Deep practice specifically focused on articles.
4. **Understanding and Using English Grammar (by Betty Azar)**
   - Best for: General advanced grammar, specifically verb tenses, gerunds, and infinitives. Highly recommended to buy with the accompanying workbook.

## How to Review

If you struggle with "in/on/at" or "a/the", consider looking up the books Lisa recommended. As a practice exercise right now, write down the difference between "I have a few friends" and "I have few friends."

# British vs. American Accent: 5 Common Words

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa explains the pronunciation differences between American and British English for five very common words. You can choose which pronunciation to use depending on the accent you want to develop. 

## The 5 Words

| Word | American Pronunciation | British Pronunciation | Notes |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| **Again** | a-GEN (rhymes with ten) | a-GAYN (rhymes with rain) | "It's nice to see you a-gen." |
| **Against** | a-GENST | a-GAYNST | "Are you for it or a-genst it?" |
| **Mobile** | MO-bul (rhymes with local) | MO-bile (rhymes with smile) | Americans usually say "cell phone," not mobile phone. |
| **Herb** | ERB (Silent 'H') | HERB (Pronounce the 'H') | "She grows erbs in her garden." |
| **Schedule** | SKE-jool (SK sound) | SHE-jool (SH sound) | "I ske-jooled it for next week." |

## Additional Word Patterns

The American pronunciation of *mobile* (-bul instead of -bile) applies to other words as well:
- **Missile**: American (MISS-ul) vs. British (MISS-ile)
- **Versatile**: American (VER-suh-tul) vs. British (VER-suh-tile)
- **Fertile**: American (FER-tul) vs. British (FER-tile)

## Good Sentences To Imitate (American Accent)

1. He said it again and again. (a-gen)
2. The teacher is against us using cell phones in the classroom. (a-genst)
3. Are you for it or are you against it?
4. He lives in a small mobile home. (mo-bul)
5. She grows herbs in her garden. (erbs)
6. I scheduled it for next week. (ske-jooled)

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying the 6 sentences above using the American pronunciation. Pay special attention to the silent 'H' in herb, and the 'SK' sound in schedule.
2. In your country, do schools generally teach American or British pronunciation?

---

# Common Workplace Vocabulary Mistakes

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa addresses three common vocabulary and pronunciation mistakes that foreign-born professionals often make in the American workplace. Making these mistakes can cause confusion or make you sound less professional.

## Key Distinctions

### 1. Advice vs. Advise
- **Advice** (Noun): Ends with an 'S' sound. The 'I' is short.
  - *Rule*: It is uncountable! NEVER say "advices." If you need to count it, say "two pieces of advice" or "some advice."
  - *Example*: "Thank you for your excellent advice."
- **Advise** (Verb): Ends with a 'Z' sound. The 'I' is long.
  - *Example*: "I strongly advise you not to argue with your boss."

### 2. Moral vs. Morale
- **Moral** (Noun/Adjective): Stresses the first syllable (MOR-ul). Relates to right vs. wrong.
  - *Example*: "It goes against his morals to participate in that activity."
- **Morale** (Noun): Stresses the second syllable (muh-RAL). Relates to the general mood or enthusiasm of a group.
  - *Example*: "Recent changes in management have increased employee morale."

### 3. Career vs. Carrier
- **Career** (Noun): Stresses the second syllable (cuh-REER). Relates to your profession.
  - *Example*: "I am fortunate that I have found a career that I love."
- **Carrier** (Noun): Stresses the first syllable (CARE-ee-er). Someone or something that transports things (like an airline or delivery service).
  - *Example*: "Which carrier has the least expensive flights to New York?"

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. My colleague gave me some great career advice. (Stress "career" and "advice").
2. I strongly advise you not to argue with your boss.
3. Morale at that company is very high.
4. He is a very moral and honest person.
5. The mail carrier delivered the package.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence asking someone for advice on a difficult situation. Make sure you don't add an "s" to advice!
2. Explain the difference between "moral" and "morale."
3. Practice saying "Career" and "Carrier" back to back, paying attention to which syllable receives the stress.

# Advanced Pronunciation Mistakes

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa works with Anna, a foreign-born actress in Hollywood, to correct common pronunciation mistakes made by advanced speakers. The spelling of many English words does not match their pronunciation, leading to frequent errors.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Extremely angry | Infuriating | Making someone extremely angry or furious. | What you did is absolutely infuriating. |
| Put under ground | Bury | To place in the ground and cover with dirt. | The dog buried the bone in the yard. |
| Measure / judge | Gauge | To measure an amount, or to judge someone's feelings. | It is difficult to gauge his mood today. |
| Very unusual | Extraordinary | Very unusual, surprising, or impressive. | He is an extraordinarily talented musician. |

## Pronunciation Guide

| Word | Correct Pronunciation | Notes |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Entrepreneur** | on-truh-pruh-NUR | Stress the final syllable. Do not say "enter". |
| **20th / 30th** | TWEN-tee-ith | Add a schwa sound (ih) before the "th" for all tens (30th, 40th, etc.). |
| **Adjective** | AJ-ik-tiv | The 'D' is totally silent. |
| **Infuriating** | in-FYOOR-ee-ay-ting | Connect "fyoor" and "ee". |
| **Bury** | BARE-ee | Pronounced exactly like the fruit "berry". Do not say "burry". |
| **Career** | cuh-REER | Stress the second syllable. |
| **Carrier** | CARE-ee-er | Stress the first syllable. |
| **Choir** | KWIRE | Sounds like "wire" with a K in front. |
| **Clothes** | CLOZE | The 'th' is almost never pronounced. Pronounce exactly like "close the door". |
| **Cupboard** | CUB-erd | The 'P' is silent, and "board" becomes "erd". |
| **Draw** | DRAH | Say "ah". (To make a picture). |
| **Drawer** | DROR | The 'W' is silent. (Part of a desk). |
| **Extraordinary** | ek-STROR-dih-nair-ee | Drop the "a" from extra. Connect the words. |
| **Failure** | FAYL-yer | Ends with a "yer" sound. |
| **Gauge** | GAYJ | Sounds like "page" with a G. |
| **Hollywood** | HAH-lee-wood | The 'O' is an 'ah' sound. The double O is the short "uh" (like book). |
| **Hotel** | ho-TEL | Stress the second syllable. The 'T' is a true T, not a fast D. |
| **Iron** | I-ern | The 'O' is silent. Do not say "i-ron". |
| **Law / Lawyer** | LAH / LOY-yer | Law has a big "ah" sound. Lawyer has a "y" sound in the middle. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. He is a successful entrepreneur.
2. It's their 30th anniversary.
3. An adjective describes a noun.
4. That is an infuriating situation!
5. Our car was buried under a thick layer of snow.
6. The choir sounds beautiful.
7. I iron my clothes.
8. I keep the glasses in the cupboard.
9. I'd like to withdraw money from the bank.
10. The gas gauge is on full.
11. She's a Hollywood actress staying at a beautiful hotel.
12. The lawyer practices law.

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice the difference between "Draw" (making a picture) and "Drawer" (furniture).
2. Record yourself saying "Entrepreneur" and "Extraordinary" smoothly.
3. Why do we pronounce "clothes" like "close"?

# Expanding Basic Vocabulary for Confidence

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Advanced learners often lack confidence because they rely on basic words (like *happy* or *tired*) instead of the rich, varied expressions native speakers use. In this video, Lisa teaches advanced synonyms and provides four tips for naturally expanding your vocabulary.

## Vocabulary Expansion

### Instead of "Happy"
Native speakers rarely just say "I'm very happy." Try these instead:
- **Delighted**: Very pleased. "I'm delighted to meet you."
- **Overjoyed**: Extremely happy. "She was overjoyed when she heard the news."
- **Thrilled**: Extremely happy and excited. "I'm thrilled about the new project."
- **Ecstatic**: Overwhelmingly happy. "He was ecstatic when he won."
- **Elated**: Joyfully proud and happy. "The team was elated after the victory."
- **Stoked**: (Slang, California surfing culture) Very excited. "I'm stoked for the weekend."

### Instead of "Tired"
"I'm very tired" can sound repetitive. Try these instead:
- **Exhausted**: Completely without energy.
- **Drained**: Emotionally or physically emptied of energy. "That meeting left me completely drained."
- **Wiped out**: Extremely tired. "I'm wiped out after that workout."
- **Worn out**: Exhausted from continuous use or work. "I feel worn out today."
- **Bushed**: (Casual) Very tired.
- **Beat**: (Casual) Exhausted. "I'm beat, I'm going to bed."
- **Pooped**: (Very casual) Tired.

## Lisa's 4 Tips for Improving Fluency

1. **Read Outside Your Specialty**: If you are an engineer, read history or psychology. Reading broadly exposes you to well-rounded vocabulary.
2. **Write It Down**: When watching TV or movies, pause to write down expressions you don't know, then look them up.
3. **Ask Native Speakers**: Be bold. If someone uses an idiom you don't know, just ask them what it means. They are usually happy to explain.
4. **Don't Be a Perfectionist**: Experiment with new expressions. You will make mistakes, and that is a necessary part of the learning process.

## Practice Prompts

1. Replace "happy" in this sentence: "I was very happy when I got the promotion."
2. Replace "tired" in this sentence: "After a 12-hour shift, I was extremely tired."
3. Think of one topic completely outside of your normal job or studies. Find a short article in English about that topic and read it today.

title: "Confusing English Words. Is Your English Accurate? Take this quiz to find out."
description: "A deep dive into highly confusing English word pairs like weary/wary and affect/effect."
category: "english-vocabulary"
theme: "mistakes"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"

---

# Highly Confusing English Word Pairs

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

English has many words that look and sound very similar but have totally different meanings. In this lesson, Lisa clears up the confusion between 10 sets of difficult English words, ensuring your English is highly accurate.

## Confusing Word Pairs

### 1. Weary vs. Wary
- **Weary** (WEE-ree): Physically or mentally exhausted/tired. "I am weary from too much work."
- **Wary** (WARE-ee): Cautious, careful, or suspicious. "You should be wary of strangers."

### 2. Misinformation vs. Disinformation
- **Misinformation**: Incorrect information given *by mistake*, not on purpose.
- **Disinformation**: Incorrect information given *on purpose* to manipulate or hide the truth.

### 3. Childish vs. Childlike
- **Childish**: (Negative) Immature, silly, inappropriate for an adult. "Stop being so childish!"
- **Childlike**: (Positive) Innocent, enthusiastic, easily excited. "She has a childlike enthusiasm for Disney."

### 4. Affect vs. Effect
*(Note: Most native speakers pronounce both as "uh-fect")*
- **Affect** (Verb): To influence or change something. "Don't let it affect you."
- **Effect** (Noun): The result of an action. "The medicine had a negative effect on me."

### 5. Complement vs. Compliment
*(Note: Pronounced exactly the same)*
- **Complement** (with an E): To go well with or complete something. "Red wine complements beef."
- **Compliment** (with an I): To praise someone or say something nice. "She received a compliment on her dress."

### 6. Principal vs. Principle
*(Note: Pronounced exactly the same)*
- **Principal** (ends in PAL): The director of a school, or a primary leader. "He is the high school principal."
- **Principle** (ends in PLE): A moral rule or belief. "Lying is against my principles."

### 7. Dual vs. Duel
*(Note: Pronounced exactly the same)*
- **Dual** (with an A): Having two parts or roles. "She has dual citizenship."
- **Duel** (with an E): A fight with weapons between two people to settle an argument.

### 8. Counsel vs. Council
*(Note: Pronounced exactly the same)*
- **Counsel** (ends in SEL): Advice, guidance, or a lawyer giving advice. "She sought counsel from her mentor."
- **Council** (ends in CIL): A group of people chosen to make rules. "The city council made a decision."

### 9. Canvas vs. Canvass
*(Note: Pronounced exactly the same)*
- **Canvas** (One S): A strong fabric used for painting or tents. "I bought a canvas tote bag."
- **Canvass** (Two Ss): A verb meaning to talk to people in a neighborhood to get opinions or votes. "The politicians canvassed the neighborhood."

### 10. Stationary vs. Stationery
*(Note: Pronounced exactly the same)*
- **Stationary** (with an A): Not moving. "I ride a stationary bike."
- **Stationery** (with an E): Office supplies like paper and envelopes. "The logo was printed on the company stationery."

## Practice Prompts

1. The new medicine will (affect / effect) your sleep.
2. The rumor was a total mistake; it was just (misinformation / disinformation).
3. The artist painted on a large (canvas / canvass).
4. The shoes (complement / compliment) your outfit perfectly.
5. Provide the answers to the above blanks to test your understanding.

### Answer Key
1. Affect (verb)
2. Misinformation (mistake)
3. Canvas (noun)
4. Complement (verb meaning "to match")

<br/>

# More Confusing English Words

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Lisa continues to clear up confusing English words that advanced learners struggle with. These pairs sound similar but function very differently in sentences.

## Confusing Word Pairs

### 1. Every day vs. Everyday
- **Every day** (Two words): Happens daily. "I walk my dog every day."
- **Everyday** (One word, stressed on "every"): Adjective meaning common, ordinary, or routine. "Please use simple, everyday language."

### 2. Sometimes vs. Sometime vs. Some time
- **Sometimes**: Occasionally. "I sometimes go out to eat."
- **Some time**: An amount of time. "It took me some time to finish."
- **Sometime**: An unspecified time in the future or past. "Let's have coffee sometime next week."

### 3. Farther vs. Further
- **Farther**: Physical distance. "The beach is farther than I expected."
- **Further**: More or additional (not physical distance). "Do you have any further questions?"

### 4. Classic vs. Classical
- **Classic**: High quality, a standard of excellence, or memorable. "That movie is a classic."
- **Classical**: A specific historical style of music, architecture, or ancient culture. "Beethoven played classical music."

### 5. Historic vs. Historical
- **Historic**: A famous or important event in history. "The moon landing was a historic event."
- **Historical**: Belonging to the past or related to the study of history. "I enjoy reading historical novels."

### 6. Economic vs. Economical
- **Economic**: Related to the economy or finances. "We expect strong economic growth."
- **Economical**: Cost-effective; saving money. "Going by train is more economical than flying."

### 7. Beside vs. Besides
- **Beside**: Next to. "She sat beside me."
- **Besides**: In addition to, or anyway. "Besides English, she speaks French. I don't want to go; besides, it's raining."

### 8. Clothes vs. Cloths
- **Clothes** (CLOZE): Garments you wear. The 'th' is completely silent. "I washed my clothes."
- **Cloths** (CLAW-ths): Pieces of fabric used for cleaning or covering. The 'th' is pronounced. "I use a cloth to wipe the table."

### 9. Continual vs. Continuous
- **Continual**: Happens repeatedly over time, but with breaks in between. "The continual interruptions made it hard to work."
- **Continuous**: Happens constantly without ever stopping. "The rain was continuous for 3 days."

### 10. In a way vs. In the way
- **In a way**: To some extent; sort of. "In a way, I agree with you."
- **In the way**: Blocking or obstructing. "Please move, you are in the way of the TV."

### 11. Bath vs. Bathe
- **Bath** (Noun): Unvoiced 'th' (soft). "I take a bath."
- **Bathe** (Verb): Voiced 'th' (vibrating). The 'A' becomes long. "She is bathing her baby." (Note: A swimsuit is called a "bathing suit.")

### 12. Ground floor vs. First floor
- **US/Canada**: The Ground Floor and the First Floor are the exact same thing (street level).
- **UK/Europe**: The Ground Floor is street level. The First Floor is one level *above* the street (which Americans call the Second Floor).

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. She likes to paint everyday objects.
2. He moved to New York sometime in 2019.
3. We need to discuss this issue further.
4. We are witnessing a historic moment.
5. This car is very economical; you save a lot on gas.
6. She sat beside me.
7. I use a cloth to wipe the table.
8. The rain was continuous for 3 days.
9. Please move out of the way.
10. She is sunbathing in her bathing suit.

## Practice Prompts

1. I need (sometime / some time) to finish my homework, but maybe we can meet up (sometime / some time) next week?
2. My house is (farther / further) down the road.
3. The dog was standing right (in a way / in the way) of the door.

<br/>

# Farther vs. Further and the American R

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa explains the difference between the confusing words *farther* and *further*. She also covers an advanced American pronunciation rule: how to pronounce the 'R' sound when it comes immediately before a 'TH'.

## Pronunciation Rules

1. **The American 'R' is never silent**: Unlike British English, Americans always pronounce the 'R' sound (e.g., *born*, *morning*, *farther*). Don't say "father" when you mean "farther."
2. **The R + TH transition**: Moving from the pulled-back tongue position of the 'R' to the forward 'TH' is tricky. Gently touch your tongue to the back of your front teeth for the 'TH'. Do not press too hard, or the 'TH' will sound like a 'D' (e.g., "brudder" instead of "brother").

## Farther vs. Further

- **Farther**: Physical distance. (Comparative of far).
  - *Example*: "I live farther from the school than you do."
- **Further**: Additional, extra, or to advance something.
  - *Example*: "Do you have any further questions?"
  - *Verb Example*: "I would like to further my career."

## Idiomatic Expressions

| Phrase | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Look no further | You have found what you are looking for; don't look anywhere else. | If you want a good car, look no further. |
| Nothing could be further from the truth | The truth is the exact opposite of what was just said. | They say he's a bad guy, but nothing could be further from the truth. |
| Furthermore | In addition; and (formal). | The car looks great; furthermore, it's cheap. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Neptune is the farthest planet from the sun.
2. If you'd like further information, please email us.
3. If you want to visit an exciting city, look no further than New York.
4. They say he's a bad guy, but nothing could be further from the truth.

## Practice Prompts

1. Make a sentence comparing the physical distance of two places using "farther."
2. Practice the transition between 'R' and 'TH' in these words: further, farther, northern, mother, brother.
3. When writing a formal essay, what is a good alternative word for "and" or "in addition"?

<br/>

# Deep Nuances in Confusing English Words

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Lisa breaks down the differences between highly confusing word pairs that even native speakers sometimes mix up. Understanding these differences takes your English to the final level of accuracy and fluency.

## Confusing Word Pairs

### 1. Appraise vs. Apprise
- **Appraise**: To estimate the value of an object (like a house or ring). "The bank will appraise the house."
- **Apprise**: To inform someone of a situation. "Please apprise me of any changes." (Always use "apprise someone *of* something").

### 2. Imply vs. Infer
- **Imply**: To suggest something indirectly (Done by the speaker). "Are you implying that I stole it?"
- **Infer**: To conclude something based on evidence (Done by the listener). "From your tone, I inferred you were unhappy."

### 3. Peak vs. Peek vs. Pique
- **Peak** (Highest point): "Peak performance." "Peak season."
- **Peek** (A quick look): "I took a peek at the test."
- **Pique** (To stimulate interest): "The article piqued my curiosity."

### 4. Criterion vs. Criteria
- **Criterion**: Singular. "One important criterion for the job is communication skills."
- **Criteria**: Plural. "The criteria for admission are listed on the website."

### 5. Immigrate vs. Emigrate
- **Immigrate**: To come INTO a country. "They immigrated to the United States."
- **Emigrate**: To EXIT a country. "His grandparents emigrated from Italy."

### 6. House vs. Home
- **House**: A physical building. "They bought a new house."
- **Home**: A place where you live and feel comfortable (emotional). "This place feels like home." "I work from home."

### 7. On time vs. In time
- **On time**: Punctual; at the exact scheduled time. "The train arrived on time."
- **In time**: Early enough before it's too late. "We arrived just in time for dinner."

### 8. Allusion vs. Illusion
- **Allusion**: An indirect reference to something. "The song makes an allusion to Greek mythology."
- **Illusion**: A false idea or image; not real. "The mirror gave the illusion of more space."

### 9. Between vs. Among
- **Between**: Used for exactly two items/people. "The secret is between John and Maria."
- **Among**: Used for more than two items/people. "The prize was shared among the winners."

## Practice Prompts

1. I arrived at the airport just (on time / in time) to catch my flight.
2. The speaker (implied / inferred) that the project would be delayed, and I (implied / inferred) that meant we wouldn't get our bonuses.
3. The strange noise (peaked / peeked / piqued) my interest.

<br/>

# English Homographs: Same Spelling, Different Pronunciation

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Homographs are words that are spelled exactly the same but have different meanings and different pronunciations. In many cases, changing the syllable stress changes the word from a noun to a verb.

## Homograph Test

### 1. Refuse
- **re-FUSE** (Verb with a Z sound): To reject. "He refused the invitation."
- **REF-use** (Noun with an S sound): Garbage or trash. "Workers cleared the smelly refuse."

### 2. Wind
- **Wind** (Short i): Moving air. "The wind blows."
- **Wind** (Long I, rhymes with find): To twist or turn. "I need to wind the old clock."

### 3. Bass
- **Bass** (rhymes with pass): A type of fish. "I ordered bass for dinner."
- **Bass** (rhymes with face): A deep low sound in music. "He likes music with deep bass."

### 4. Conduct
- **CON-duct** (Noun): Behavior. "He was praised for his good conduct."
- **con-DUCT** (Verb): To lead or direct. "He will conduct the orchestra tonight."

### 5. Console
- **CON-sole** (Noun): A machine/device. "He plays games on his console."
- **con-SOLE** (Verb): To comfort someone who is sad. "She consoled her friend."

### 6. Entrance
- **EN-trance** (Noun): A door or way in. "Let's meet at the entrance."
- **en-TRANCE** (Verb): To charm or amaze magically. "The magician entranced the audience."

### 7. Invalid
- **IN-vuh-lid** (Noun): A sick or weak person needing care. "She is helping the invalid."
- **in-VAL-id** (Adjective): Not usable or acceptable. "The expired ticket is invalid."

### 8. Object
- **OB-ject** (Noun): A physical item. "What is that object?"
- **ob-JECT** (Verb): To disagree. "She objected to the decision."

### 9. Minute
- **MIN-it** (Noun): 60 seconds. "Please wait a minute."
- **my-NOOT** (Adjective): Extremely small or tiny. "She noticed the minute details."

### 10. Produce
- **PRO-doos** (Noun): Fresh fruits and vegetables. "I bought fresh produce."
- **pro-DOOS** (Verb): To make or create. "He produces music."

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice saying the noun and verb forms of *produce*, *object*, *console*, and *conduct*. Notice how the stress shifts to the first syllable for nouns, and the second syllable for verbs.
2. Write a sentence using the word "minute" to mean "very small."

<br/>

# Swell, Swollen, and Swallow

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Lisa explains the difference between the confusing English words "swell," "swollen," and "swallow." She then covers native idiomatic expressions that use these words.

## Vocabulary Definitions

### Swell (Verb)
- **Meaning**: To get bigger due to injury or illness.
- **Forms**: Swell (present), Swelled (past), Swollen (participle).
- **Usage Note**: The past tense (swelled) is rarely used. Native speakers prefer to use the adjective "swollen" or the noun "swelling."

### Swollen (Adjective) & Swelling (Noun)
- **Swollen**: "His ankle is swollen." "Her eye is swollen."
- **Swelling**: "You may have some swelling after surgery."

### Swallow (Verb / Noun)
- **Meaning 1 (Verb)**: When food or drink goes down your throat. "When I had a sore throat, it hurt to swallow."
- **Meaning 2 (Noun)**: A type of small bird. "There are swallows in my neighborhood."

## Idiomatic Expressions

| Phrase | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Have a swollen head | To be arrogant; to think you are more important or intelligent than you really are. | After he won the championship, he got a swollen head. |
| Swallow your pride | To humbly accept a lower position, admit a mistake, or ask for forgiveness when you feel too proud to do so. | You need to swallow your pride and admit that you made a mistake. |
| A hard pill to swallow | A difficult situation or sad fact that you are forced to accept. | Not getting accepted to the university was a hard pill to swallow. |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. He put ice on a swollen ankle.
2. Her wedding ring didn't fit her swollen fingers.
3. The insect bite caused pain and swelling.
4. When I had a sore throat, it hurt to swallow.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever had to "swallow your pride"? What happened?
2. What is an example of a "hard pill to swallow" in life or business?

# The "AH" vs. "UH" Vowel Sounds

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Many advanced learners confuse two very different American vowel sounds: the open, wide "AH" sound (like in *lock*, *cop*, *fond*) and the neutral, relaxed "UH" sound (like in *luck*, *cup*, *fund*). 

## Pronunciation Rules

### 1. The Open "AH" Sound
- **Physical action**: Drop your jaw and open your mouth very wide.
- **Examples**: Lock, Cop, Fond, Body, Donald.
- **Spelling**: Often spelled with an 'O'.

### 2. The Neutral "UH" Sound
- **Physical action**: Do not move your jaw or open your mouth much at all. The sound comes from the throat. If you open your mouth, your tongue drops and the sound changes.
- **Examples**: Luck, Cup, Fund, Buddy, Trump.
- **Spelling**: Often spelled with a 'U', but surprisingly often spelled with an 'O' (e.g., *money*, *some*, *love*).

## Word Comparisons

Practice saying these pairs, ensuring your jaw drops low for the first word, but stays closed and neutral for the second word:

| Open "AH" (Drop Jaw) | Neutral "UH" (Keep Jaw Still) |
| --- | --- |
| Lock | Luck |
| Cop | Cup |
| Fond | Fund |
| Body | Buddy |

## Tricky Spelling Exceptions

Many very common English words are spelled with an 'O' but are pronounced with the neutral "UH" sound:
- Money (Muh-nee)
- Some (Suhm)
- Nothing (Nuh-thing)
- Other (Uh-ther)
- Love (Luhv)

## Good Phrases to Practice

To master this, practice phrases that contain *both* sounds back-to-back:
1. **A lot of money**: Drop your jaw for "lot", keep it closed for "money".
2. **I love to jog**: Closed for "love", wide open for "jog".
3. **Another job**: Closed for "another", wide open for "job".
4. **Donald Trump**: Wide open for "Donald", closed for "Trump".

<br/>

# Linking with the word "and"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

In English, certain word pairs go together so commonly that native speakers treat them as a single unit. When they do this, they completely drop the "a" and "d" in the word "and," reducing it to just an "n" sound.

## The Rule

When "and" connects two words that naturally belong together, don't say the full word "and." Instead, just say **"n"**. 

## Common Examples

| Written Phrase | Pronounced As |
| --- | --- |
| Salt and pepper | salt-n-pepper |
| Mom and dad | mom-n-dad |
| Black and white | black-n-white |
| Bacon and eggs | bacon-n-eggs |
| Brother and sister | brother-n-sister |
| Rock and roll | rock-n-roll |
| In and out | in-n-out |

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. May I have some salt 'n' pepper?
2. I went with my mom 'n' dad.
3. I prefer black 'n' white photography.
4. I had bacon 'n' eggs for breakfast.
5. We're brother 'n' sister.
6. I love rock 'n' roll music.
7. Let's go to In 'N' Out Burger.

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself reading the sentences above. Did you pronounce the 'D' in "and"? If so, try again and eliminate it completely.
2. Can you think of any other common pairs of words connected by "and"? (e.g. up and down, left and right). Practice linking those.

<br/>

# A Dangerous Vowel Mistake (And Hollywood Pronunciation)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

While visiting the Dolby Theatre in Hollywood, Lisa explains the pronunciation of "Brad Pitt" and "Hollywood" to help students fix dangerous vowel mistakes. Confusing the tense "EE" sound with the neutral "IH" sound can result in accidentally saying a bad word.

## The Dangerous Vowel Mistake (IH vs. EE)

Many languages do not have the short, neutral "IH" sound (like in *hit*). Instead, speakers substitute the tense "EE" sound (like in *heat*). 
- **Relaxed / Neutral (IH)**: The tongue is relaxed in the back of the mouth. (e.g., *it*, *sit*, *hit*, *fit*, *bit*, *Pitt*).
- **Tense (EE)**: The tongue is tense and high in the mouth. (e.g., *eat*, *seat*, *heat*, *feet*, *beat*, *Pete*).

**Why it's dangerous**: If you use the tense "EE" sound when trying to say the word *sheet*, it sounds perfectly fine. But if you try to say *sheet* and accidentally use the neutral "IH" sound... you will say a very bad word! 

## Pronouncing "Brad Pitt"

- **Brad**: The 'a' is an "aah" sound (like in *sat*, *van*, *man*). It is not "bread" (like in *set*, *then*, *men*).
- **Pitt**: The 'i' is the relaxed "ih" sound. Do not say "Pete".

## Pronouncing "Hollywood"

1. **The 'O' (Hah-)**: The 'O' is pronounced as a big "ah" sound. "Hah-lee."
2. **The 'W' (-w-)**: You must round your lips to create the W, but you must immediately pull them back.
3. **The Double O (-wood)**: Do not push your lips forward and say "ooh" (like *food* or *moon*). It is the neutral, relaxed sound (like *book*, *foot*, *understood*).

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. Brad Pitt is in the film *Once Upon a Time in Hollywood*.
2. Please take a seat, don't just sit there.
3. These shoes fit my feet perfectly.
4. I need a clean sheet for the bed.

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice alternating between these pairs: Sit/Seat, Fit/Feet, Bit/Beat, Hit/Heat. Notice the tension in your tongue on the second word.
2. Say the word "Hollywood" slowly. Make sure your lips only round for the 'W' and then relax for the 'ood'.

<br/>

# Regional Accents vs. The Standard American Accent

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-culture

## Summary

Lisa interviews Susan Bennett, a professional voiceover artist famous for being the original voice of Siri. They discuss what it means to speak with "no accent" (the standard American accent) and look at examples of strong regional dialects from New York, Boston, and California.

## The "Standard American" Accent

When a native speaker tells another native speaker, "You don't have an accent," they mean the person speaks with a **General American** or **Neutral** accent.
- Also known as "Broadcaster speech."
- It means listeners cannot tell which region or city the speaker is from.
- It lacks the exaggerated vowel sounds found in places like New York, Boston, or the Deep South.

## Vocabulary And Expressions

| Simple phrase | Advanced expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Noticeable | Pronounced | Strongly marked; obvious. | Their accent is much less pronounced. |
| Advertising song | Jingle | A short song used in commercials to help you remember a product. | I sang jingles at different recording studios. |
| Text to read | Copy | The text or script used in advertising or broadcasting. | Come over here and read this copy. |

## Famous Regional Accents

1. **New York Accent**:
   - Known for exaggerating the "aw" sound. 
   - Words like *dog*, *coffee*, *walk*, and *water* have a very distinct, heavy "aw" sound.
2. **Boston (New England) Accent**:
   - Known for dropping the 'r' sound and using a very large "ah" sound.
   - The famous test phrase is: *"Park my car in Harvard yard."* (Pahk my cah in Hah-vahd yahd).
3. **California (Surfer/Valley) Accent**:
   - Generally, California has a neutral accent. However, a specific coastal subculture exaggerates and elongates vowels (e.g., saying "broooo" or "whaaat").

## How to Review

Listen to American news broadcasters (like CNN or local news). You will hear the "Standard American Accent" that Susan uses. Notice how clear the vowels are and how firmly the 'R' sounds are pronounced, completely lacking any strong regional flavor.

<br/>
