# Dropped Syllables in English

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

In many languages, you pronounce every vowel that is written in a word. English is different. In several very common English words, a vowel sound completely disappears, reducing the total number of syllables in the word. 

## The Disappearing Vowels

You must memorize these common words, as their spelling is misleading. Do not pronounce the vowel that is crossed out!

| Word | Incorrect (Pronouncing all vowels) | Correct Pronunciation |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Chocolate** | cho-co-late (3 syllables) | CHOK-lit (2 syllables) |
| **Vegetable** | veg-e-ta-ble (4 syllables) | VEJ-tuh-bul (3 syllables) |
| **Catholic** | cath-o-lic (3 syllables) | CATH-lik (2 syllables) |
| **Average** | av-e-rage (3 syllables) | AV-rij (2 syllables) |
| **Basically** | ba-sic-al-ly (4 syllables) | BAY-sic-lee (3 syllables) |
| **Beverage** | bev-e-rage (3 syllables) | BEV-rij (2 syllables) |
| **Broccoli** | broc-co-li (3 syllables) | BROK-lee (2 syllables) |
| **Business** | bus-i-ness (3 syllables) | BIZ-niss (2 syllables) |

*(Note: "Opera" is also often pronounced as 2 syllables: OP-ruh).*

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I'd like to order some vegetables, please.
2. What's the average temperature here?
3. Broccoli is a green vegetable.
4. I had several pieces of chocolate.
5. That's an interesting beverage.
6. He owns a successful business.

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying: "I basically ate average vegetables and chocolate." Ensure you drop the necessary syllables!

<br/>

# Pronouncing "World" and "Work"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

The word "world" is notoriously difficult for non-native speakers because of two tricky consonant clusters: 'WOR' and 'RL'. In this lesson, Lisa breaks down the mechanics of the mouth to make these words easy.

## Rule 1: The "WOR" combination

When a word starts with "WOR", the 'O' is **not** pronounced as an 'O'. Do not round your lips and say "war". The 'O' completely disappears.
Instead, pronounce it exactly like the word **"were"** (w + er). 

1. **Work**: Say "were" + "k". (Not "wark").
2. **Word**: Say "were" + "d". (Not "ward").
3. **Worry**: Say "were" + "ee". (Not "war-ee").
4. **World**: Starts with "were".

*Remember: The W requires slightly rounded lips, but the American 'R' is made in the back of the throat. Do not roll or trill the 'R'!*

## Rule 2: The "RL" combination

When moving from an American 'R' (tongue pulled back) to an 'L' (tongue touching the front teeth), you must add an invisible, extra vowel sound in between them. 
Imagine a small "uh" sound between the 'R' and the 'L'.

- **World**: (Were) + (uh-ld) = Were-uld.

Say it slowly: *Were... uld*. Now, blend them together smoothly: *World*. 

## Good Sentences To Imitate

1. I go to work every day.
2. That's a new word.
3. Don't worry about it.
4. He traveled around the whole world.
5. It's a worldwide business.

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice saying "War" and "Were". Notice how your lips push forward for "War" but stay relaxed for "Were".
2. Now practice saying: "I went to work, learned a new word, and didn't worry about the world." Use the "were" sound for all of them!

# Popular American Slang Words

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa and native speaker Drake introduce popular American slang words. Learning slang helps you understand TV shows, movies, social media, and casual conversations much better, making your English well-rounded.

## Slang Vocabulary

| Slang Word/Phrase | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Epic** | Awesome, memorable, legendary, or amazing. | "That roller coaster was epic!" |
| **I can't even** | Used when a situation is so ridiculous, shocking, or overwhelming that you have no words for it. | "Look at what he's wearing... I can't even." |
| **FOMO** | "Fear Of Missing Out". The fear that your friends are having fun without you. | "I stayed home on Friday night and had major FOMO." |
| **Legit** | Authentic, real, or genuinely excellent. | "That restaurant's pizza is legit." |
| **Vanilla** | Boring, bland, unexciting, or basic (used to describe things or personalities). | "He has a very vanilla personality." |
| **I'm down** | "I agree to do that," "I'm interested," or "I would love to do it." | "Do you want to go bowling tonight?" "I'm down." |
| **Simp** | (Negative) A guy who is overly nice to a girl, constantly agreeing with her and buying her things just to get her attention. | "He bought her another expensive gift? He's such a simp." |
| **Off the chain** | Extremely good, crazy good, or amazing. | "The music at that party was off the chain." |
| **Bougie** | High-maintenance. Someone who only likes the finer, more expensive things in life (high-end restaurants, brand names). | "She's too bougie to eat at McDonald's." |
| **A-Game** | Your highest level of performance; your very best. | "I have an interview tomorrow, so I need to bring my A-game." |
| **No biggie** | "No big deal" / "It's not a problem." | "You forgot your pencil? No biggie, borrow mine." |
| **Rent-free** | When you can't stop thinking about someone, or they bother you a lot, they are "living rent-free in your head." | "Why do you care what he thinks? He's living rent-free in your head." |

## Advanced Expressions Used in the Video

1. **To miss out on**: To lose a chance or not benefit from something. "Don't miss out on this opportunity."
2. **To be open to**: To be willing to consider or do something. "I'm open to getting married."
3. **To worship the ground someone walks on**: To admire and love someone excessively. "He worships the ground she walks on."
4. **The finer things in life**: Luxurious, high-quality, expensive things. "They enjoy traveling and the finer things in life."
5. **Top dollar**: The highest possible price. "People pay top dollar for those concert tickets."

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe a time you experienced "FOMO."
2. Have you ever known someone who was a little bit "bougie"? What did they do?
3. What is an activity that you are totally "down" to do this weekend?

# Rules for Using "The"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-grammar

## Summary

The English articles "a", "an", and "the" are notoriously difficult. In this lesson, Lisa explains the specific rules for when you must use the definite article "the," and when you must leave it out.

## Rule 1: Everyday Places and Activities

**Rule**: Do NOT use "the" for everyday places when referring to the *activity* or *routine* (school, class, church, bed, work, jail, prison). 
**Exception**: Use "the" when referring to the specific *physical building* or object.

| No "The" (Activity / Routine) | Use "The" (Physical Building/Object) |
| --- | --- |
| I go to **school** every day. | **The school** is on the corner. |
| I am going to **bed** now. | **The bed** is very soft. |
| He goes to **church** on Sundays. | I'll meet you at **the church**. |
| He is at **work**. | **The work** is difficult. |
| He is in **prison**. | **The prison** is secure. |

*Note on "Hospital"*: 
- American English: "He is in **the** hospital."
- British English: "He is in hospital."

## Rule 2: Musical Instruments

**Rule**: Use "the" when you are talking about *playing* the instrument. Use "a/an" when talking about the physical object.
- "I play **the** piano."
- "I bought **a** piano."

## Rule 3: Geography

1. **Lakes**: No "the". (*Lake Tahoe, Lake Michigan*).
2. **Rivers**: Use "the". (*The Nile River, The Amazon River*).
3. **Mountains**: No "the" for individual mountains. (*Mount Everest, Mount Fuji*).
4. **Countries**: No "the" for singular countries (*Japan, France*). 
   - *Exception*: Use "the" if the country is plural, or includes a group name/political title (*The United States, The United Kingdom, The Netherlands, The Philippines*).

## Rule 4: Universities

**Rule**: Do not use "the" for universities (*Harvard University, Stanford*). 
**Exception**: Use "the" if the name includes the word "of" (*The University of California*).

## Rule 5: Time Expressions (Next / Last)

**Rule**: Do not use "the" with *next* and *last* when referring to a time measured from right now.
- "I'm going to Italy **next week**."
- "We had dinner **last night**."
**Exception**: Use "the" when telling a story about the past.
- "He arrived **the next day**."

## Rule 6: Numbers and Dates

**Rule**: Use "the" for ordinal numbers (1st, 2nd, 3rd), but not for cardinal numbers (1, 2, 3).
- "This is **the first** time." / "I'm on **the third** page."
- "I finished chapter two." / "I am on page three."

**Rule for Dates**: 
- Use "the" if the date comes before the month: "It is **the 4th** of July."
- No "the" if the month comes first: "It is July 4th."

## Practice Prompts

1. Which is correct: "I visited Lake Michigan" or "I visited the Lake Michigan"?
2. Which is correct: "She plays guitar" or "She plays the guitar"?
3. Why do we say "The United States" but just "Canada"?

<br/>

# Common Homophone Pronunciation Mistakes

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Many common English words look completely different but are pronounced exactly the same (homophones). Students often mispronounce them because they try to follow the spelling. In this lesson, Lisa clears up these pronunciation traps.

## Pronunciation Groups

### Group 1: There / Their / They're
All three words are pronounced exactly the same: **"THAIR"**.
- **There**: A place. "Over there."
- **Their**: Possessive. "This is their house."
- **They're**: Contraction of "they are." Do NOT pronounce the 'A'. It is simply "there". "They're happy."

*Sentence*: "They're upset that their luggage wasn't there."

### Group 2: Do / Due / Dew
All three words are pronounced exactly the same: **"DOO"**.
*(Note: In British English, "due" is pronounced "dyoo", but in American English it is simply "doo").*
- **Do**: Verb. "I do my homework."
- **Due**: When something is scheduled or required. "My rent is due on the 1st." / "When is the baby due?" / "My bill is overdue."
- **Dew**: Water droplets on the grass in the morning. "The grass was wet with morning dew."

### Group 3: One / Won
Both words are pronounced exactly the same: **"WUN"**.
- **One**: Number 1.
- **Won**: Past tense of "to win". Do NOT pronounce it "Wohn." 

*Sentence*: "They won only one game."

### Group 4: So / Sew / Sow
All three words are pronounced exactly the same: **"SOH"**.
- **So**: "I think so."
- **Sew**: To make clothes using a needle and thread. "She bought a sewing machine." (Do NOT say "su").
- **Sow**: To plant seeds in the ground. "A farmer sows the seeds."

## Advanced Idiom

**"You reap what you sow."**
- *Meaning*: You eventually have to face the consequences of your actions. If you do good things, good things will happen to you. If you plant bad seeds, you will harvest bad results.

## Practice Prompts

1. Read this sentence aloud: "They're over there getting their bags."
2. Read this sentence aloud: "I won one dollar."
3. Read this sentence aloud: "She used a sewing machine so she could sew."

<br/>

# A Pronunciation Test: Same or Different?

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Lisa tests your English pronunciation by asking whether pairs of words are pronounced the same or differently. This exercise highlights subtle vowel differences, consonant voicing, and completely identical homophones.

## The Test: Are they pronounced the same or differently?

### Different Pronunciations
1. **Beer vs. Bear**: (Different). *Beer* rhymes with ear. *Bear* rhymes with hair. (Bear means the animal, or the verb "to tolerate: I can't bear it").
2. **Career vs. Carrier**: (Different). *Career* stresses the 2nd syllable (cuh-REER). *Carrier* stresses the 1st syllable (CARE-ee-er).
3. **Where vs. Were**: (Different). *Where* rhymes with hair. *Were* rhymes with her. "Where were you?"
4. **Loose vs. Lose**: (Different). *Loose* has an 'S' sound and a shorter vowel. *Lose* has a 'Z' sound and a longer vowel. "He wears loose clothes to lose weight."
5. **He's vs. His**: (Different). *He's* uses the tense "EE" sound. *His* uses the relaxed "IH" sound.
6. **Peas vs. Peace**: (Different). *Peas* ends with a 'Z' sound (longer vowel). *Peace* ends with an 'S' sound (shorter vowel).
7. **Polish vs. Polish**: (Different). *Polish* (the language/people) uses a long "O". *Polish* (nail polish) uses a short "ah" sound.
8. **Pear vs. Peer**: (Different). *Pear* (fruit) rhymes with hair. *Peer* (colleague) rhymes with ear.
9. **Moral vs. Morale**: (Different). *Moral* stresses the 1st syllable. *Morale* stresses the 2nd syllable.
10. **Sale vs. Sell**: (Different). *Sale* uses the long "A" sound. *Sell* uses the short "eh" sound.
11. **Nobel vs. Noble**: (Different). *Nobel* stresses the 2nd syllable (No-BEL). *Noble* stresses the 1st syllable (NO-bul).

### Same Pronunciations (Homophones)
1. **Wore vs. War**: (Same). "I wore my jacket during the war."
2. **Sweet vs. Suite**: (Same). "The hotel suite is sweet."
3. **Road vs. Rode**: (Same). "I rode my bike on the road."
4. **Finish vs. Finnish**: (Same). "Did the Finnish man finish the test?"
5. **Air vs. Heir**: (Same). An *heir* is someone who inherits money. "The heir breathed the fresh air."
6. **Past vs. Passed**: (Same). The "-ed" ending on passed makes a 'T' sound. "In the past, I passed the test."

### Regional Difference
- **Bean vs. Been**: 
  - *British English*: They are pronounced the **same** (Both are "bean"). 
  - *American English*: They are **different**. "Bean" is tense. "Been" is pronounced exactly like the word "bin" (relaxed IH sound).

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice the difference between *Loose* and *Lose*. Make sure your vocal cords vibrate on the 'Z' sound for *lose*.
2. Practice the difference between *Sale* and *Sell*. "I want to sell my car. It is on sale."

# Advanced Rules for "A" and "An"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-grammar

## Summary

You know the basic rules: use "a/an" with singular countable nouns (*a car, an apple*), and don't use them with uncountable nouns (*milk, beef, equipment*). But what happens when native speakers break these rules? In this lesson, Lisa explains how adding "a" can completely change the meaning of a word.

## Nouns that Change Meaning with "A"

Some nouns are uncountable in their general sense, but become countable when referring to a specific item, container, or instance.

| Word | Uncountable Meaning (No "a") | Countable Meaning (With "a") |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Milk / Water / Beer / Coffee** | The general liquid. ("I drink water.") | A container (bottle, glass, cup). ("I'd like *a* water.") |
| **Hair** | All the hair on your head. ("She has beautiful hair.") | A single strand. ("I found *a* hair in my food.") |
| **Chicken** | The meat you eat. ("I had chicken for lunch.") | The whole animal. ("I baked *a* chicken.") |
| **Iron** | The metal material. ("The pan is made of iron.") | The electric device for clothes. ("I need *an* iron to iron my shirt.") |
| **Company** | Guests, visitors, or socializing. ("We are having company for dinner.") | A business organization. ("He started *a* company.") |

## "A Number Of" vs. "The Number Of"

This is a very common grammar trap. One article changes the meaning and the required verb tense.

1. **A number of**: Means "several" or "many" (an unspecified amount). It requires a **plural verb**.
   - *Example*: "**A number of** people **have** applied for the job."
   - *Example*: "**A number of** students **are** absent."

2. **The number of**: Refers to a specific statistical amount. It requires a **singular verb**.
   - *Example*: "**The number of** people we need to hire **is** ten."
   - *Example*: "**The number of** students **has** increased."

## Practice Prompts

1. Is this correct? "I bought an equipment for my gym." (Answer: No, equipment is always uncountable).
2. What is the difference between "I have company" and "I have a company"?
3. Fill in the blank (is/are): "A number of customers ______ complaining."

# 20 "Hand" Idioms for Fluent English

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Native speakers use idioms constantly. In this video, Lisa and Drake teach 20 common American English expressions that all use the word "hand." 

## Expressions with "Hand"

| Expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Hand-me-down** | A second-hand item passed from an older person to a younger one. | "I wore my brother's hand-me-down sweaters." |
| **To know something like the back of your hand** | To know something extremely well; to be very familiar with it. | "I know these streets like the back of my hand." |
| **My hands are tied** | I am unable to help you or do something (usually because of rules). | "I wish I could give you a refund, but my hands are tied." |
| **An extra pair of hands** | Extra help. | "I need an extra pair of hands to move this couch." |
| **The situation at hand** | The current situation that needs immediate attention. | "Let's focus on the situation at hand." |
| **To have the upper hand** | To have an advantage, power, or control in a situation. | "The taller player has the upper hand in basketball." |
| **To be in good hands** | To be safe, secure, and well taken care of. | "Don't worry about your car, you're in good hands with this mechanic." |
| **Talk to the hand** | (Slang/Rude) "I am not listening to you anymore. Go away." | "You're annoying me. Talk to the hand." |
| **Hands down** | Without question; easily; unquestionably. | "This is hands down the best dessert I've ever had." |
| **The handwriting is on the wall** | It is obvious that something bad is going to happen soon. | "I'm looking for a new job. The handwriting is on the wall here." |
| **Put your hands together** | To applaud or clap for someone. | "Please put your hands together for our next speaker!" |
| **To throw your hands up** | To give up out of frustration. | "I couldn't solve the math problem, so I just threw my hands up." |
| **I gotta hand it to you** | I have to admit that you did a good job (giving a reluctant compliment). | "I didn't think you could fix it, but I gotta hand it to you, you did." |
| **To go hand in hand** | To go together perfectly; to be closely connected. | "Peanut butter and jelly go hand in hand." |
| **On hand** | Available and accessible right now. | "Do you have any cash on hand?" |

## Advanced Vocabulary Used

- **To zone out**: To lose concentration or stop paying attention. "I was so bored I completely zoned out."
- **To win by a landslide**: To win by a huge, overwhelming margin. "Our team won by a landslide."
- **To be "over it"**: To no longer care about a situation or person. "I'm so over it."

## Practice Prompts

1. What is a topic or place that you "know like the back of your hand"?
2. Have you ever had to "throw your hands up" and quit something?

# 20 "Mind" Idioms for Fluent English

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Continuing their series on idioms, Lisa and Drake teach a collection of essential English expressions that all revolve around the word "mind."

## Expressions with "Mind"

| Expression | Meaning / usage | Natural example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Mind games** | Psychological manipulation to confuse or intimidate someone. | "Stop playing mind games and tell me the truth." |
| **In the back of my mind** | A subconscious thought; something you know but aren't actively focusing on. | "I was enjoying the party, but in the back of my mind, I knew I had an exam tomorrow." |
| **Great minds think alike** | Said when two people have the same smart or funny idea at the exact same time. | "You ordered pizza too? Great minds think alike!" |
| **You read my mind** | "I was just thinking the exact same thing." | "I was just about to suggest we leave. You read my mind." |
| **To put your mind to it** | To give something all of your effort and focus to accomplish it. | "You can learn English if you just put your mind to it." |
| **Out of sight, out of mind** | If you don't see a problem, you stop worrying about it. | "I hid the junk food in the cupboard. Out of sight, out of mind." |
| **Mind your manners** | Be polite; behave properly. | "Mind your manners at the dinner table." |
| **Out of your mind** | Crazy; irrational. | "You want to jump out of an airplane? You're out of your mind!" |
| **To take your mind off something** | To distract yourself from a problem or worry. | "I like to exercise to take my mind off my stress." |
| **To give someone a piece of your mind** | To angrily tell someone exactly what you think about their bad behavior. | "That mechanic ripped me off. I'm going to give him a piece of my mind." |
| **It blew my mind** | It shocked, surprised, or amazed me. | "The plot twist at the end of that movie blew my mind." |
| **It slipped my mind** | "I completely forgot" (used for minor, harmless mistakes). | "I meant to call you back, but it slipped my mind." |
| **Peace of mind** | A feeling of being safe, calm, and free from worry. | "Having good insurance gives me peace of mind." |
| **State of mind** | Your current mood or psychological condition. | "I can't study right now; I'm not in the right state of mind." |
| **To take a load off your mind** | To feel relieved because a big worry or problem has been solved. | "Finishing that huge project really took a load off my mind." |

## Advanced Vocabulary Used

- **To bluff**: To pretend you are going to do something to scare someone, when you actually have no intention of doing it.
- **I could really use...**: "I want" or "It would benefit me to have." ("I could really use a nap right now.")
- **To sweep something under the rug**: To hide or ignore a problem, hoping people will forget about it.
- **To blow off steam**: To do a physical activity to get rid of stress or anger.
- **A go-getter**: A highly motivated, ambitious person who gets things done.

## Practice Prompts

1. What is an activity you do to "take your mind off" work?
2. Has a movie or book ever "blown your mind"? Which one?

# Real Estate Vocabulary and Daily Expressions

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Lisa visits a $2.3 million open house in Los Angeles and interviews the real estate agent, Raquel. They use highly natural, native-level vocabulary related to homes, cooking, and renting.

## House & Real Estate Vocabulary

| Word / Phrase | Meaning |
| --- | --- |
| **Cozy** | Giving a feeling of comfort, warmth, and relaxation. |
| **To stage (a home)** | To temporarily decorate a house with rented furniture to make it look beautiful for buyers. |
| **Jack and Jill bathroom** | A bathroom located between two bedrooms, with doors opening into both rooms. |
| **Stackable washer and dryer** | A washing machine and dryer designed to be stacked vertically to save space. |
| **Burner** | The round heating elements on top of a stove. ("This stove has 8 burners.") |
| **Chef's Kitchen** | A very large, high-end kitchen with professional appliances. |
| **Kitchen Island** | A freestanding cabinet and countertop in the middle of a kitchen. |
| **ADU (Accessory Dwelling Unit)** | A small, separate guest house in the backyard (often a converted garage). |
| **Cosmetic (fixes)** | Minor, aesthetic fixes (like paint or lights) that don't affect the structure of the house. |
| **Loungy / Lounge** | A relaxed, comfortable space meant for lying back and relaxing. |

## Advanced Expressions

1. **You're more than welcome**: An enthusiastic invitation. "You are more than welcome to join us for dinner."
2. **To put something on the back burner**: An idiom meaning to make a task a low priority and delay dealing with it. "I put my hobby on the back burner while studying for exams."
3. **Plenty of / Ample**: More than enough. "We have *ample* space." "There is *plenty of* food."
4. **Room vs. Rooms**: 
   - *Countable*: "There are no *rooms* left in the hotel." 
   - *Uncountable*: "There is no more *room* (space) in my suitcase."
5. **To entertain (guests)**: To host people at your house, providing food and hospitality. "I love to entertain on the weekends."
6. **To rent vs. To rent out**:
   - *To rent*: Usually means you are paying money to live somewhere. ("I rent an apartment.")
   - *To rent out*: Means you are the owner, and someone else is paying you. ("I am renting my house out to college students.")

## Pronouncing Numbers

- **Specific Millions**: Do NOT add an 's' to "million" if you give a specific number. "It costs 2.3 million" (Not "millions"). 
- **Hundreds shortcut**: Native speakers rarely say "Four thousand five hundred." They say **"Forty-five hundred"** (4500).

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe your kitchen. Does it have an island? How many burners are on your stove?
2. What is a project or goal that you recently "put on the back burner"?

# Advanced News Vocabulary (Protests & Conflict)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Lisa uses a Wall Street Journal article about protests in Los Angeles to teach advanced vocabulary related to conflict, politics, and law enforcement. She also highlights tricky pronunciation rules for these words.

## News Vocabulary

| Word/Phrase | Meaning | Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **To launch** | To start something new or important (a project, an event). | "The company launched a new product." |
| **ICE** | Immigration and Customs Enforcement (a US government agency). | "Protesters gathered outside the ICE office." |
| **To escalate** | To become bigger, more serious, or more intense. | "The small argument escalated into a huge fight." |
| **To chant** | To shout words repeatedly in a group rhythm. | "The fans chanted the team's name." |
| **Word spread** | News or information was shared with many people. | "As word spread about the sale, people rushed to the store." |
| **To pick up** | To resume after stopping, or to gain strength. | "The wind picked up, and the storm got worse." |
| **Roughly** | About; approximately. | "The trip will take roughly two hours." |
| **Personnel** | The employees or staff of an organization. | "All military personnel must report to the base." |
| **A clash** | A fight or conflict between two groups. | "There was a clash between the police and the protesters." |
| **To disband** | To break up a group and make them leave. | "The police disbanded the crowd." |
| **Pockets of...** | Small, isolated groups or areas of something. | "There are still pockets of snow in the mountains." |
| **To break out** | To start suddenly (used for fires, fights, or diseases). | "A fire broke out in the kitchen." |
| **To spill onto** | To move quickly from one place and spread into another. | "The crowd spilled onto the main street." |
| **Thoroughfare** | A main road or major street where many people travel. | "The parade blocked the city's main thoroughfare." |

## Tricky Pronunciation

1. **Launch vs. Lunch**: *Launch* uses a big, open "AW" sound. *Lunch* uses a short, neutral "UH" sound.
2. **Address (Noun vs. Verb)**: 
   - *Noun*: Stress the 1st syllable, use a wide 'A'. (**A**-dress). "What is your email address?"
   - *Verb*: Stress the 2nd syllable, reduce the 'A' to 'uh'. (uh-**DRESS**). "We need to address the problem."
3. **Personnel vs. Personal**:
   - *Personal*: Stress the 1st syllable. (**PER**-son-al).
   - *Personnel*: Stress the 3rd syllable. (per-son-**NEL**).
4. **Tear Gas**: *Tear* rhymes with *ear* (the water that comes from your eye when crying).

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence using the verb "to break out" (e.g., about a fire or a fight).
2. Practice the pronunciation difference between the noun "address" and the verb "address".

# Advanced Idioms from Real Conversation

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Lisa and her friend Drake have a natural conversation about Drake moving to Las Vegas and picking up boxing. This lesson breaks down the advanced phrasal verbs, idioms, and natural expressions Drake uses.

## Natural American Expressions

| Expression | Meaning / Usage | Natural Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **To make a move** | To make a conscious, big decision to relocate or act. (Emphasizes the *decision*, not just the physical act of moving). | "I made the move to Las Vegas about a year ago." |
| **To pick up (a hobby)** | To start learning or doing something new. | "I picked up boxing almost full-time." |
| **As opposed to** | In contrast to; rather than; instead of. | "Why did you choose boxing as opposed to something else?" |
| **Pretty much** | Almost completely; very close to being true. | "I've been a boxing fan pretty much since I was a kid." |
| **To dabble in** | To do something casually or as a hobby, without serious commitment. | "I like to dabble in photography in my free time." |
| **To dive right into** | To start doing something with a lot of energy and without hesitation. | "I decided to dive right into the new project." |
| **A lot is on the line** | There is a high risk; there are significant consequences if you fail. | "A lot is on the line when you compete." |
| **The ins and outs** | The specific details, rules, and facts about how something works. | "I had to learn the ins and outs of the business." |
| **To inch one's way into** | To make very slow, gradual progress toward something difficult. | "He inched his way into the top ranks of the company." |
| **Everything that goes into it** | All the different components, effort, and factors involved in a task. | "I learned the footwork, the bag work, and everything that goes into it." |
| **It's been a journey** | Used to describe a significant experience with many challenges, ups and downs, and personal growth. | "Graduating from college has been a journey." |
| **Like a high** | A strong feeling of pleasure or excitement from an activity (not drugs). | "I get a high from skydiving." |
| **A rush** | A sudden, intense feeling of excitement or energy throughout your body. | "Before the competition, my body is in a rush." |
| **To keep a level head** | To remain calm in a stressful or difficult situation. | "You must keep a level head during negotiations." |
| **To be in the moment** | To be fully present and focused on the current experience without distractions. | "I need to be in the moment when I box." |
| **Cake** | A shortened version of "a piece of cake" (very easy). | "If you can survive being punched, the rest of the world is cake." |
| **There's only so much...** | There is a limit or maximum amount of something you can handle or do. | "There's only so much friendship you can build in one year." |
| **Once** | As soon as; when. | "Once I get a raise, I'll buy a car." |

## Grammar: Special Emphasis with Auxiliary Verbs

Native speakers often show strong agreement by repeating the auxiliary verb of the previous sentence.

- **Statement**: "It's been a long time." (It has been...)
  - **Response**: "It **has**." or "It **sure has**."
- **Statement**: "It was cold last night."
  - **Response**: "It **was**." or "It **sure was**."
- **Statement**: "They've been gone a long time."
  - **Response**: "They **sure have**."

## Practice Prompts

1. What is a hobby that you like to "dabble in"?
2. Describe a time in your life when "a lot was on the line." What happened?
3. Practice agreeing with emphasis: "That's a beautiful sunset." (Response: "It sure ______.")

# Top 10 Common Grammar Mistakes to Fix

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-grammar

## Summary

Even advanced learners make fossilized grammar mistakes. Lisa provides a test of 10 common sentences that sound correct to many learners but contain critical errors. This note breaks down the corrections and the rules behind them.

## The Corrections

### 1. "Such a" with Uncountable Nouns
- **Incorrect**: We had *such a* nice weather last week.
- **Correct**: We had **such nice weather** last week.
- **Rule**: Do not use "a/an" with uncountable nouns (like weather, advice, furniture) or plural nouns. You only use "such a" with singular countable nouns (*such a nice day*).

### 2. Subject vs. Object Pronouns
- **Incorrect**: They invited my wife and *I* to dinner.
- **Correct**: They invited my wife and **me** to dinner.
- **Rule**: "Me" is an object pronoun. If you remove "my wife and," you would never say "They invited I to dinner." You would say "They invited me." Therefore, it must be "my wife and me."

### 3. Have to vs. Don't Have to vs. Must Not
- **Incorrect**: My doctor told me I have to exercise and I *don't have to* smoke.
- **Correct**: My doctor told me I have to exercise and I **must not** smoke.
- **Rule**: "Don't have to" means it is your choice (you are not required to do it, but you can if you want). "Must not" means it is forbidden. 

### 4. Either... Or (Not Or... Or)
- **Incorrect**: I plan to study *or* biology *or* mathematics.
- **Correct**: I plan to study **either** biology **or** mathematics.
- **Rule**: In English, we do not repeat "or." We use "either... or" to present two options.

### 5. Matching Auxiliary Verbs with "Neither"
- **Incorrect**: "I don't speak French." -> "*Neither am I*."
- **Correct**: "I don't speak French." -> "**Neither do I**." (Or "**I don't either**.")
- **Rule**: The auxiliary verb in your response must match the tense of the statement. 
  - *I can't do it* -> *Neither can I.*
  - *I won't do it* -> *Neither will I.*
  - *I haven't done it* -> *Neither have I.*

### 6. Pluralizing Uncountable Nouns
- **Incorrect**: Do I have to do all of *these* difficult *homeworks*?
- **Correct**: Do I have to do all of **this** difficult **homework**?
- **Rule**: Homework, vocabulary, furniture, advice, and luggage are uncountable. They never take an "s", and you must use "this" instead of "these."

### 7. Present Perfect vs. Past Simple with Specific Times
- **Incorrect**: Have you ever been to NY? Yes, *I've been* there three years ago.
- **Correct**: Yes, **I was** there three years ago.
- **Rule**: If you specify exactly *when* something happened (last week, three years ago, yesterday), you must use the Past Simple, never the Present Perfect.

### 8. Let vs. Allow (The "To" Infinitive)
- **Incorrect**: Please let me *to* help you.
- **Correct**: Please **let me help** you.
- **Rule**: We do not use "to" after the verbs *let*, *have*, and *make*. (e.g., *I made him go. I let her drive.*) Most other verbs require "to" (e.g., *I allowed him to go. I permitted her to drive.*)

### 9. Have someone DO something
- **Incorrect**: You should have someone else *to do* it.
- **Correct**: You should **have someone else do** it.
- **Rule**: "To have someone do something" means to arrange for them to do it. It does not take a "to". (This is different from "I have to do it," which means obligation).

### 10. Remember vs. Remind
- **Incorrect**: I forgot his name, please *remember me* his name.
- **Correct**: I forgot his name, please **remind me** of his name.
- **Rule**: *Remember* is the opposite of forget (an internal mental action). *Remind* is when someone else helps you to remember something.

## Practice Prompts

1. Correct this sentence: "He bought three new furnitures for his house."
2. Correct this sentence: "I didn't watch the movie." -> "Neither was I."
3. Correct this sentence: "My boss made me to stay late."

<br/>

---

# Professional Intonation: Stop Using "Up Talk"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Intonation (the rise and fall of your pitch) dramatically affects how people perceive you. If you use the wrong intonation at work, you may sound insecure, immature, or unprofessional. In this lesson, Lisa explains the danger of "up talk" and how to sound like a confident leader.

## The Basic Rule of Intonation

1. **Rising Intonation (Pitch goes UP ↗)**: Used for asking questions.
   - *Example*: "Is he from China? ↗"
   - *Example*: "Has the meeting started yet? ↗"

2. **Falling Intonation (Pitch goes DOWN ↘)**: Used for statements, facts, and giving orders.
   - *Example*: "He's from mainland China. ↘"
   - *Example*: "The meeting has already started. ↘"

## The Problem: "Up Talk"

**"Up Talk"** is a bad habit where a speaker uses rising intonation at the end of a normal statement, making it sound like a question. 

*Example of Up Talk*: "The meeting is at 12:00? ↗ And you should bring a pen? ↗"

### Why is Up Talk bad?
- It makes you sound like you are asking for permission.
- It makes you sound insecure and unsure of what you are saying.
- It is highly associated with immature speech patterns (often used by young teenagers or certain influencers).
- In a professional environment, 85% of supervisors reported that up talk would hurt an employee's chances of getting a promotion. It destroys your authority.

## How to Sound Like a Leader

When making a statement, giving an opinion, or giving an instruction at work, your pitch MUST go down at the end of the sentence. Tell, don't ask.

Practice saying these sentences with a strong, confident, **falling pitch ↘** at the end:

1. "Let's start the meeting. ↘" (If you go up, it sounds like: *Is it okay if we start?*)
2. "I need you to do this for me. ↘" (You are telling, not asking).
3. "You made a few mistakes on this. ↘"
4. "We need to finish it by Friday. ↘"
5. "I believe this is our best option. ↘"

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying: "My name is [Name], and I am a [Profession]." Did your voice go up at the end, or down?
2. Practice giving a confident command: "Please send me the report by 5 PM." Ensure your pitch drops on "5 PM."

<br/>

category: "english-pronunciation"
theme: "vowels"

created: "2026-06-04"

---

# Vowel Reduction in Unstressed Syllables (The Schwa)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

One of the biggest differences between English and many other languages is how vowels are pronounced. In English, you do not pronounce every vowel clearly. You must stress one syllable and *reduce* the other vowels. If a syllable is unstressed, the vowel (a, e, i, o, u) almost always changes to an "uh" sound (the schwa).

## The Rule of Stress and Reduction

1. **Find the stressed syllable**: The vowel in this syllable is pronounced clearly and is held longer.
2. **Reduce the unstressed syllables**: The vowels in these syllables lose their spelling sound and become a short, weak "**uh**" sound.

If you pronounce every vowel equally, you will sound very unnatural. 

## Examples of Vowel Reduction

### 1. Words with "A"
- **Banana**: The stress is on the middle syllable (ba-NA-na). The first and last 'a' reduce to 'uh'. 
  - *Pronunciation*: buh-NA-nuh. (Not: ba-na-na).
- **Japan**: The stress is on the second syllable. The first 'a' reduces.
  - *Pronunciation*: juh-PAN. (Not: ja-pan).
- **Brazil**: The stress is on the second syllable. The 'a' reduces.
  - *Pronunciation*: bruh-ZIL. (Not: bra-zil).

### 2. Words with "E"
- **System**: The stress is on the first syllable. The second 'e' reduces.
  - *Pronunciation*: SIS-tuhm. (Not: sis-tem).
- **Problem**: The stress is on the first syllable. The 'e' reduces.
  - *Pronunciation*: PRAH-bluhm. (Not: prob-lem).
- **Sweden**: The stress is on the first syllable. The 'e' reduces.
  - *Pronunciation*: SWEE-duhn. (Not: swee-den).

### 3. Words with "I" / "O" / "U"
- **Belgium**: The stress is on the first syllable. The 'i' and 'u' blend into one reduced sound.
  - *Pronunciation*: BEL-juhm.
- **Finland**: The stress is on the first syllable. The 'a' reduces.
  - *Pronunciation*: FIN-luhnd.
- **Jordan**: The stress is on the first syllable. The 'a' reduces.
  - *Pronunciation*: JOR-duhn.

## Practice Sentences

Exaggerate the stress on the capitalized syllables, and make the reduced vowels very short "uh" sounds.

1. "That's a SPE-shuhl MEH-thuhd." (Special method)
2. "That's a MAH-duhrn SIS-tuhm." (Modern system)
3. "The DAWK-tuhr said it was a CAH-muhn PRAH-bluhm." (Doctor / common / problem)
4. "LIN-kuhn and EH-di-suhn are FAY-muhs uh-MEHR-i-kuhns." (Lincoln / Edison / famous Americans)

## Practice Prompts

1. Look at the word "President". Which syllable is stressed? How do the other 'e's sound?
2. Practice saying "Japan" and "Banana" ensuring the unstressed 'A's sound like "uh".

<br/>

# The 3 Sounds of "CH" in English

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

The letters "CH" in English are incredibly tricky because they can be pronounced three completely different ways depending on the origin of the word. Furthermore, in some words, the letters are completely silent! This note tests and categorizes the most difficult "CH" words.

## 1. The Standard "CH" Sound (tʃ)
This is the most common pronunciation (like in *child, teacher, much*).

- **Chief**: A leader.
- **Chick**: A baby bird.
- **Arch**: A curved structure.
- **Archery**: The sport of shooting a bow and arrow.
- **Champion**: A winner.
- **Channel**: A TV station or body of water.

## 2. The "SH" Sound (ʃ)
Words that have a French origin usually retain the "SH" sound.

- **Chef**: A professional cook.
- **Chic**: Elegant and stylish.
- **Chicago**: The US city. (Also note the state: **Illinois** has a silent 's' - pronounced Ill-in-oy).
- **Charlatan**: A fraud; a dishonest person pretending to have skills.
- **Chalet**: A mountain house. (The 't' is also silent).
- **Champagne**: The sparkling wine.
- **Chandelier**: A hanging crystal light fixture.
- **Chauffeur**: A person hired to drive a car.
- **Chivalry / Chivalrous**: Honorable, polite, gentlemanly behavior.
- **Michigan**: The US state.

## 3. The "K" Sound (k)
Words with Greek origins often pronounce "CH" as a hard "K".

- **Choir**: A group of singers.
- **Chaos / Chaotic**: Complete confusion.
- **Archeology**: The study of ancient societies.
- **Architect**: A person who designs buildings.
- **Ache / Headache / Stomachache**: Continuous pain.
- **Chemical / Chemistry / Chemotherapy**: Science and medical terms.
- **Chord**: A combination of musical notes. (Note: A **cord** is a wire or rope. They sound exactly the same).
- **Chronic**: Continuing for a long time (e.g., chronic pain).
- **Echo**: A repeating sound.

## 4. The Silent "CH"
- **Yacht**: A large, expensive boat. The 'ch' makes no sound at all. (Pronounced: yot).

## Bonus Rule: Schedule
This word is pronounced differently depending on the country:
- **American English**: Pronounced with a "SK" sound (*SKED-jool*).
- **British English**: Pronounced with a "SH" sound (*SHED-jool*).

## Practice Sentences

Read these out loud, paying close attention to the CH sounds:

1. The rich man from **Chicago** (*sh*) has a **chauffeur** (*sh*) and a **chef** (*sh*).
2. The **champion** (*ch*) celebrated with **champagne** (*sh*).
3. The **chef** (*sh*) works at a **chic** (*sh*) restaurant.
4. I heard the **echo** (*k*) from the **yacht** (*silent*).
5. **Charlie** (*ch*) sings in a **choir** (*k*) in **Chicago** (*sh*).
6. The **charlatan** (*sh*) caused complete **chaos** (*k*).

<br/>

category: "english-pronunciation"
theme: "consonants"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"

language: "en"
tags: ["english-pronunciation", "consonants", "mistakes", "youtube", "review"]
---

# Voiced vs. Voiceless Consonants

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Russian and German speakers often naturally un-voice consonants at the end of words (turning a 'v' into an 'f' or a 'd' into a 't'). This lesson teaches how to keep consonants voiced and how it affects the pronunciation of plurals.

## The Mistake: Dropping the Voice

A **voiced** consonant uses your vocal cords (you can feel the vibration in your throat). A **voiceless** consonant is just air.

Many languages automatically change a voiced consonant into a voiceless one at the end of a word. English does not. You must force the vibration until the very end.

| Voiced | Voiceless Mistake | Correct Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **V** (Vibration) | **F** (Air only) | **Five** (Not fife). **Twelve** (Not twelf). **Have** (Not haf). |
| **D** (Vibration) | **T** (Air only) | **Limited** (Not limitet). **Bed** (Not bet). |
| **J** (Vibration) | **CH** (Air only) | **Judge** (Not chuch/juch). |
| **G** (Vibration) | **K** (Air only) | **Egg** (Not ek). **Dog** (Not dok). |

*Warning*: If you drop the voice, you might change the meaning of the word entirely. 
- *Save* (Keep safe) vs. *Safe* (A secure box).
- *Bed* (Furniture) vs. *Bet* (Gambling).
- *Believe* (Verb) vs. *Belief* (Noun).

## The Plural "S" Rule

The voicing of the final consonant determines how you pronounce a plural 's'.

1. **If the word ends in a VOICED consonant**, the plural 's' is pronounced as a **Z**.
   - *Egg* ends in voiced 'g'. -> **Eggs** (eg-Z).
   - *Dog* ends in voiced 'g'. -> **Dogs** (dog-Z).
   - *Friend* ends in voiced 'd'. -> **Friends** (friend-Z).
   - *Believe* ends in voiced 'v'. -> He **believes** (believ-Z).

2. **If the word ends in a VOICELESS consonant**, the plural 's' remains an **S**.
   - *Cat* ends in voiceless 't'. -> **Cats** (cat-S).
   - *Let* ends in voiceless 't'. -> **Lets** (let-S).

## Practice Prompts

1. Put your hand on your throat. Say "FFFF" (no vibration) and then "VVVV" (vibration). Now say: "I have five dollars."
2. Read these words and pronounce the final "s": cats, dogs, friends, beds. Which ones sound like 'Z'?

<br/>

title: "English Pronunciation for Spanish Speakers"
description: "Learn how to stop adding an extra vowel sound in front of English words that start with the letter 'S'."
category: "english-pronunciation"
theme: "mistakes"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"
visibility: "public"
language: "en"

---

# Fixing the "Es-pecial" Mistake

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

A very common fossilized mistake for native Spanish speakers is adding an "E" sound in front of English words that begin with an 'S' followed by a consonant (like *sp, st, sl*). Lisa works with Frank from Spain to fix this habit using linking.

## The Problem

In Spanish, words do not naturally start with an 'S' + consonant. They always start with an 'E' (e.g., *España, estudiante*). 

Because of this, Spanish speakers unconsciously add an "eh" sound to English words:
- *Spain* becomes **Es-pain**.
- *Student* becomes **Es-tudent**.
- *Special* becomes **Es-pecial**.
- *Stomach* becomes **Es-tomach**.

## The Solution: Linking Words

The best way to force yourself to drop the extra "E" is to connect the 'S' word directly to the final sound of the word before it.

1. **"I'm sleeping"**
   - *Mistake*: "I am... es-leeping."
   - *Fix*: Connect the 'M' directly to the 'S'. Mmmmm-Ssss-leeping. **"I'm sleeping."**

2. **"It's special"**
   - *Mistake*: "It's... es-pecial."
   - *Fix*: Connect the final 'S' of "it's" to the beginning 'S' of "special". **"It's_special."**

3. **"He's strong"**
   - *Mistake*: "He is... es-trong."
   - *Fix*: Connect the 'Z' sound of "he's" to the 'S'. **"He's_strong."**

## Practice Sentences

Try reading these sentences out loud. Focus on immediately starting the 'S' sound without vibrating your vocal cords first. 

1. I want to **st**udy in the United **St**ates.
2. **Sp**anish is **sp**oken in **Sp**ain.
3. That **st**udent is **sm**art.
4. She's **sc**ared and she doesn't want to **st**ay here.
5. Don't **st**op **sm**iling.

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice the hardest phrase from the video: "Spanish is spoken in Spain."
2. What are 3 other English words that start with "St"? Practice saying them without an 'E' in front.

# 15 English Proverbs about Money

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Proverbs are short, well-known phrases that express a common truth. Lisa teaches 15 American proverbs about money. Before beginning, she corrects a very common pronunciation error: **Money** is pronounced with an "uh" sound (Muh-nee), not an "o" or "a" sound.

## The Proverbs

| Proverb | Meaning / Usage |
| --- | --- |
| **Money doesn't grow on trees.** | Money is hard to earn; it is not free. (Often said to children who want to buy things). |
| **Time is money.** | Time is valuable; do not waste it when you could be working or producing. |
| **Money talks.** | People with a lot of money have power and influence to get what they want. |
| **Money can't buy happiness.** | Wealth will not cure depression or guarantee long-term joy. |
| **The best things in life are free.** | Family, love, and health are more valuable than anything you can buy. |
| **Save it for a rainy day.** | Save your money for a future emergency or a difficult time in your life. |
| **Beggars can't be choosers.** | If you get something for free (or ask for help), you shouldn't complain about the quality of what you are given. |
| **You can't take it with you.** | When you die, your money stays behind. Don't be overly materialistic. |
| **There is no such thing as a free lunch.** | Nothing is truly free; there is always a hidden cost or a catch. |
| **A fool and his money are soon parted.** | Foolish people make bad financial decisions and lose their money quickly. |
| **You get what you pay for.** | If you buy something very cheap, it is probably going to be bad quality. |
| **Neither a borrower nor a lender be.** | Do not borrow money from friends, and do not lend it to them, or it will ruin the friendship. (From Shakespeare). |
| **Money makes the world go round.** | Money is essential; everything in society relies on it to function. |
| **The love of money is the root of all evil.** | Greed causes people to do terrible, criminal, and immoral things. (Biblical). |
| **It takes money to make money.** | You need initial capital or an investment if you want to start a business and generate wealth. |

## Practice Prompts

1. Do you agree that "money can't buy happiness"? Why or why not?
2. Tell a story about a time when you learned that "you get what you pay for."

# The 10 Negative Prefixes (Vocabulary Test)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

English has 10 different prefixes that mean "not" or express a negative opposite (un-, in-, im-, il-, ir-, a-, ab-, dis-, mis-, non-). Native speakers rarely say "not regular," they say "irregular." This note tests your knowledge of the correct prefixes for 20 advanced words.

## The Prefix Test Answers

### Prefix: UN-
- **Aware -> Unaware**: Not knowing about something. ("I was *unaware* of the situation.")
- **Caring -> Uncaring**: Unkind; not showing sympathy. ("His *uncaring* attitude shocked me.")
- **Reasonable -> Unreasonable**: Not fair or sensible. ("It's *unreasonable* to expect me to work Sunday.")
- **Reliable -> Unreliable**: Someone you cannot depend on. ("He was fired for being *unreliable*.")
- **Satisfied -> Unsatisfied**: Feeling unfulfilled (e.g., still hungry, or a goal not reached). 

### Prefix: IN-
- **Accurate -> Inaccurate**: Not correct; wrong. ("I apologize for the *inaccurate* information.")

### Prefix: DIS-
- **Prove -> Disprove**: To show that something is not true. ("You cannot *disprove* the existence of God.")
- **Respect -> Disrespect**: To treat rudely. ("I didn't intend to *disrespect* you.")
- **Satisfied -> Dissatisfied**: Unhappy or displeased with a situation or product. ("If you are *dissatisfied* with the product, return it.")

### Prefix: MIS-
- **Behave -> Misbehave**: To act badly (usually used for children). ("If you *misbehave*, I will call your parents.")
- **Fortune -> Misfortune**: Bad luck; an unfortunate event. ("Losing his house was a great *misfortune*.") Note: *Unfortunate* is the adjective, *misfortune* is the noun.
- **Judge -> Misjudge**: To have an unfair/wrong opinion, or to guess incorrectly. ("I completely *misjudged* him.")
- **Manage -> Mismanage**: To handle a business or situation badly. ("The CEO *mismanaged* the finances.")
- **Spell -> Misspell**: To spell a word incorrectly. ("I *misspelled* your name.")
- **Understand -> Misunderstand**: To interpret something wrongly. ("I feel very *misunderstood*.")

### Prefix: AB- / A-
- **Normal -> Abnormal**: Not normal. ("This warm weather is *abnormal* for December.")
- **Symptomatic -> Asymptomatic**: Having an illness but showing no symptoms. ("He has the virus but is *asymptomatic*.")

### Prefix: IR-
- **Repairable -> Irreparable**: Impossible to repair or rectify. (Pronounced: ih-REP-er-uh-bl). ("The scandal caused *irreparable* damage.")

### Prefix: NON-
- **Smoker -> Non-smoker**: A person who doesn't smoke. ("This section is for *non-smokers*.")
- **Toxic -> Non-toxic**: Not poisonous. ("I need to buy *non-toxic* paint.")

### Prefix: UN- (For Verbs)
- **Plug -> Unplug**: To disconnect from electricity. ("Try *unplugging* the router.")

## Practice Prompts

1. What is the difference between being "dissatisfied" and being "unsatisfied"?
2. Write a sentence using the word "irreparable" (e.g., irreparable damage).

description: "Learn the 5 critical reasons why word stress changes your meaning, your melody, and how well native speakers understand you."
category: "english-pronunciation"

mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"

---

# 5 Reasons Word Stress is Critical

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

If you pronounce every English sound perfectly but use the wrong rhythm and stress, native speakers will not understand you. In English, word stress holds meaning, intent, and emotion.

## Reason 1: You will be misunderstood.
A native speaker's brain listens for a specific melody. If you stress the wrong syllable, the word becomes unrecognizable.
- **Abbreviations**: Always stress the *last* letter. (e.g., M & **A**, U.C.L.**A.**, F.B.**I.**). If you say **M** & A, people won't know what you mean.
- **Syllable Stress**: A doctor asked, "Would you like a re-**FILL**?" and her patients were confused. She needed to stress the first syllable: "Would you like a **RE**-fill?"

## Reason 2: You will send the wrong message.
The word you choose to stress changes the emotional intent of the sentence.
- "I want to talk to you." (Neutral).
- "I want to talk to **YOU**." (I don't want to talk to anyone else; can sound dictatorial or angry).
- "**I** want to talk to you." (You might not want to, but I do).
- "I **WANT** to talk to you." (I really do! Believe me).

## Reason 3: You will sound like a robot.
If you stress every single word equally (which is common in some languages), you will sound like an automated computer. You will sound "unenthusiastic" and lack passion. English requires peaks (stressed words) and valleys (reduced words) to sound engaging.

## Reason 4: Stress forces you to slow down.
Many learners speak too fast. When you properly stress a word, you must open your mouth wider and hold the vowel longer. This automatically slows your pace down to a natural, understandable rhythm.
- *Example*: "When I joined a re-**CRUIT**-ing e-**VENT**... I talked to con-**SULT**-ing companies." (Holding those syllables forces clarity).

## Reason 5: The dictionary meaning changes.
In English, the same words can have entirely different definitions based on stress.
- **Compound Nouns**: The stress goes on the *first* word. 
  - "I live in the **White** House." (I am the President of the United States).
  - "I live in a white **house**." (My house is painted white).
- **Nouns vs. Verbs**: 
  - "**RE**-cord" (Noun - a vinyl disc).
  - "re-**CORD**" (Verb - to capture audio).
  - "**DE**-sert" (Noun - the Sahara).
  - "de-**SSERT**" (Noun - cake after dinner).

## Practice Prompts

1. Read this sentence three times, stressing the bold word to change the meaning: "**She** didn't steal the money." / "She didn't **steal** the money." / "She didn't steal the **money**."
2. Practice the abbreviation rule: Say "U.S.A", "C.E.O", and "A.T.M" while stressing the final letter loudly.

<br/>

# Fixing the "L" Sound (For Portuguese Speakers)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Portuguese (especially Brazilian Portuguese) speakers naturally round their lips and make a "W" or "U" sound when they see an "L" at the end of a word. In this lesson, Lisa and her student Roberto demonstrate how to keep the lips relaxed and move the tongue to correctly pronounce the dark "L".

## The Mistake: Rounding the Lips

If you are from Brazil, you probably say "Brazil" and it sounds like "Brazi-w" or "Brazi-u". 

- **Feel** sounds like "feew"
- **Call** sounds like "caw"
- **Will** sounds like "wiw"

This happens because you are pushing your lips forward and making them into a small circle (like saying "Oh" or "Ooh").

## The Fix: Neutral Lips, Tongue Up

To make a proper American "L" sound at the end of a word:
1. **Do NOT round your lips**. Keep them completely relaxed and neutral. Some teachers recommend slightly smiling to prevent the lips from pushing forward.
2. **Move the tip of your tongue UP**. Place it right behind your top front teeth. 

## Practice Prompts

Try reading these sentences out loud. Focus intensely on keeping your lips flat and relaxed, while tapping your tongue behind your top teeth for every 'L'.

1. I **will** **call** Paul.
2. How do you **feel**? (Response: I **feel** good).
3. I **will** **mail** it.
4. Just a **little**.

# The "N" vs "NG" Sounds (For Chinese Speakers)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Many Chinese speakers drop the "N" sound entirely when it appears at the end of an English word. Furthermore, distinguishing between "N" and "NG" at the end of a word (like *thin* vs. *thing*) is extremely difficult. Lisa breaks down exactly what your tongue should be doing.

## The Problem with "N"

When an "N" is at the beginning of a word (*never*) or middle (*Canada*), Chinese speakers pronounce it perfectly. 

However, when it is at the end of a word (*can, man, seven*), Chinese speakers often keep their tongue down. The result is that the "N" disappears completely, and "can" sounds like "ca".

## How to Fix the "N"
To pronounce the "N" at the end of a word:
1. Move the tip of your tongue **UP** to the roof of your mouth, just behind your teeth.
2. Keep the tongue up, and push the air out through your **nose**. 
3. *Example*: "yes I ca-nnn" (hold the tongue up).

### Linking the "N"
A great trick is to link the final "N" to a vowel in the next word. Because Chinese speakers are good at middle-N sounds, linking solves the problem.
- Instead of "can I", connect it: **Ca-nI**.
- Instead of "when I", connect it: **Whe-nI**.

## "N" vs. "NG"

The difference between these two sounds is entirely based on *which part* of the tongue goes up.

| Sound | Tongue Position | Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **N** | The **FRONT** of the tongue goes up. | Thi**n**, si**n**, ru**n** |
| **NG** | The **BACK** of the tongue goes up (like you are about to make a 'G' sound). The front is relaxed. | Thi**ng**, si**ng**, ru**ng** |

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice linking the 'N': Say "When are you going?" as "Whe-nare you going?"
2. Exaggerate the tongue movements: Say "young son". (For *young*, the back of the tongue goes up. For *son*, the front of the tongue goes up).
3. Say these pairs and focus on the tongue position: Thin / Thing. Sin / Sing.

# 8 Classic English Proverbs

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Proverbs are short, traditional sayings that offer wisdom or advice. Native speakers use them constantly in daily life. This note covers 8 essential proverbs that you need to know to achieve full fluency.

## The Proverbs

### 1. Don't make a mountain out of a molehill.
- **Meaning**: Do not exaggerate a minor problem into a huge crisis. 
- **Usage**: "It's just a small scratch on the car. Don't make a mountain out of a molehill."
- **Pronunciation Note**: Native speakers often "hold" the 'T' in mountain (moun-n), they do not clearly pronounce it (moun-tin).

### 2. Look before you leap.
- **Meaning**: Think carefully about the consequences before you make a big decision.
- **Usage**: "I know you want to quit your job and start a business, but look before you leap."

### 3. Let sleeping dogs lie.
- **Meaning**: Leave a situation as it is to avoid causing conflict; do not unnecessarily create a problem by talking about a sensitive issue.
- **Usage**: "She didn't mention the argument from last week, so I decided to let sleeping dogs lie."

### 4. All that glitters isn't gold.
- **Meaning**: Just because something looks beautiful or valuable on the outside, doesn't mean it actually is.
- **Usage**: "That cheap watch looks great, but all that glitters isn't gold."

### 5. Don't bite off more than you can chew.
- **Meaning**: Do not take on too much work or responsibility that you cannot handle.
- **Usage**: "I took a second job and signed up for three classes. I bit off more than I could chew."

### 6. You can lead a horse to water, but you can't make him drink.
- **Meaning**: You can give someone an opportunity or advice, but you cannot force them to take it or accept your help.
- **Usage**: "I got him an interview, but he refused to go. You can lead a horse to water, but you can't make him drink."

### 7. It's no use crying over spilled milk.
- **Meaning**: Do not waste time being upset about a past accident or mistake that cannot be changed.
- **Usage**: "I accidentally deleted the file. Well, there's no use crying over spilled milk. I'll just rewrite it." (Note: *spilled* in US English, *spilt* in UK English).

### 8. We'll cross that bridge when we come to it.
- **Meaning**: Do not worry about a potential future problem until it actually happens. Deal with it later.
- **Usage**: "What if it rains on the day of the wedding?" "We'll cross that bridge when we come to it."

## Practice Prompts

1. Think of a proverb in your native language that means the exact same thing as "Look before you leap."
2. Have you ever "bitten off more than you could chew"? What happened?

<br/>

# Advanced Vocabulary: Real Conversation (Pets & Business)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-vocabulary

## Summary

Lisa interviews Janetta, the owner of a dog-walking business in Los Angeles called "Spoiled Pooch." They discuss pets, business, and Los Angeles culture using highly natural expressions. 

## Animal Vocabulary

| Word | Meaning | Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Pooch** | Slang/informal word for "dog." | "That's a cute little pooch." |
| **Spoiled** | Treated very well, often with too much generosity. | "He behaves like a spoiled child." |
| **Bunny** | An informal word for a rabbit (often used by or with children). | "We do cat and bunny visits." |
| **Fur child / Fur baby** | A pet that an owner loves and treats like a human baby. | "He's not just a dog; he's my fur baby." |
| **To roam** | To wander or walk around freely without a clear direction. | "The dogs used to roam on 10 acres of land." |
| **Breed** | A specific type of dog or animal (e.g., German Shepherd, Poodle). | "What breed is your dog?" |
| **A rescue** | (Noun). An adopted dog that was previously homeless or abandoned. | "Her dog is a rescue." |

## Business & Daily Expressions

1. **Clientele**: A group or type of customers. "That store attracts wealthy clientele."
2. **Interestingly enough**: Used to introduce a fact that is unexpected or interesting. "Interestingly enough, the hardest part was the interview."
3. **That's an "L.A." thing**: Something that is highly associated with a specific city or culture. "Treating dogs like babies is an L.A. thing."
4. **That's my thing / That's not my thing**: Something you enjoy or do not enjoy. "Hiking just isn't my thing."
5. **The utmost**: The absolute greatest amount or highest degree. "We treat the dogs with the utmost care." "This is of utmost importance."
6. **A quirk**: An unusual habit or slightly strange personality trait. "Everyone has their own quirks."
7. **There's more to it**: It is more complicated than it appears. "Dog walking looks easy, but there's more to it than I thought."
8. **Transportation (L.A. specific)**: In Los Angeles, asking if you have "reliable transportation" specifically means asking if you have a working car. (In NY, it means the subway).
9. **Trustworthy**: Someone you can trust; honest and reliable. "You must be trustworthy to work in someone's home."
10. **To fill in**: To do someone else's job temporarily because they are absent. "Can you fill in for me on Saturday?"
11. **Words to live by**: Great life advice to follow forever. "Find something you love, and you'll never work a day in your life. Those are words to live by."
12. **Instagram worthy**: A photograph or location that looks beautiful enough to be posted on social media. "The lighting makes this photo Instagram worthy."

## Practice Prompts

1. Are dogs in your country treated like "fur babies," or is that just an "L.A. thing"?
2. What is a piece of advice you received that you consider "words to live by"?

title: "English word stress with Idioms | Accurate English"

category: "english-pronunciation"

mood: "study"

# Word Stress: The "Adjective + Noun" Rule

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Many English learners do not feel confident because they lack natural rhythm. A huge secret to natural rhythm is knowing whether to stress the adjective or the noun. This note covers the primary rule and applies it to four essential idioms.

## The Rule: Stress the Noun!

In English, when you have an adjective followed by a noun, **you must stress the noun more than the adjective**. 

Many languages stress the adjective. If you do this in English, you will sound very unnatural. 

*Exceptions*: You only stress the adjective if you are correcting someone (e.g., "I wanted the *blue* sweater, not the green one!").

### Examples:
- "That's a new **house**." (Not: *new* house).
- "That's a big **room**." (Not: *big* room).
- "You did a good **job**." (Not: *good* job).

## Idioms Using the "Adjective + Noun" Rule

Practice saying these common idioms, making sure to hit the noun hard and reduce the adjective. 

### 1. Once in a blue MOON.
- **Meaning**: Very rarely; almost never.
- **Practice**: "I see my old friend once in a blue **moon**." (Stress "moon", do not stress "blue").

### 2. No big DEAL.
- **Meaning**: It's okay; it's not a problem; it's not serious.
- **Practice**: "We had a little disagreement, but it's no big **deal**." 

### 3. On the same PAGE.
- **Meaning**: Two people are thinking the same way or have the same understanding of a situation.
- **Practice**: "They are on the same **page** about wanting a small wedding."

### 4. Come a long WAY.
- **Meaning**: To have made a lot of good progress compared to the past.
- **Practice**: "You've come a long **way** in only 3 months."

## Practice Prompts

1. Complete this sentence out loud, remembering to stress the noun: "I love the food at that new _______."
2. Write a sentence using the idiom "on the same page."

<br/>

category: "english-pronunciation"

mood: "study"

---

# 16 Commonly Mispronounced Words

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

Even advanced English speakers struggle with the pronunciation of these 16 words. This guide highlights the silent letters, difficult clusters, and tricky vowel shifts you need to know.

## Tricky Consonants and Silent Letters

| Word | Pronunciation Notes |
| --- | --- |
| **Months** | Most native speakers drop the "th" entirely. Just say **muns**. ("two muns"). |
| **Phlegm** | The 'ph' is an 'f' sound, and the 'g' is silent. **Flem**. |
| **Salmon** | The 'L' is completely silent. **Sam-un**. |
| **Subtle** | The 'b' is completely silent. **Suh-dl** (with a flap-t). |
| **Comb / Bomb / Tomb** | The 'b' is silent in all three. Note the vowels: Comb = o, Bomb = ah, Tomb = ooh. |
| **Sixth** | Very difficult cluster. Make a long 's', then slide your tongue to a 'th'. **Siks-th**. |
| **Water** | Round lips for 'W', open wide for 'AH', use a fast-D (flap-T). **Wah-der**. |

## Word Stress & Vowel Shifts

| Word | Pronunciation Notes |
| --- | --- |
| **Literature** | Lih-der-uh-chur. (Fast-D, schwa sound). |
| **Maintenance** | Main-te-nens. Hold the middle 't'. Do not say "maintain-ance". |
| **Photograph vs. Photographer** | **PHO-to-graph** (Stress 1st syllable, 'O' sound). **pho-TOG-ra-pher** (Stress 2nd syllable, 'AH' sound). |
| **Regularly** | Reg-you-ler-ly. Do not say "reg-ooh-lar". |
| **Theater** | Thee-uh-der. (Fast-D). |
| **War vs. Wore** | They are pronounced exactly the same. **Wore**. |
| **Were vs. Where** | They are NOT the same. *Were* sounds like *wer* (silent e). *Where* sounds like *wear*. ("Where were they?"). |
| **One vs. Won** | They are pronounced exactly the same. ("Wun"). |
| **Woman vs. Women** | **Woman** = Wuh-man (like book). **Women** = Wih-min (like big). |

## Practice Prompts

1. Read this sentence out loud: "The photographer took a photograph of the salmon."
2. Read this sentence out loud: "Where were the women going?"

<br/>

---
title: "Fix Your Grammar Mistakes - Don't Add an S to These Common Words"
description: "Learn which nouns are uncountable and never take an 'S', including common mistakes like 'informations', 'advices', and 'luggages'."
category: "english-grammar"
theme: "nouns"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"
visibility: "public"
language: "en"
tags: ["english-grammar", "nouns", "mistakes", "youtube", "review"]
---

# Stop Adding "S" to Uncountable Nouns

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-grammar

## Summary

One of the most common mistakes advanced English learners make is adding an "S" to pluralize nouns that are uncountable or irregular. You cannot say "informations" or "advices". 

## The "Never Add S" List (Uncountable Nouns)

The following words are uncountable. You cannot add an "S", and you must use a singular verb (e.g., *is*, not *are*).

1. **Luggage**: "I have three pieces of luggage." (Not: *luggages*). Note: *Suitcase* is countable (suitcases).
2. **Advice**: "She gave me some good advice." (Not: *advices*). Note: *Suggestion* is countable (suggestions).
3. **Vocabulary**: "I want to learn more vocabulary." (Not: *vocabularies*). Note: *Word* is countable (words).
4. **Slang**: "He uses a lot of slang." (Not: *slangs*).
5. **Equipment**: "The gym has new equipment." (Not: *equipments*).
6. **Furniture**: "I bought two pieces of furniture." (Not: *furnitures*).
7. **Jewelry**: "She bought some beautiful jewelry." (Not: *jewelries*).
8. **Information**: "He gave me a piece of information." (Not: *informations*).
9. **Homework**: "I finished my homework." (Not: *homeworks*). Note: *Assignment* is countable (assignments).
10. **Clothing**: "The store sells clothing." (Not: *clothings*). Note: *Clothes* is always plural. 

> [!TIP]
> **How to count them:**
> If you need to specify a number, use a "unit word" like "a piece of" or "an item of". 
> *Correct*: "Two pieces of advice," "Three items of clothing," "Four pieces of luggage."

## Irregular Animals (Plural without S)

Some animal words are both singular and plural. Never add an "S".
- **Fish**: One fish, ten fish. (*Fishes* is only used when talking about multiple scientific *species* of fish).
- **Deer**: One deer, five deer. (Not: *deers*).
- **Sheep**: One sheep, a hundred sheep. (Not: *sheeps*).
- **Moose**: One moose, two moose. (Not: *mooses*).
- **Mice**: One mouse, three mice. (Not: *mouses*).

## Practice Prompts

1. Fix the error: "Thank you for all of your good advices and informations."
2. Translate this idea into correct English: "I have too many luggages to carry."

<br/>

category: "english-grammar"

created: "2026-06-04"

---

# The Ultimate Prepositions Guide

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-grammar

## Summary

Prepositions are often considered the hardest part of learning English because they rarely translate directly from your native language. This guide covers the rules for places, dates, and the specific prepositions that must follow certain adjectives and verbs.

## Places, Addresses, and Dates

| Scenario | Rule | Examples |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Cities / Enclosed Spaces** | Use **IN** | *in* New York, *in* the building. |
| **Specific Locations / Activities** | Use **AT** | *at* the airport, *at* the bus stop. |
| **Street Names (No number)** | Use **ON** | I live *on* Main Street. |
| **Full Addresses (With number)** | Use **AT** | I live *at* 537 Main Street. |
| **Months** | Use **IN** | Her birthday is *in* April. |
| **Specific Dates** | Use **ON** | Her birthday is *on* April 10th. |

*Note*: "Let's meet *at* the restaurant" means the location (maybe outside). "Let's meet *in* the restaurant" means physically inside the building.

## Adjectives + Prepositions

You must memorize which prepositions follow these common adjectives:
- **Good / Bad / Terrible AT**: "I am bad *at* math."
- **Known FOR**: "She is known *for* her beautiful voice."
- **Married / Engaged TO**: "She is married *to* John." (Never use *with*!).
- **Disappointed IN (a person)**: "I am disappointed *in* you."
- **Disappointed WITH (a thing)**: "I was disappointed *with* the movie."
- **Absent FROM**: "He was absent *from* work yesterday."
- **Dressed IN**: "He is dressed *in* black."
- **Composed OF**: "Water is composed *of* hydrogen and oxygen."

## Verbs + Prepositions

- **Consist OF**: "The meal consists *of* rice."
- **Discriminate AGAINST**: "It's illegal to discriminate *against* employees."
- **Decide ON**: "I can't decide *on* which restaurant to go to."
- **Decide AGAINST (+ ing)**: "I decided *against* buying the car." (Meaning: I decided not to).
- **Plan TO (+ verb)**: "I plan *to* finish it." (A definite intention).
- **Plan ON (+ ing)**: "I plan *on* finishing it." (An expectation or preparation).

### Made Of vs. From vs. Out Of
- **Made OF**: The original material is still visible. ("The table is made *of* wood").
- **Made FROM**: The original material is transformed and no longer visible. ("Wine is made *from* grapes").
- **Made OUT OF**: Emphasizes creativity or unusual use. ("She made a heart *out of* paper").

## Practice Prompts

1. Fill in the blanks: "He is married ____ Sarah, and they live ____ 123 Elm Street."
2. Fill in the blanks: "I am terrible ____ tennis, so I decided ____ playing in the tournament."

<br/>

---

# The Short "I" vs. Long "E"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

In many languages (like Russian), there is only one "E" sound. In English, there are two distinct sounds: the long, tense "E" (*beat*) and the short, relaxed "I" (*bit*). Mixing these up can completely change the meaning of what you are trying to say.

## The Long "E" (Tense)

- **How to make it**: Your tongue is high, and your lips are stretched into a slight smile. The muscles in your face are tense.
- **Spelling**: Usually spelled with **EE** or **EA**.
- **Examples**: B**ea**t, s**ea**t, h**ea**t, f**ee**l, sh**ee**p, l**ea**ve. 

## The Short "I" (Relaxed)

- **How to make it**: Your tongue is slightly lower, your jaw drops a tiny bit, and your lips are completely relaxed. It is very fast.
- **Spelling**: Usually spelled with the letter **I**.
- **Examples**: B**i**t, s**i**t, h**i**t, f**i**ll, sh**i**p, l**i**ve. 

> [!WARNING]
> If you use the tense Long E instead of the relaxed Short I, you will say the wrong word:
> - "Where do you **live**?" -> "Where do you **leave**?"
> - "Fill the glass." -> "**Feel** the glass."
> - "Look at that **ship**." -> "Look at that **sheep**."
> - "I was **hit**." -> "I was **heat**."

## Rule: Endings are always Long E

When an English word ends in an "E" or "Y" sound, it is **always** the long, tense "E" sound. There are no English words that end in the short, relaxed "I" sound.

- **Examples**: H**e**, w**e**, bus**y**, happ**y**, cazz**y**.

## Practice Prompts

1. Say this pair out loud, feeling the tension in your lips change: Heat / Hit. 
2. Say this sentence out loud, distinguishing the words: "Where do you live, and when do you leave?"

---

# The 3 Essential Comma Rules

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-grammar

## Summary

Good writing requires good punctuation, but comma rules in English are often different from other languages. This guide covers the three primary rules that confuse non-native speakers: relative clauses, subordinating conjunctions, and coordinating conjunctions.

## Rule 1: Relative Clauses (Who, Which, That)

A relative clause gives more information about a noun. You only use commas if the information is "extra" and not essential to the sentence.

1. **Non-Restrictive (Extra Info) = COMMAS**
   - *Example*: "My uncle, who is a dentist, lives in New York."
   - *Why*: If you take out the clause ("My uncle lives in New York"), the sentence still makes perfect sense. The clause is just bonus information.
   
2. **Restrictive (Essential Info) = NO COMMAS**
   - *Example*: "The students who study hard will get an A."
   - *Why*: If you take out the clause ("The students will get an A"), the meaning of the sentence completely changes. The clause is essential to identifying *which* students.

## Rule 2: Subordinating Conjunctions

Subordinating conjunctions (*because, after, although, before, even if, whenever*) attach a dependent clause to an independent clause. 

1. **Conjunction in the middle = NO COMMA**
   - *Example*: "I couldn't call you yesterday because I was studying."
2. **Conjunction at the beginning = COMMA in the middle**
   - *Example*: "Because I was studying, I couldn't call you yesterday."

## Rule 3: Coordinating Conjunctions (FANBOYS)

Coordinating conjunctions (*For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So*) connect things together. 

1. **Connecting two complete sentences = COMMA REQUIRED**
   - *Example*: "He speaks English well, but he needs to work on his writing."
   - *Why*: Both halves of the sentence have a subject and verb and could stand alone as separate sentences.
2. **Second half is incomplete = NO COMMA**
   - *Example*: "He likes to watch movies and play video games."
   - *Why*: "play video games" does not have a subject, so it cannot stand alone as a sentence. Do not use a comma before 'and'.

## Practice Prompts

1. Does this sentence need a comma? "Los Angeles which is a big city has a lot of traffic."
2. Does this sentence need a comma? "I was hungry so I ate an apple."
