---

# Understanding Jokes and Wordplay

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

Humor is often the hardest part of a new language to understand because it relies on cultural context and double meanings. If you understand these jokes, your English is highly advanced.

## Vocabulary for Jokes

- **To get it**: To understand a joke. ("I don't get it.")
- **To go over my head**: To completely miss the humor or meaning. ("Everyone laughed, but it went over my head.")
- **The punchline**: The final sentence or phrase that makes the joke funny.
- **To butcher a joke**: To ruin a joke by telling it badly or forgetting the details.
- **To crack up**: To laugh very hard. ("That movie cracked me up.")
- **Corny**: Silly, old-fashioned, or predictable. ("A corny joke.")
- **A dad joke**: A clean, corny joke that relies on obvious wordplay. Fathers famously tell them.

## Jokes with Double Meanings (Idioms)

1. **Why don't skeletons fight each other? Because they don't have the guts.**
   - *Guts*: The inside of a stomach (which skeletons don't have).
   - *Idiom*: To have the courage to do something.

2. **Why was the calendar nervous? Because its days were numbered.**
   - *Literal*: A calendar has numbers on it.
   - *Idiom*: If your days are numbered, something bad is going to happen to you soon (you will be fired, die, etc.).

3. **Why do cows wear bells? Because their horns don't work.**
   - *Horns*: Animal horns vs. car horns.

4. **Why did the scarecrow win an award? Because he was outstanding in his field.**
   - *Outstanding in his field*: Being excellent at a specific profession.
   - *Literal*: A scarecrow is physically standing outside in a farm field.

5. **Why are frogs so happy? Because they eat whatever bugs them.**
   - *Bugs*: Insects (which frogs eat).
   - *Idiom*: To "bug" someone means to annoy or bother them.

6. **Why did the cookie go to the doctor? Because it felt crummy.**
   - *Crummy*: Made of crumbs/falling apart.
   - *Idiom*: To feel "crummy" is a casual way to say you feel sick or unwell.

7. **What did the grape say when stepped on? Nothing. It just let out a little wine.**
   - *Wine / Whine*: Homophones. Wine is the drink. To "whine" means to complain in an annoying, high-pitched voice.

8. **I stayed up all night wondering where the sun went. Then it dawned on me.**
   - *Dawn*: Sunrise.
   - *Idiom*: If something "dawns on" you, you suddenly realize or understand it.

9. **Did you hear about the restaurant on the moon? Great food, but no atmosphere.**
   - *Atmosphere*: Oxygen/air around a planet.
   - *Atmosphere*: The mood, ambiance, or design of a restaurant.

## Practice Prompts

1. Tell a "dad joke" from your native country, and explain why it is funny.
2. Write a sentence using the expression "it dawned on me."

title: "Improve Your American Accent Fast - 4 TIPS"
description: "Four highly effective, practical tips to immediately improve your American accent and speak more clearly in professional settings."
category: "english-pronunciation"

mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"

---

# 4 Tips for a Better Accent

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

This lesson features an interview with a communication coach and a live coaching session with a postdoctoral scholar, providing four actionable tips for sounding clear and confident.

## Tip 1: Master Key Workplace Words
Make a list of the 30-50 words you use every day in your profession (medical terms, engineering terms, company names). Make sure you know exactly which syllable to stress.
- *Example*: Develop (de-**VEL**-op, not **DE**-vel-op).
- *Example*: Refill (**RE**-fill, not re-**FILL**).

## Tip 2: Slow Down
Many non-native speakers speak too fast because they think it makes them sound more fluent. 
- Fast speech often results in missing or muffled consonant sounds.
- It takes physical time to move your tongue and lips between complex sounds (e.g., from the "v" to the "m" in *five minutes*).
- **Professional speech is clear speech.** Slowing down makes you sound more authoritative.

## Tip 3: Stress Content Words, Not Function Words
Do not apply strong stress to pronouns (*I, me, you*) or prepositions (*to, for, at*). Stress the **verbs** and **nouns** (the content words) because they carry the meaning.
- *Incorrect*: **I** agree with **YOU**. (This implies no one else agrees).
- *Correct*: I **AGREE** with you. 

## Tip 4: Change Your Mindset
Do not be paralyzed by a fear of being judged for your accent.
- You are speaking because you are a hired expert in your field; you deserve to be there.
- Most people do not care about your accent as long as you are speaking clearly.
- Ignore negative comments from insecure or jealous individuals. Speak with a strong, confident voice.

## Practice Prompts

1. Record yourself saying this sentence quickly, and then very slowly: "Professional speech is clear speech."
2. Write down five long, difficult words that are specific to your job or major. Which syllable is stressed in each?

category: "english-pronunciation"

---

# Listening for the Letter "O"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

To improve your pronunciation, you must first improve your listening skills. The letter "O" is particularly tricky in English because it makes several very different sounds.

## The Unstressed "O" (The Schwa)
When the letter "O" is in an unstressed syllable, it usually reduces to a relaxed "uh" sound (the schwa). 
- doct**o**r (doc-t*uh*r)
- c**o**ntrol (c*uh*n-trol)

## The Stressed "O": 3 Possibilities
When an "O" is in the stressed syllable, it can make one of three sounds:
1. **The "AH" Sound**: D**o**ctor (*dahk-ter*). Open your mouth wide.
2. **The "OH" Sound**: Contr**o**l (*cun-trohl*).
3. **The "UH" Sound**: C**o**lor (*cuh-ler*).

> [!TIP]
> Do not rely strictly on spelling! The only way to know the correct pronunciation is to listen to native speakers or check a phonetic dictionary.

## Practice Prompts

1. Look at the word "photography". Which "O" is stressed? Which one is reduced to a schwa?
2. How is the "O" pronounced in the word "London"? (Ah, Oh, or Uh?)

<br/>

category: "study-skills"

---

# Powerful Vocabulary Building Strategies

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: study-skills

## Summary

Learning random lists of words is not an effective way to achieve fluency. This lesson outlines sustainable, long-term habits for acquiring a rich and powerful vocabulary.

## 4 Tips for Vocabulary Acquisition

### 1. Read a Novel in English
Do not just read textbooks. Find a fiction novel that truly excites you. Because you want to know what happens in the story, you will be highly motivated to keep reading, even when it is difficult. 

### 2. Use a Physical, Paper Dictionary
Using an online dictionary is fast, but it is often a passive experience. Using an "old school" paper dictionary provides several benefits:
- You can physically underline words when you learn them.
- When you leaf through the pages later, you see all the words you underlined a year ago, which is highly motivating.
- You see related root words directly above and below the word you are searching for.
- *The Dot Method*: Put a pencil dot next to a word. If you look it up again and see a dot, you know you forgot it. After two or three dots, write it in a dedicated notebook.

### 3. Immediately Make a Sentence
When you look up a new word, do not just read the definition and close the book. Say the word out loud and immediately use it in an original sentence related to your own life.

### 4. Read a Wide Variety of Subjects
If you only read business books, you will struggle to talk about politics, art, or everyday events. Read articles on diverse topics so you can converse with anyone about anything.

## Practice Prompts

1. What does the expression "old school" mean? Use it in a sentence.
2. Why is an online dictionary sometimes less effective for long-term memory than a paper dictionary?

---

# Does Perfect Grammar Matter?

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: study-skills

## Summary

There is a long-standing debate: should learners focus heavily on grammar, or just aim to communicate? The answer depends entirely on your goals.

## When Grammar Matters
1. **Academic Settings**: Universities require excellent grammar for essays, research papers, and grades.
2. **Professional Settings**: Studies show that professionals with strong grammar get promoted more frequently. Employers trust employees with good grammar to handle client emails and reports.

## When Grammar Doesn't Matter (As Much)
If you are traveling as a tourist, ordering in a restaurant, or casually chatting with friends online, perfect grammar is unnecessary. The only goal is basic communication.

> [!NOTE]
> Do not let a fear of making mistakes paralyze you or stop you from speaking. However, you should still strive for excellence. Do not settle for "good enough" if your goal is true professional fluency. 

## Mistakes Native Speakers Make

Native speakers do make grammar mistakes, but they are usually different from the mistakes ESL learners make. Native speakers often confuse homophones or use overly casual structures.

1. **Adjectives vs. Adverbs**:
   - *Incorrect*: He speaks English good. Please drive careful.
   - *Correct*: He speaks English **well**. Please drive **carefully**.
2. **Pronoun Subject vs. Object**:
   - *Incorrect*: Me and her went to the store. / He asked my friend and I to help.
   - *Correct*: **She and I** went to the store. / He asked my friend and **me** to help.
3. **Past Simple vs. Past Participle**:
   - *Incorrect*: I have never swam there. / He could have forgot.
   - *Correct*: I have never **swum** there. / He could have **forgotten**.
4. **Possessive Apostrophes**:
   - *My parents' house* (Apostrophe after the 's' for plurals).
   - *My mother's house* (Apostrophe before the 's' for singulars).
5. **Homophones (Spelling Mistakes)**:
   - Writing *should of* instead of *should have*.
   - Confusing *they're*, *their*, and *there*. 

## Practice Prompts

1. Correct this sentence: "Me and my brother play tennis real good."
2. Write a sentence explaining why good grammar is important in your specific career field.

# Real Conversations: 10 Idioms in LA

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson features idioms and advanced vocabulary extracted from an interview with Michael, a videographer attending a major restaurant event in Los Angeles.

## Vocabulary & Idioms

1. **To debut** (*day-byoo*): To introduce something to the public for the first time. The "T" is silent. ("The restaurant is debuting its new menu tonight.")
2. **Out of this world**: Absolutely amazing or fantastic. ("The sushi was out of this world.")
3. **To check [something] out**: To investigate or examine something you are interested in. ("I need to go check out that new coffee shop.")
4. **I got to [verb]**: I had the opportunity/chance to do something. ("I got to try some amazing local brews.") *Note: Do not confuse with "I've got to", which means "I must."*
5. **A [noun] snob**: Someone who likes high-quality things and knows a lot about a specific subject (e.g., a *beer snob*, a *coffee snob*, a *music snob*).
6. **To stick with me**: To stay in my memory; not easy to forget. ("That incredible meal will stick with me for a while.")
7. **The [noun] scene**: The current events and culture of a specific community in a city. ("The LA food scene is very diverse.")
8. **Background**: Your past, history, or childhood. ("Tell me about your professional background.")
9. **Upbringing**: How your parents raised you; how you grew up. ("His strong work ethic is a result of his upbringing.")
10. **A mishmash**: A confused or unique mixture of different things.

## Practice Prompts

1. Are you a "snob" about anything (coffee, chocolate, music)? Explain why.
2. What is the difference between your "background" and your "upbringing"?

# 13 Professional English Expressions

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

If you want to sound confident and fluent at work, you must learn the specific expressions native speakers use in business settings. This lesson breaks down an interview with Ross, a Public Relations (PR) specialist.

## Expressions for the Workplace

1. **In the field**: Working in a specific profession. ("I have been in the PR field for 8 years.")
2. **To get into it**: To start or enter a profession. ("I got into PR by accident.")
3. **To line up**: To arrange, plan, or schedule something. ("I have three job interviews lined up for next week.")
4. **To back out (on someone)**: To cancel a promise or agreement. ("The company backed out of the job offer at the last minute.")
5. **Skill set**: The specific combination of skills needed for a job. ("He doesn't have the right skill set for this position.")
6. **To be on deadline**: To have to finish a task by a strict time limit. ("Journalists are always working on deadline.")
7. **To be strong AT [something]**: To be very good at something. *Note: Do not say "strong IN" or "good IN".*
8. **To move my way up**: To get promoted; to climb the corporate ladder. ("I started as an intern and moved my way up.")
9. **Time-sensitive**: Urgent; must be done by a specific deadline. ("These documents are time-sensitive and must be signed today.")
10. **Supply and demand**: The economic relationship between the availability of a product/job and the desire for it. ("The supply of writers is overwhelming the demand.")
11. **X, Y, or Z**: Used when giving a list of hypothetical examples. ("Our product is better because of X, Y, or Z.")
12. **In a [adjective] fashion**: In a particular manner or way. ("Please handle this in a professional fashion.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe your "skill set" using at least three specific abilities you have.
2. What is a "time-sensitive" task you have to complete this week?

# 14 Idioms for Overcoming Difficulty

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This is part two of an interview with Drake, a restaurant worker in LA, filmed during a period of high unemployment. He uses several idiomatic expressions to describe how he stays positive and productive during hard times.

## Idioms of Resilience and Positivity

1. **To pull together**: To work hard as a group to achieve something. ("We need to pull together to fix the economy.")
   - *Alternative*: **Pull yourself together** (Calm down emotionally).
2. **Moving forward**: Looking toward the future; progressing. ("We need to be careful moving forward.")
3. **Mindset**: Attitude; how you see the world. ("You need to keep a positive mindset.")
4. **There's no use in [verb+ing]**: It is not helpful; it's useless. ("There's no use in panicking.")
5. **To pick up [a hobby]**: To start learning a new skill or hobby. ("I just picked up painting.")
6. **A bunch of**: A lot of. ("I bought a bunch of paint and canvases.")
7. **To lighten the mood**: To make the atmosphere less serious and more happy. ("I told a joke to lighten the mood.")
8. **Day-to-day life**: Your normal, everyday routine.
9. **For the time being**: Temporarily; for now. ("The gym is closed for the time being.")
10. **To fall out of shape**: To lose your physical fitness because you stopped exercising.
11. **To weather the storm**: To survive and successfully deal with a major crisis. ("It's a bad recession, but we will weather the storm.")
12. **No matter what**: Unconditionally; regardless of what happens. ("I will achieve my goal no matter what.")
13. **To stick it out**: To endure a difficult situation all the way to the end without giving up. ("This class is hard, but I will stick it out.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence giving advice using the phrase "There's no use in...".
2. Describe a time when you had to "weather the storm" during a difficult period in your life.

# 15 Idioms with the Word "Under"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This quick lesson covers 15 highly common idiomatic expressions that all begin with the word "under". 

## The "Under" Idioms

1. **Under the weather**: Feeling a little bit sick.
2. **Under the microscope**: Being watched or judged very carefully by others.
3. **To get under one's skin**: To deeply annoy or irritate someone. ("Her complaining gets under my skin.")
4. **Under the table**: Getting paid in secret cash to avoid taxes (illegal).
5. **Under the radar**: Doing something secretly so nobody notices.
6. **To come under fire**: To be severely criticized or attacked for a decision.
7. **To throw [someone] under the bus**: To betray a friend or colleague for your own personal benefit.
8. **Under one's breath**: Speaking very softly (often complaining) so no one else can hear.
9. **Right under my nose**: Directly in front of you; extremely obvious. ("He stole the money right under my nose.")
10. **Under the gun**: Feeling intense pressure to meet a strict deadline.
11. **Water under the bridge**: A past conflict or problem that is now forgiven and forgotten. ("We argued years ago, but it's water under the bridge.")
12. **To sweep it under the rug**: To hide a problem and pretend it doesn't exist instead of fixing it.
13. **To go under the knife**: To have surgery.
14. **Under the influence**: Drunk; intoxicated (often related to driving).
15. **Under pressure**: Experiencing intense stress.

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence about a time you felt "under the gun" at work or school.
2. What is the difference between "throwing someone under the bus" and "sweeping it under the rug"?

# 16 Casual Idioms in Context

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson extracts 16 highly common, casual English idioms from an interview with Drake, a young host working at The Cheesecake Factory in Los Angeles.

## Vocabulary and Expressions

1. **Give or take**: Plus or minus; approximately. ("It's a one-hour drive, give or take a few minutes.")
2. **Given the fact that**: Considering; because of. ("The hotel will be expensive given the fact that it's in New York.")
3. **How do you deal with it?**: How do you handle this stressful situation? What is your solution?
4. **The wait (noun)**: The amount of time you must wait for a table. ("How long is the wait?")
5. **To keep a calm/cool head**: To stay calm in a stressful emergency. 
6. **A rule of thumb**: A general, unwritten rule that most people follow. ("As a rule of thumb, leave a 20% tip at a restaurant.")
7. **To hit the mark**: To achieve a goal accurately.
8. **To make a living**: To earn enough money to pay for your basic needs (rent, food). ("It's hard to make a living on minimum wage.")
9. **A people person**: Someone who is highly social, friendly, and loves talking to others. 
10. **A big night out**: A special evening spent outside the house to celebrate something.
11. **To find common ground**: To find shared interests or similarities with someone you might otherwise disagree with.
12. **You name it**: Everything is included; anything you can think of. ("We sell clothes, electronics, furniture—you name it.")
13. **Oh my gosh / Oh my goodness**: A polite, clean alternative to saying "Oh my God."
14. **There's no place like home**: An expression meaning your home is the best, most comfortable place in the world.
15. **It's unreal**: It is amazing, fantastic, or unbelievable. ("The weather in LA is unreal.")
16. **To make it**: To achieve a massive, difficult dream (like becoming a famous actor). ("He moved to Hollywood to try to make it.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Are you a "people person"? Give an example of why or why not.
2. Write a sentence using the phrase "given the fact that."

# Idioms for Health and Modern Lifestyle

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson features an interview with Bruce, a "Rolfer" (alternative medicine practitioner) in Santa Monica. He discusses the physical consequences of our modern, sedentary lifestyles using several high-level idiomatic expressions.

## Vocabulary & Idioms

1. **To address**: To deal with an issue or give it attention. ("The boss addressed the problem at the meeting.")
2. **From head to toe**: The entire body. ("The child was covered in mud from head to toe.")
3. **For hours on end**: For a long time continuously, without stopping. ("He plays video games for hours on end.")
4. **Relentless**: Persistent, constant, harsh (doesn't stop). ("The relentless noise kept me awake.")
5. **Worst-case scenario**: The absolute worst or most serious thing that could possibly happen in a situation.
6. **Sedentary**: Inactive; sitting a lot. ("A sedentary job.")
7. **To offset**: To compensate for something; to restore balance. ("He exercises to offset his sedentary job.")
8. **Couldn't be more truthful**: It is completely true. (Opposite: *Nothing could be further from the truth* - It is completely false).
9. **Wiggle room**: Extra space to negotiate, change your mind, or have options. ("Is there any wiggle room on the price?")
10. **A flight of stairs**: One continuous set of stairs between floors.
11. **To crawl around**: To walk on your hands and knees like a baby.
12. **An aisle seat**: The seat next to the walkway on an airplane. *Note: The 'S' is silent (eye-ull).*
13. **You kind of want to [verb]**: A casual way of giving advice; "I suggest you do this." ("You kind of want to listen to your lawyer.")
14. **To hang out**: To spend time casually. 
15. **To begin with**: First of all; my first point. ("I don't want to go because it's too cold to begin with.")
16. **The perfect storm**: A rare combination of several negative events that creates a uniquely terrible result.

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe your typical workday. Is it "sedentary" or active? How do you "offset" the physical stress of your job?
2. What is a "worst-case scenario" for traveling on an airplane?

# 20 Slang Words and Idioms

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson extracts 20 conversational expressions from unscripted interviews with chefs, security guards, and attendees at the LA Times "101 Best Restaurants" event.

## Conversational Expressions

1. **I gotta say**: I must say; used to emphasize a point. ("I gotta say, that test was hard.")
2. **Out of this world**: Amazing; fantastic. 
3. **Got to [verb]**: Had the opportunity to. ("I got to try Thai food.")
4. **As opposed to**: Compared to; instead of. ("You should study as opposed to playing video games.")
5. **To move on**: To move forward; to change the topic. ("Let's move on to the next question.")
6. **To put [something] to shame**: To completely outperform or be much better than something else. ("Your cooking puts mine to shame.")
7. **You can't go wrong**: It is always a good, safe choice. ("You can't go wrong with the black dress.")
8. **To pop up**: To appear suddenly and unexpectedly. ("New stores are popping up everywhere.")
9. **By leaps and bounds**: Very quickly (usually referring to growth or improvement). ("Her English is improving by leaps and bounds.")
10. **Born and raised**: A phrase indicating your true home and identity. ("I'm a California girl, born and raised.")
11. **I couldn't take it**: I could not tolerate it; it was too much. ("The food was too spicy; I couldn't take it.")
12. **To die for**: Extremely good. ("That dessert is to die for.")
13. **Phenomenal**: Extraordinary; unusually great. 
14. **To have a kick to it**: To taste surprisingly spicy. ("This chicken has a kick to it.")
15. **A signature dish**: The most famous or popular food item a restaurant makes. 
16. **To blow [someone] out of the water**: To easily destroy the competition. ("We won 9 to 1; we blew them out of the water.")
17. **Hors d'oeuvres** (*or-dervs*): Small appetizer snacks (French origin). 
18. **The place to be**: A very popular, trendy location. 
19. **Here and there**: In various places; occasionally. ("You make some mistakes here and there.")
20. **They go**: A highly casual slang term meaning "They said." ("I go 'no', and he goes 'yes'.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe a local restaurant that serves food that is "out of this world." What is their "signature dish"?
2. Use the phrase "by leaps and bounds" in an original sentence about learning a new skill.

# 21 Advanced Words and Idioms

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson features an interview with Craig, an actor who moved from New York to Los Angeles. He discusses his acting career, softening his New York accent, and the different "vibes" of the two major cities.

## Vocabulary and Expressions

1. **To dwindle**: To decrease, shrink, or become smaller over time. ("My bank account is dwindling.")
2. **To typecast**: To always cast an actor in the exact same type of role. ("He is always typecast as the villain.")
3. **Subtle** (*suh-tul*): Barely noticeable; soft. The 'B' is silent.
4. **To stand out**: To be very noticeable. 
5. **To get stuck**: To be trapped in one place without changing. ("I got stuck in traffic.")
6. **To book (a job)**: To be hired for an acting role. (Also means to reserve a hotel/flight).
7. **Southerner** (*suh-ther-ner*): A person from the southern region of the US. *Note: "South" has an "ow" sound, but "Southern" has an "uh" sound.*
8. **To wear/put on different hats**: To have a variety of different roles or responsibilities at work.
9. **Versatile** (*ver-suh-tul*): Adaptable; having many different uses.
10. **High-strung**: Easily excited, angry, or stressed. ("That dog is very high-strung.")
11. **To get out of your way**: To move aside so you can pass.
12. **Thrown to the wolves**: Abandoned in a highly difficult, competitive, or dangerous situation.
13. **Mean**: Unkind, cruel. 
14. **To go about your business**: To do your normal routine without paying attention to others.
15. **To take [something/someone] on**: To do a challenging task, or to fight someone.
16. **Laid-back**: Relaxed, casual, not easily stressed.
17. **Chill**: Calm, not highly emotional.
18. **Vibe**: The mood, feeling, or atmosphere of a place. ("LA has a laid-back vibe.")
19. **All year round**: Continually throughout the entire year.

## Practice Prompts

1. Are you a "high-strung" person or a "laid-back" person? Why?
2. Write a sentence explaining a situation where someone was "thrown to the wolves."

# Advanced Business Vocabulary (NYT)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

True fluency means being able to comfortably read and understand a major newspaper like *The New York Times*. This lesson extracts advanced vocabulary from an article about the shifting US job market and economy.

## Advanced Vocabulary

1. **Turnover (rate)**: The rate at which employees leave a company (quit or fired) and are replaced. ("A high turnover rate is expensive for companies.")
2. **To lose its sizzle**: To lose its excitement or heat. ("The hot job market is losing its sizzle.")
3. **Frenzied**: Wild, uncontrolled, highly excited. ("The children were in a frenzy.")
4. **Wage**: Salary; how much money you make at work.
5. **To abate**: To reduce, lessen, or become less intense. ("The storm finally abated.")
6. **Billboard**: A massive outdoor sign used for advertising. 
7. **Steep**: Having a sharp angle (a steep hill), OR being unreasonably expensive (a steep price). "Steep competition" means intense competition.
8. **To drive up**: To cause something to increase rapidly. ("Inflation is driving up gas prices.")
9. **To sweeten the deal**: To offer extra money or perks to make a business transaction more attractive to the other party.
10. **Through the roof**: Rising to a very high, unexpected level (usually prices or demand). ("Sales went through the roof.")
11. **To die down**: To gradually become less strong or noisy. ("The party died down around 2 AM.")
12. **To freeze [prices/hiring]**: To hold something at a fixed level; stopping all increases. ("The company froze hiring.")
13. **Altogether**: Completely; totally. ("They stopped hiring altogether.")
14. **The tables have turned**: The situation has completely reversed (someone weak is now strong).

## Practice Prompts

1. What causes a company to have a high "turnover" rate? 
2. Use "sweeten the deal" in a sentence describing a negotiation (e.g., buying a car, accepting a job offer).

# Idioms in the News: Fast Food Wars

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson analyzes a news article about "value menu" price wars between major fast-food chains. Journalists frequently use colorful idiomatic expressions to make their writing more engaging. 

## Vocabulary & Expressions

1. **To hit the road**: To leave or start a journey (usually driving). ("We need to hit the road before it gets dark.")
2. **On the run**: Constantly moving; very busy. ("I'm always on the run, so I eat lunch in my car.")
3. **Par for the course**: Typical; exactly what you would normally expect to happen (comes from golf terminology). ("Rejection is par for the course when applying for jobs.")
4. **A joint**: A highly casual restaurant or bar. ("Let's eat at the new burger joint.")
5. **In the midst of**: In the middle of; during. ("I am in the midst of a major project.")
6. **To lure**: To attract, tempt, or persuade someone to do something. ("The store lured customers with huge discounts.")
7. **Woe(s)**: Deep trouble, problems, or sorrow. ("The company is facing financial woes.")
8. **Month-long / Year-long**: Lasting for the entirety of that time period. ("A month-long sale.")
9. **To follow suit**: To copy someone; to do the exact same thing someone else just did. ("McDonald's lowered prices, and Wendy's followed suit.")
10. **To trumpet**: To announce something loudly, forcefully, and proudly. ("The government trumpeted the success of the new policy.")

## Grammar Note: Pluralizing Money

When the dollar amount *modifies* a noun (acts as an adjective), you remove the "S" from dollars.
- **Correct**: It is a 5-dollar meal. (Modifies "meal")
- **Correct**: The meal costs 5 dollars. 
- **Correct**: It is a 2-million-dollar house.
- **Correct**: The house costs 2 million dollars.

## Practice Prompts

1. What is an annoying problem at your job that is just "par for the course"?
2. Write a sentence explaining a time you had to do something "on the run."

<br/>

---

# Professional English & Design Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson features part two of an interview with Ashley Fitch, an interior designer working for a major architecture firm in Los Angeles. She shares expressions related to business, budgets, and the realities of working in design.

## Professional Expressions

1. **On staff**: Employed by a company. ("We have 10 architects on staff.")
2. **ASAP**: As soon as possible.
3. **To have [something] in mind**: To have a specific plan or idea. ("Do you have a budget in mind for this project?")
4. **Up front**: Paid or communicated in advance. ("They didn't tell us the budget up front.")
5. **To go over budget**: To spend more money than was originally planned. 
6. **Way over budget**: Using "way" instead of "very" or "too much" (e.g., "way too hot", "way different").
7. **Value engineering (VE)**: A business term for cutting costs or doing something cheaper.
8. **A way to put it**: A specific manner of expressing or saying something. ("That's a nice way to put it.")
9. **A dagger to the heart**: Something that hurts you deeply and emotionally.
10. **A misconception**: An incorrect view or belief based on misunderstanding.
11. **Behind the scenes**: What happens in private, away from public view.
12. **Blood, sweat, and tears**: Extremely hard work requiring immense effort and suffering.
13. **You would think that**: It is logical to expect it, but it's not actually true. ("He's an adult; you would think he'd make better choices.")
14. **To appreciate**: To recognize the value or beauty of something. ("I appreciate modern art.")
15. **A fixer-upper**: A house that is cheaper but needs a lot of repairs. (Opposite: *Move-in ready*).
16. **It doesn't speak to me**: I don't feel a personal connection to it; it is not my style.
17. **Might as well**: The most logical, practical thing to do in a situation.
18. **To start from scratch**: To start from the very beginning, creating something entirely new.
19. **To get into the field**: To choose and enter a specific profession. 
20. **Vividly**: Clearly; producing a strong, powerful image in your mind. ("I vividly remember my first day of school.")

## Grammar Note: Family Tree
Do not say "Grand grand mother." 
- Grandmother -> **Great**-grandmother -> **Great-great**-grandmother.
- The brother of your grandmother is your **Great uncle**.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever worked on a project that required "blood, sweat, and tears"? Describe it.
2. What is a common "misconception" people have about your job or your country?

# Descriptive News Vocabulary: Wildfires

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson uses a news article about devastating wildfires in Los Angeles to teach highly descriptive, advanced vocabulary. These words are incredibly useful for describing major events, nature, and destruction.

## Vocabulary of Nature and Destruction

1. **Unprecedented**: Completely new; it has never happened before. ("The scale of destruction was unprecedented.")
2. **To flatten**: To make completely flat or level; to destroy a building so it collapses.
3. **A swath** (*swawth*): A large strip, section, or area of land. ("The fire destroyed a huge swath of the forest.")
4. **To render**: To cause something to become a certain condition. ("The flood rendered the roads unusable.")
5. **Quaint**: Charming, old-fashioned, or unusual in a highly pleasant way. ("A quaint little village.")
6. **Whimsy** (noun) / **Whimsical** (adj): Playful, imaginative, and fun. 
7. **Lush**: Full of healthy, abundant green plants. ("A lush tropical garden.")
8. **To billow**: To move in large waves (used for smoke, curtains, or sails). ("White smoke billowed from the wreckage.")
9. **Wreckage**: The broken, ruined parts of something that has been destroyed. ("The wreckage of the car.")
10. **Grim**: Highly serious, depressing, unpleasant, and showing no hope. ("The doctor gave a grim prognosis.")
11. **Prized**: Highly valued and important. ("My most prized possession.")
12. **To back up onto [something]**: When the backside of a house or building directly faces another area. ("The house backs up onto the beach.")
13. **To incinerate**: To burn something completely until it turns to ash.

## Creating Verbs from Adjectives
Many verbs are created by adding "-en" to an adjective:
- Flat -> Flatten
- Short -> Shorten
- Soft -> Soften
- Sharp -> Sharpen
- Wide -> Widen
- Hard -> Harden
- Bright -> Brighten

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a sentence describing a "quaint" place you have visited.
2. Use the word "unprecedented" to describe a recent event in the news or in your life.

# Accent Reduction Coaching

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

This short video serves as a promotional overview for Lisa's one-on-one accent reduction training program. While it doesn't teach specific vocabulary, it highlights why professionals seek to improve their accents.

## Key Concepts
- **Clarity over Perfection**: The goal of accent reduction is not necessarily to erase your identity, but to ensure you are easily understood *the first time* you speak. 
- **Focus on the Message**: When you speak clearly, listeners focus on the content of your message rather than the way you are pronouncing the words.

# Public Speaking with a Foreign Accent

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: study-skills

## Summary

This lesson features an interview with Ross, a public relations (PR) account supervisor who trains athletes and CEOs on how to speak to the media. He provides fantastic advice for anyone nervous about speaking English with an accent in public.

## Public Speaking Advice (Ross's Tips)
- **Do not sound rehearsed**: If you memorize a speech "word for word," you will sound like a robot. People might think you are "covering something up" (hiding the truth).
- **Be an authority**: You were hired because you are an expert. Focus on your expertise, not your accent. 
- **Be relatable**: People want to work with someone they feel comfortable with. If you speak naturally, you "come off as" a regular person. 

## Vocabulary & Expressions

1. **Folks**: An informal word for people. *Note: "My folks" means "My parents".*
2. **A [noun] nut**: An obsessed, highly enthusiastic fan of something. ("He's a sports nut," or "She's a health nut.")
3. **One (pronoun)**: A formal way of saying "someone." ("How does *one* get a job here?")
4. **To get your point across**: To successfully communicate your message so people understand you.
5. **Succinct** (*suk-sinkt*): Clearly expressed in very few words; brief and direct. 
6. **Work ethic**: A strong belief that hard work is important and morally good. ("He has a great work ethic.")
7. **To come off as**: To appear, seem, or give a certain impression. ("He came off as arrogant.")
8. **An adage** (*ad-ij*): A wise saying, proverb, or accepted truth. ("There is an old adage...")

## Practice Prompts

1. Do you agree with Ross that it is better to speak naturally with mistakes rather than sounding perfectly rehearsed like a robot? Why?
2. What is an "adage" (wise saying) from your native country? Translate it into English.

# Idioms and the Word "Going On"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This is part two of an interview with Megan, an LA restaurant worker originally from a very small town in Michigan. This lesson breaks down complex pronunciations and the multiple meanings of common phrases.

## Vocabulary & Idioms

1. **You can't beat it**: It is the absolute best; nothing is better. ("You can't beat LA weather.")
2. **Teeny-tiny**: Very, very small.
3. **Rural** (*rur-ull*): In the countryside; away from the city. *Note: This is notoriously difficult to pronounce. The L is very soft.*
4. **To pass along**: To give or transmit from one person to another. ("My mom passed along her love of horses to me.")
5. **Practically**: Almost; virtually. ("I've watched practically every video.")
6. **Driven**: Highly hard-working, ambitious, and motivated. 
7. **A side project**: A secondary job or creative project done in addition to your main job.
8. **Pace of life**: The speed at which daily life happens. ("The pace of life is slower in the country.")
9. **To get away with it**: To do something wrong, risky, or bad, but not get caught or punished for it.

## The 3 Meanings of "Going On"
1. **Happening**: "There's so much *going on* in this city."
2. **Approaching a time/age**: "I've lived here *going on* four years." (Almost 4 years). "He's *going on* 12."
3. **How are you?**: "What's *going on*?" (Casual greeting. Answer: "Not much.")

## Grammar Note: "There's" for Plurals
In highly casual spoken English, native speakers often use "There's" (There is) with plural nouns, even though it is technically grammatically incorrect.
- *Strict Grammar*: "There **are** interesting people to meet."
- *Casual Speech*: "There**'s** interesting people to meet."

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe the "pace of life" in your hometown. Is it fast or slow?
2. Write a sentence using the phrase "to get away with it."

# 20 Modern Slang Words

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

In this lesson, Drake teaches modern slang expressions frequently used by younger generations and seen constantly on social media (Instagram, Twitter, etc.). While you shouldn't use these in formal business settings, knowing them is essential for understanding modern pop culture.

## Slang & Expressions

1. **Tea / To spill the tea**: Gossip. ("Do you have any tea on Aunt Linda? Spill the tea!")
2. **Slide into the DMs**: To send a direct message (DM) to someone on social media, usually with romantic or flirtatious intentions.
3. **Don't be a stranger**: (Not slang, but common) Stay in touch; don't disappear. 
4. **Cringe / Cringy**: The physical feeling of being embarrassed for someone else when they do something awkward.
5. **You do you**: Be yourself; do whatever makes you happy (often said when giving advice).
6. **I feel you / I feel that**: I understand you; I agree with you.
7. **I see where you're coming from**: I understand your point of view. 
8. **I'm not feeling it**: I don't agree with it, or I'm not enjoying it. ("I'm not feeling this party.")
9. **Lame**: Boring, uncool, or not exciting.
10. **Mood**: Used as a caption for a picture to say "I currently feel the exact same way as this picture."
11. **Fire**: Extremely good; fantastic. ("That food was fire.")
12. **Sus / Sketchy**: Suspicious; untrustworthy.
13. **Low-key**: Secretly; you don't want everyone to know. ("I low-key have a crush on him.")
14. **High-key**: Obvious; everyone knows it.
15. **Thirsty**: Desperate for attention (often romantic/sexual attention). Used negatively.
16. **No cap**: No lie; I am telling the absolute truth. ("I can do 100 pushups, no cap.")
17. **Yas**: A highly enthusiastic way to say "Yes!"
18. **Gucci**: Good, nice, or fine. ("Don't worry bro, it's all gucci.")
19. **Stan**: An obsessed, mega-fan of a celebrity (originates from an Eminem song). ("He is a total Kendrick Lamar stan.")
20. **Keep it 100**: Keep it 100% real; be completely honest with me.
21. **Savage**: Someone who is brutally honest and does not care about other people's feelings. 
22. **To tell [someone] off**: To verbally attack someone or tell them exactly why you are angry at them. ("I got fired because I told my boss off.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever had to "tell someone off"? What happened?
2. Write a sentence using "low-key" or "high-key."

title: "Natural American Accent - linking consonants"
description: "A short pronunciation lesson on how to smoothly link words ending in 'T' or 'D' with words beginning with a consonant."
category: "english-pronunciation"
theme: "linking"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"
visibility: "public"
language: "en"
tags: ["english-pronunciation", "linking", "consonants", "youtube", "review"]
---

# Linking Consonants: Held T and D

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

To speak smoothly and naturally, native speakers constantly link words together. This lesson focuses on a specific rule: what happens when a word ends in a 'T' or 'D' and the next word starts with a consonant.

## The Rule: Holding the Consonant
When a word ends in a 'D' or a 'T', and the next word begins with a consonant, **do not release your tongue**, but also **do not skip the letter completely**. You must "hold" the sound.

**Examples with 'D':**
- *Good job* (Hold the D, don't say "goo-duh job", but don't say "goo job").
- *Good morning*
- *Red car*

**Examples with 'T':**
- *That man*
- *That way*
- *I can't go* (This is why "can" and "can't" are so hard to differentiate! We do not clearly pronounce the 'T' in "can't go." We just abruptly stop the airflow).

*Note: If the following word starts with a VOWEL, the rule changes and you link them smoothly (e.g., "Good effort" becomes "Goo-deffort").*

## Practice Prompts

1. Read these phrases aloud, practicing holding the first consonant: "I don't need it," "What day is it," "Tell me about that."

category: "vocabulary"
theme: "modern-english"

---

# Controversial Modern Expressions

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

Language is constantly evolving. This lesson covers popular new expressions that many people use daily, but that *other* people find highly annoying, ungrammatical, or insincere.

## The Expressions

1. **Adulting (verb)**: Doing normal grown-up responsibilities like paying bills or doing laundry. ("Adulting is hard today.")
    - *Why people hate it*: It sounds immature; adults should just do their chores without complaining.
2. **Fur baby**: A pet dog or cat.
    - *Why people hate it*: People who don't like pets find it silly to compare an animal to a human child.
3. **I was today years old when...**: A humorous way to say you just learned an obvious fact today. 
4. **It is what it is**: A phrase meaning the situation cannot be changed, so you must accept it.
    - *Why people hate it*: It sounds like giving up and immediately ends a conversation. It is severely overused.
5. **Literally**: True or factual. 
    - *Why people hate it*: People constantly use it incorrectly for exaggeration (e.g., "I literally died laughing"). 
6. **My bad**: A casual way to say "My mistake" or "I'm sorry."
    - *Why people hate it*: It is grammatically incorrect (an adjective after a possessive pronoun instead of a noun). 
7. **That's my truth**: Used to describe one's personal experience or feelings.
    - *Why people hate it*: Truth is objective (a fact). Feelings are subjective. You cannot have a personal "truth."
8. **I'm obsessed with [something]**: I really love it; I'm excited about it.
    - *Why people hate it*: It is an extreme exaggeration that sounds fake or insincere. 
9. **With all due respect**: A phrase used right before heavily criticizing someone.
    - *Why people hate it*: The "respect" usually feels completely fake.
10. **Bestie**: Best friend.
11. **To circle back**: Corporate jargon meaning "let's discuss this later." 
    - *Why people hate it*: It is annoying office-speak that usually means "I don't want to deal with this right now."
12. **I'm sorry you feel that way**: A non-apology.
    - *Why people hate it*: The speaker is refusing to take responsibility for their actions and is instead blaming the victim's feelings.
13. **A Karen**: A pushy, demanding, entitled woman who complains constantly (especially to store managers).
    - *Why people hate it*: It has ruined a perfectly nice, normal name for real women named Karen. 

## Practice Prompts

1. Do you use the word "literally" for exaggeration in your native language? Do you find it annoying?
2. Have you ever received a "non-apology" like "I'm sorry you feel that way"? How did it make you feel?

<br/>

# 10 Advanced Adjectives (Part 10)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson adds 10 more advanced adjectives to your vocabulary arsenal. Mastering these specific words will instantly elevate your professional and academic English.

## Advanced Words

1. **Unanimous** (*yoo-nan-uh-mus*): Everyone in the group completely agrees; having the exact same opinion. ("The jury's decision was unanimous.") *Do not confuse with 'anonymous' (unknown).*
2. **Secular**: Having absolutely no connection with religion. ("He went to a secular high school.")
3. **Sedentary**: Sitting down a lot; lacking physical activity. ("He needs to change his sedentary lifestyle.")
4. **Lucrative**: Highly profitable; making a lot of money. ("He is looking for lucrative business ideas.")
5. **Harsh**: Too strong, angry, or unkind. ("His harsh words made the child cry.")
6. **Fleeting**: Lasting for only a very short time before passing. ("He had a fleeting interest in karate.")
7. **Stagnant**: Not moving, flowing, or growing. ("The politician promised to fix the stagnant economy.")
8. **Sole**: Only one; single. ("The sole purpose of my trip is to see my grandparents.")
9. **Idle**: Not working, not active, or not being used. ("The workers were idle while the machine was broken.")
10. **Delinquent**: 
    - Meaning 1: Breaking the law (usually a minor crime committed by a young person). 
    - Meaning 2: Late or overdue (used for payments). ("They lost their home because of delinquent mortgage payments.")

## Pronunciation Note: Compound Nouns vs. Adjective+Noun
- **Adjective + Noun**: Stress the NOUN. (e.g., little **boy**)
- **Compound Noun**: Stress the FIRST word. (e.g., **high** school, **business** ideas, **karate** classes).

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever had a "lucrative" idea? What was it?
2. Write a sentence describing a time you had to pay a "delinquent" bill or fee.

# Advanced Alternatives to "Very" (Part 2)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

To sound truly advanced in English, you should stop relying on the word "very." This lesson provides 10 high-level, extremely descriptive adjectives to replace basic "very + adjective" combinations.

## 10 Advanced Upgrades

1. Very rainy ➔ **Pouring** ("It's been pouring for over an hour.")
2. Very expensive ➔ **Exorbitant** ("The prices in this store are exorbitant.")
3. Very good ➔ **Exceptional** or **Superb** ("The food is superb.")
4. Very hot ➔ **Sweltering** or **Boiling** ("The afternoons are sweltering.")
5. Very surprised ➔ **Stunned** ("I was stunned by the news.")
6. Very bad ➔ **Atrocious** or **Appalling** ("His grammar is atrocious.")
7. Very terrible ➔ **Horrendous** ("He committed a horrendous crime.")
8. Very painful ➔ **Excruciating** or **Unbearable** ("She had an excruciating headache.")
9. Very small ➔ **Minuscule** ("Your problems are minuscule compared to theirs.")
10. Very old ➔ **Ancient** ("That is an ancient building.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Describe the worst, most "sweltering" summer day you have ever experienced.
2. Have you ever seen a movie that was absolutely "atrocious"? What was it?

title: "Part 2 - How to Apologize to Clients and Customers - Professional English"
description: "Master the art of the professional apology using formal vocabulary and structures specifically suited for business communication."
category: "business-english"

mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"

---

# Professional Apologies (Part 2)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: business-english

## Summary

Apologizing gracefully is a critical business skill. In this lesson, Lisa teaches advanced, highly professional ways to apologize to clients and customers, elevating your communication far beyond a simple "I'm sorry."

## Formal Apology Expressions

Instead of the basic "Sorry," use these sophisticated alternatives, especially in emails:
1. **We regret...**: Highly formal. ("We regret that we are unable to give you a refund.")
2. **We deeply / sincerely regret...**: ("We deeply regret the misunderstanding.")
3. **We apologize for the negative experience you had.**
4. **I want to extend my sincerest apologies for the poor service you received.** *(Note: "Poor service" is a polite way to say "bad service.")*
5. **Please accept my sincerest apologies that your experience was anything less than you should expect.**

## Apologizing for Delays

1. **To get back to [someone]**: To respond or reply. 
   - *Casual*: "I'm sorry it took me so long to get back to you."
   - *Formal*: "My sincerest apologies for not getting back to you sooner."
2. **Delay**: 
   - "I'm sorry for the delay in responding to your email."
   - "I apologize for the delayed response."

## Apologizing for Being Late

1. **To keep [someone] waiting**:
   - *Texting before arriving*: "I'm sorry to keep you waiting."
   - *When you finally arrive*: "I'm sorry to have kept you waiting."
2. **Running late**: The most natural way to say you are delayed. 
   - "I'm running five minutes late."
3. **On [one's] way**: Currently traveling to the location. 
   - "She's on her way."

## Practice Prompts

1. Write a professional email apologizing to a client for taking three days to respond to their question.
2. You are 10 minutes late to meet a friend. Text them using the phrase "running late."

title: "Part 6 - 10 More Advanced English Words for More Fluent English"
description: "Expand your vocabulary for describing people with 10 advanced adjectives like 'tactful', 'tenacious', and 'gullible'."
category: "vocabulary"

---

# 10 Advanced Adjectives for People (Part 6)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson focuses entirely on advanced adjectives used specifically to describe human personality and character traits, ranging from highly positive to deeply negative.

## Advanced Vocabulary

### Positive Traits
1. **Tactful**: Always knows the polite, correct thing to say in sensitive or difficult situations. ("Please find a tactful way to give her the bad news.")
2. **Trustworthy**: Honest; someone you can completely trust. ("My trustworthy neighbor.")
3. **Tenacious**: Hard-working and determined; someone who never gives up even when facing obstacles.
4. **Witty**: Funny in a fast, highly intelligent way. ("A witty sense of humor.")
5. **Conscientious**: Honest and extremely hard-working; someone who carefully ensures they do things correctly.

### Neutral / Negative Traits
6. **Frugal**: Very careful about spending money (not necessarily selfish, just economical).
7. **Gullible**: Easily fooled or tricked because they trust everyone too much. ("The salesman convinced the gullible customer to buy the broken car.")
8. **Outspoken**: Speaking very directly with strong opinions, rarely caring what others think.
9. **Greedy**: Always wanting more (usually money or food) and never being satisfied.
10. **Conceited**: Arrogant; feeling that you are much better than everybody else.

## Practice Prompts

1. Think of the most "tenacious" person you know. What makes them so determined?
2. Why is being "outspoken" sometimes a good trait, but sometimes a bad trait?

---
title: "Part 8 - Ten More Advanced English Words for More Fluent Speech"
description: "Master the pronunciation and usage of 10 advanced adjectives ending in '-ous', including 'tedious' and 'ambiguous'."
category: "vocabulary"

# 10 Advanced "-ous" Adjectives (Part 8)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson teaches 10 advanced adjectives that all end in "-ous." Lisa focuses heavily on pronunciation, noting that the "-ous" suffix is ALWAYS reduced to an "us" sound in English, unlike in Latin-based languages.

## Advanced "-ous" Words

1. **Monotonous**: Always the same; boring and flat. ("A monotonous voice.")
2. **Tedious**: Boring due to endless, slow repetition (often used for jobs). ("A tedious task.")
3. **Hazardous**: Highly dangerous or risky. ("Smoking is hazardous to your health.")
4. **Infamous** (*IN-fuh-mus*): Famous for a very bad or evil reason. ("The infamous criminal.") *Note: Stress the FIRST syllable.*
5. **Anonymous**: Having an unknown or hidden name. ("An anonymous letter.")
6. **Vigorous**: Highly energetic, physical, and strong. ("Vigorous exercise.")
7. **Tremendous**: Extremely large, impressive, or great in amount. ("Tremendous progress.")
8. **Studious**: Loving to study and read. ("His brother is very studious.")
9. **Ambiguous**: Unclear; having two or more possible meanings. ("The poem is intentionally ambiguous.")
10. **Oblivious**: Completely unaware of what is happening around you. ("He was oblivious to his surroundings.")

## Practice Prompts

1. What is the most "tedious" chore you have to do at home or at work?
2. Write a sentence describing a situation where instructions were too "ambiguous."

# Easily Confused Phrasal Verbs

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

Phrasal verbs are essential for fluency, but they are notoriously tricky because changing just one preposition alters the entire meaning. This lesson clarifies several easily confused sets of phrasal verbs.

## Phrasal Verb Sets

### 1. Fill
- **To fill [someone] in**: To provide missing information to someone. ("Please fill me in on what happened.")
- **To fill in FOR [someone]**: To temporarily substitute for someone at work. ("Can you fill in for me tomorrow?")
- **To fill in**: To write in a blank space. ("Fill in the blank.")
- **To fill out**: To complete an entire document or form. ("Fill out the application.")
- **To fill up**: To make completely full with liquid. ("Fill up the gas tank.")

### 2. Walk & Call
- **To walk in ON [someone]**: To accidentally enter a room while someone is doing something private.
- **To call [someone] out**: To verbally challenge someone's bad behavior; to demand they explain themselves. ("I had to call him out for lying.")

### 3. Come & Break
- **To come down to**: To ultimately depend on. ("The decision comes down to money.")
- **To come down with**: To become sick. ("I am coming down with a cold.")
- **To break out in**: To suddenly develop a skin rash or sweat. ("I broke out in a rash.")
- **To break out of**: To escape. ("He broke out of prison.")

### 4. Put
- **To put up**: To build or erect. ("Put up a building/tent.")
- **To put [someone] up**: To let someone stay at your house. ("I'll put you up in the guest room.")
- **To put up with**: To tolerate something highly annoying. ("I can't put up with this noise!")

### 5. Car Phrasal Verbs
- **To pull up**: To stop your car next to a location. ("The bus will pull up here.")
- **To pull over**: To move to the side of the road and stop (usually by the police). ("I was pulled over.")
- **To pull into**: To steer into a parking spot or garage.

### 6. Turn
- **To turn in**: To go to bed, OR to submit an assignment. 
- **To turn to [someone]**: To seek help or advice from someone. ("I turn to my brother for advice.")
- **To turn into**: To transform or become. ("Ice turns into water.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever had to "put up with" a bad roommate or noisy neighbor? How did you handle it?
2. Who is the person you "turn to" when you need good advice?

<br/>

# 23 Phrasal Verbs with "GET"

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

The verb "to get" is arguably the most versatile verb in the English language. This lesson explains 23 highly common phrasal verbs using "get" that native speakers use daily.

## Phrasal Verbs with GET

### Travel & Action
1. **Get around**: To travel from place to place. ("It's easy to get around by train.")
2. **Get around to it**: To finally do a delayed/procrastinated task. ("I'll clean my room when I get around to it.")
3. **Get away**: To escape or take a vacation. (*Noun: a getaway*).
4. **Get to it**: To start doing something. ("I'll get to it tomorrow.")
5. **Get down to**: To begin doing something seriously. ("Let's get down to business.")

### Conflict & Consequences
6. **Get away with [something]**: To avoid punishment for doing something wrong. ("He got away with murder.")
7. **Get back at [someone]**: To take revenge on someone.
8. **Get to [someone]**: To deeply annoy or upset someone. ("The noise is getting to me. Don't let it get to you.")
9. **Get [someone] down**: To make someone feel sad or depressed. ("Rainy days get me down.")

### Communication & Relationships
10. **Get back to [someone]**: To reply to a message.
11. **Get back with [someone]**: To resume a romantic relationship after a breakup.
12. **Get together**: To meet socially. (*Noun: a get-together*).
13. **Get through to [someone]**: To succeed in making someone understand your point. ("I can't get through to him.")

### Recovery & Progress
14. **Get over [someone/something]**: To recover emotionally or physically. ("Get over a breakup / Get over the flu.")
15. **Get it over with**: To quickly finish an unpleasant task so you don't have to deal with it anymore.
16. **Get through [something]**: To survive or finish a difficult period. ("Get through a crisis.")
17. **Get it together**: To organize your life, stop making foolish decisions, and improve.
18. **Get on**: To make progress. ("How are you getting on at school?")
19. **Get on with it**: To continue doing something after being interrupted. 

### Transportation Rules
20. **Get IN / Get OUT OF**: Used for Cars.
21. **Get ON / Get OFF**: Used for Buses, Planes, Trains, Boats, Motorcycles. 

## Practice Prompts

1. What is a household chore you have been procrastinating on, but you finally need to "get around to"?
2. Write a sentence explaining how long it took you to "get over" your last cold or flu.

# Advanced Newspaper Vocabulary

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa breaks down a real *Time Magazine* article about Amazon suing fake-review groups. Analyzing real-world news articles is an incredible way to absorb advanced vocabulary used in formal, business, and journalistic contexts.

## Journalistic & Legal Vocabulary

1. **To sue**: To take legal action against someone in court. 
2. **Allegedly** (*uh-LEDGE-id-lee*): Said to have happened, but without proof. ("He allegedly stole the money.")
3. **Fraud**: The crime of using dishonest methods to get something valuable (money). (*Person: a fraudster*).
4. **Behemoth** (*buh-HEE-muth*): A huge creature, or metaphorically, a massive and powerful organization (like Amazon or Apple).
5. **To broker**: To arrange a deal or agreement between two groups. ("They brokered a peace agreement.")
6. **Third party**: A person or company involved in an agreement but not one of the main two participants. 
7. **To incentivize**: To motivate someone to do something, usually with a reward (like money). ("The company incentivized employees with bonuses.")
8. **Misleading**: Making you believe something that is not true. ("A misleading advertisement.")
9. **Scheme**: A clever plan, especially to do something bad or illegal.
10. **Perpetrator**: A person who commits a crime or does something illegal.
11. **On the heels of**: Happening soon after something else. ("The disaster comes on the heels of the earthquake.")
12. **To renew**: To begin again with more energy. ("Renewed efforts.")
13. **To quell**: To suppress, crush, or reduce completely. ("Police quelled the riot.")
14. **Guidelines**: Rules or instructions.
15. **To bring someone up to speed**: To give someone the most recent, latest information. ("Please bring me up to speed on the project.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever bought a product because of a "misleading" review?
2. Write a sentence asking a coworker to "bring you up to speed" after you return from a vacation.

# Conversational Fluency with Drake (Part 2)

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

In part two of Lisa's conversation with Drake, he discusses moving from Los Angeles to Las Vegas. This lesson focuses on extracting natural, everyday expressions used by native speakers to describe places, costs, and weather.

## Key Expressions & Vocabulary

1. **The Strip**: A famous stretch of land or boulevard known for entertainment (e.g., the Las Vegas Strip, the Sunset Strip).
2. **To picture**: To imagine or visualize. ("I can't picture myself living there.")
3. **Condo vs. Apartment**: 
   - *Condo*: You own it. You pay a **mortgage** (loan payment to the bank).
   - *Apartment*: You rent it. You pay **rent** to a landlord. 
4. **Laid-back**: Relaxed; not easily stressed. ("He has a very laid-back personality.")
5. **Outskirts**: The outer edges of a city, usually quieter or less developed. ("I live on the outskirts of town.")
6. **You're looking at...**: A conversational way to say "This is the approximate cost." ("If you want a new car, you're looking at $30,000.")
7. **Pretty decent**: Good or satisfactory, but not amazing. ("The food was pretty decent.")
8. **Just as [adjective]**: Equally. ("The summer is just as bad as the winter.")
9. **Rough / Tough**: Difficult, cruel, or hard to endure. ("That's a tough situation." / "I had a rough day.")

## Pronunciation Note: Dropping the 'T'
In American English, when a 'T' directly follows an 'N', native speakers frequently drop the 'T'.
- Winter ➔ *Winner*
- Twenty ➔ *Twenny*
- Interview ➔ *Innerview*
- International ➔ *Innernational*

## Practice Prompts

1. Do you prefer living in the city center or on the "outskirts"? Why?
2. Describe a movie that was "pretty decent" but not a masterpiece.

# Fitness & Body Vocabulary with Isaiah

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

Lisa interviews Isaiah, a personal trainer at an exclusive Los Angeles gym (Equinox) and a fitness model. This video is packed with vocabulary related to health, fitness, body image, and general conversational idioms.

## Fitness & Body Vocabulary

1. **Physique**: The form, structure, or shape of a person's body. ("He has an athletic physique.")
2. **Build**: Similar to physique. ("He has a lean build.")
3. **Lean**: Having very little body fat.
4. **Bulked up / Bulky**: Having very large, thick muscles (like a bodybuilder).
5. **Definition**: Highly visible muscles due to low body fat. ("She has great muscle definition.")
6. **Toned**: Firm muscles with low fat (similar to definition).
7. **Washboard stomach**: A stomach with highly visible abdominal muscles (looks like an old-fashioned washboard).
8. **Glutes / Booty**: The buttocks muscles. (Booty is slang; glutes is anatomical/fitness terminology). 
9. **To get in shape / Tip-top shape**: Perfect physical condition. 

## Conversational Expressions & Verbs

1. **To better [oneself]**: To improve. ("I'm trying to better myself.")
2. **To uphold**: To maintain; to keep from deteriorating. ("To uphold an image.")
3. **To pinpoint**: To locate exactly. 
4. **-esque (suffix)**: Resembling or like. ("Modelesque" = like a model. "Picturesque" = like a picture).
5. **Fine line**: A very small difference between two things. ("There's a fine line between confidence and arrogance.")
6. **To attain**: To achieve a goal. 
7. **Tedious**: Boring and repetitive. ("This job is tedious.")
8. **Outstanding**: Excellent; superior. ("I feel outstanding.")
9. **A lot that goes into...**: It requires a lot of effort and complex parts. ("There's a lot that goes into planning a wedding.")
10. **Anywhere between**: Used for estimating a range. ("It costs anywhere between 10 and 20 dollars.")
11. **Go about your day**: Continue your normal daily routine. 
12. **Sweet tooth**: A strong craving or desire for sugary foods.
13. **To indulge in**: To allow yourself to enjoy a pleasure. ("I indulged in some chocolate.")
14. **To make up for [something]**: To compensate for doing something bad by doing something good later. 
15. **To throw a curveball**: To completely surprise someone with an unexpected challenge. 
16. **That guy**: Slang for a person (usually a man) behaving badly or annoyingly. ("Don't be *that guy*.")
17. **Rates**: The cost for services. ("What is your hourly rate?")
18. **To go by [name]**: To use a specific name/nickname. ("His name is Robert, but he goes by Bob.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Do you have a "sweet tooth"? What do you usually "indulge in"?
2. Write a sentence about a time life "threw you a curveball."

# Newspaper Vocabulary: The Lottery Winner

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: vocabulary

## Summary

This lesson uses a real article from the *Los Angeles Times* about a young man who won a $2 billion lottery jackpot and immediately bought a massive house. It covers great idioms for money, speed, and real estate.

## Money & Real Estate Expressions

1. **To drop [money]**: (Slang) To spend a large amount of money on something very expensive. ("He dropped 25 million on a mansion.")
2. **Mansion**: A massive, highly expensive house. 
3. **To not waste any time / To waste no time**: To do something very quickly, often surprisingly fast. ("He wasted no time finding a new job.")
4. **To come forward**: To present yourself publicly or to the authorities. ("A witness came forward.")
5. **Record-setting**: Achieving something that has never been done before. ("A record-setting prize.")
6. **Lump sum**: A single, complete payment of money, rather than smaller monthly or yearly installments. 
7. **Splashy**: Highly colorful, showy, or designed to attract a lot of attention. ("A splashy wedding.")
8. **To amount to**: To add up to a specific total. ("The bill amounted to $200.")
9. **Priciest**: The superlative form of "pricey"; the most expensive. 
10. **Slight**: Small in amount. ("A slight discount," "A slight headache.")
11. **To list**: To make a property publicly available for sale. (*Noun: A listing*).

## Grammar Rule: Pluralizing "Million", "Thousand", "Hundred"
- **If you provide a specific number, DO NOT add an 'S'**: 
  - "Two million dollars." 
  - "Five thousand people."
- **If you do NOT provide a specific number, you MUST add an 'S'**: 
  - "Millions of dollars." 
  - "Thousands of people."

## Practice Prompts

1. If you won the lottery, would you take the "lump sum" or the yearly installments? Why?
2. If you had endless money, what is something ridiculous you would "drop" a million dollars on?

# Public Speaking & Communication Tips

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: business-english

## Summary

Lisa interviews Alex Lyon, a communication coach, to discuss how non-native speakers can sound more professional, confident, and engaging during presentations and meetings.

## Key Communication Tips

1. **Pacing**: Speak at a medium pace. Emphasize the last three words of your sentence and pause briefly. 
2. **Volume**: Imagine a volume dial. If you usually speak at a '6', consciously turn it up to an '8'. You must project your voice to reach the back of the room.
3. **Gestures**: Use your hands about once per sentence. Keep them "softball-sized" or "football-sized." Stiff, robotic bodies look unenthusiastic. 
4. **Posture**: Put your body into a confident posture (shoulders back, chin up), even if you are terrified. Acting confident physically often tricks your brain into *feeling* confident.
5. **Organization**: Stick to the classic structure: Introduction, Body (3 points), Conclusion. 
6. **Speaking Up**: In meetings, don't sit silently. You must "speak up" (participate and contribute). 

## Vocabulary Used in the Interview

1. **Cadence**: The flow and rhythm of your speech. ("A professional speaking cadence.")
2. **Jittery**: Highly nervous, shaking, and unable to relax. ("I feel jittery before a presentation.")
3. **To get it over with**: To quickly finish an unpleasant task so you no longer have to worry about it. 
4. **Composed**: Calm and in control of your emotions (the opposite of jittery). 
5. **To project [your voice]**: To speak loudly and clearly so everyone in a large room can hear you.
6. **To come across**: How you appear or are perceived by other people. ("She comes across as very confident.")
7. **As you go**: While you progress or do something. ("You will figure it out as you go.")

## Practice Prompts

1. Do you normally feel "jittery" or "composed" when speaking in public? 
2. In your culture, do people gesture a lot when they speak, or do they keep their bodies still?

# 16 Workplace Expressions

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: business-english

## Summary

In this lesson, Lisa interviews Ashley, an interior designer for exclusive restaurants like Nobu. The interview highlights 16 highly common expressions used daily in professional and corporate environments.

## Professional Expressions

1. **To take off**: To quickly become successful or popular. ("Her career really took off.")
2. **Prior to**: A formal way to say "before." ("Prior to the meeting...")
3. **To jump into**: To start doing something quickly or immediately. ("Let's jump into the presentation.")
4. **I should say**: A phrase used to politely correct yourself when you make a speaking mistake.
5. **To pop up**: To appear suddenly or unexpectedly in multiple places. ("New restaurants are popping up everywhere.")
6. **The fabric (of a place)**: The unique identity, culture, and history of a city or organization. 
7. **Right on the water**: Located directly next to the ocean or a lake. 
8. **High-grossing**: Making a massive amount of money/profit. ("A high-grossing movie.")
9. **As well**: Also; in addition. ("I'll take the salad as well.")
10. **To fly in**: To arrive by airplane. ("The CEO is flying in tomorrow.")
11. **To sign off on [something]**: To officially approve a project or decision. ("We can't start until the director signs off on it.")
12. **To get on a call**: To speak on the phone or a video meeting instead of communicating via email. ("Let's get on a call to figure out the details.")
13. **Blanket term**: A single word or phrase used to describe a broad range of related things. 
14. **All of the above**: Everything that was previously mentioned in a list. 
15. **Site vs. Sight vs. Cite**: 
    - *Site*: A location (Construction site).
    - *Sight*: Vision (Eyesight).
    - *Cite*: To quote or mention.

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever had to wait for a boss or manager to "sign off on" a project? 
2. Write a sentence suggesting to a coworker that you should "get on a call" instead of emailing back and forth.

title: "Professional English - How to sound confident with intonation"
description: "Discover the critical difference between falling intonation (confidence) and rising intonation (uptalk), and how it affects your professional image."
category: "business-english"
theme: "pronunciation"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"
visibility: "public"
language: "en"
tags: ["business-english", "pronunciation", "intonation", "youtube", "review"]
---

# Confident Intonation & Uptalk

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: business-english

## Summary

Your words might be confident, but if your intonation (the melody of your voice) is wrong, you will sound insecure. This lesson focuses on the power of falling intonation and the dangers of "uptalk."

## Falling Intonation (Confidence)
A downward pitch at the end of a sentence communicates certainty, completion, and authority. Use falling intonation for:
1. **Facts**: "My name is John." (Pitch goes down on John).
2. **Commands**: "Please close the door."
3. **The end of a list**: "I bought apples, bananas, and *pears*." (Pitch falls on pears).
4. **Confirmations**: "Yes, that's right."

## Rising Intonation
An upward pitch at the end of a sentence communicates that something is incomplete or questioning.
1. **Yes/No Questions**: "Did you finish the report?"
2. **Unfinished Thoughts**: "If I have time..." (Pitch goes up, waiting for the rest of the sentence). 

## The Danger of Uptalk (Upspeak)
**Uptalk** is the habit of using rising intonation at the end of a statement, making it sound like a question. 
- *Example*: "My name is Alex?" "I think we should hire candidate B?"
- **Why it's bad**: It makes the speaker sound unsure, insecure, weak, and less intelligent. It forces the listener to constantly validate the speaker. 
- **The Fix**: Consciously force your pitch *down* at the end of your declarative sentences to sound like a confident leader.

## Practice Prompts

1. Practice saying your name, job title, and where you live using strong **falling intonation**. Do not sound like you are asking a question!

<br/>

title: "Professional English Practice"
description: "Break bad communication habits like over-apologizing and minimizing accomplishments to sound like a true leader in the workplace."
category: "business-english"
theme: "leadership"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"
visibility: "public"
language: "en"

---

# Speaking Like a Leader

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: business-english

## Summary

Lisa and communication coach Alex Lyon discuss how to adjust your professional phrasing to sound like a confident leader. They highlight specific habits to break—like over-apologizing—and provide powerful alternative phrases.

## Habit 1: Over-Apologizing
Apologizing for minor things makes you appear weak and indecisive. Substitute "I'm sorry" with "Thank you."
- Instead of "Sorry to disagree" ➔ **"Thank you for hearing me out."**
- Instead of "Sorry you had to do extra work" ➔ **"Thank you for putting in that extra effort."**
- Instead of "Sorry to interrupt" ➔ **"I'd like to jump in here for a moment."**
- Instead of "Sorry to bother you" ➔ **"Do you have a minute to talk?"**

## Habit 2: Minimizing Accomplishments
While humility is valued in some cultures, in the American workplace, minimizing your success can hurt your career. 
- Don't deny compliments; simply say "Thank you."
- Talk about your achievements in terms of beneficial outcomes: *"Our team managed to cut customer complaints by 50%."*

## Habit 3: Uncertain Speech
Eliminate words that make you sound unsure. 
- Instead of "I think / I feel" ➔ **"I predict / I anticipate / I envision."**
- Instead of "I think..." ➔ **"Based on the data, it appears that..."**
- Instead of "I don't know" ➔ **"I don't have that information right now"** OR **"I'm not currently in a position to answer that."**
- Instead of "I'll try" ➔ **"I'll make it happen"** OR **"I'll do my best to ensure it's completed."**

## Habit 4: Creating Doubt with Questions
Stop asking weak questions at the end of your statements out of habit. 
- Instead of "Does that make sense?" (which implies you explained it badly or they are too dumb to understand) ➔ Ask, **"What are your thoughts?"** or **"Do you have any questions?"**

## Practice Prompts

1. Have you ever found yourself saying "I'm sorry" when you didn't actually do anything wrong? 
2. Rewrite this sentence to sound more confident: "I'll try to finish this report by Friday, I think."

<br/>

title: "Pronunciation of English L sound after long vowels. Accent reduction | Accurate English"
description: "A highly specific pronunciation drill for correctly articulating the 'L' sound when it follows a long vowel (like in 'feel' or 'school')."
category: "english-pronunciation"
theme: "vowels-and-consonants"
mood: "study"
created: "2026-06-04"
updated: "2026-06-04"
visibility: "public"
language: "en"
tags: ["english-pronunciation", "consonants", "vowels", "youtube", "review"]
---

# The "L" Sound After Long Vowels

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

When an "L" comes at the end of a word, directly after a long vowel, native American English speakers insert a very tiny, almost invisible extra syllable (a schwa sound) before the L.

## The Technique: Adding the "uh" Sound

If you pronounce words like "feel" as a single, hard syllable, it will sound foreign. You must transition from the long vowel to the L by creating a slight "uh" sound.
- *Feel* ➔ Sounds like **Fee-ul**
- *School* ➔ Sounds like **Schoo-ul**
- *Smile* ➔ Sounds like **Smy-ul**
- *Mail* ➔ Sounds like **May-ul**

**Important Note on the American L:**
The American L is not "heavy" or strong. Do not push your tongue hard against your teeth. Lightly touch your tongue behind your top teeth and hold it there longer.

## Practice Prompts

1. Slowly pronounce these words, ensuring you hear the tiny transition syllable: "Tool," "Oil," "Deal," "Pale."

<br/>

# Dropping the 'T' After 'N'

Date: 2026-06-04
Category: english-pronunciation

## Summary

In standard American English pronunciation, there is a very common rule regarding words that contain an 'N' followed by a 'T'.

## The Rule: N + T = N

When a 'T' directly follows an 'N', native speakers frequently drop the 'T' entirely. The word simply flows as if the 'T' does not exist. 

While pronouncing the 'T' is perfectly acceptable and grammatically correct, dropping the 'T' sounds much more natural and conversational to American ears.

**Examples:**
- Twenty ➔ **Twenny**
- Interview ➔ **Innerview**
- Atlanta ➔ **Adlanna** (or *Ad-lan-uh*)
- International ➔ **Innernational**
- Plenty ➔ **Plenny**
- Winter ➔ **Winner**

## Practice Prompts

1. Read this sentence aloud naturally: "I have an international job interview in twenty minutes."
